Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **Abstract**  
# Using Proverbs to Contextualize Christianity in the Builsa Culture  
# of Ghana, West Africa  
The purpose of this research is to discover how Builsa proverbs can help a  
hermeneutical community begin the process of contextualizing Christianity in Builsa  
culture. A process to promote contextualization is emphasized rather than a finished  
product. A narrative format demonstrates how proverbs facilitate contextualization as  
Scripture engages culture.  
Data was collected using participant-observation, group discussions, and intensive  
interviews from 1992 - 2004 in Ghana and the U.S. Composite characters embodying  
typical responses of the group are presented in the narrative in order to describe the  
contextualization process.  
Both the emic and etic perspectives were employed. In the emic perspective, the  
Builsa church leaders in the Bible Church of Africa were better equipped to engage  
cultural issues with Scripture after they first learned to engage root values in their own  
proverbs with Scripture. After digging to the roots of Builsa proverbs, the Builsa  
reflected upon aspects of their own primal religion and culture that were often neglected  
in church. Proverbs became a mirror to reflect back some of their own hidden cultural  
values. These roots were then engaged with Scripture in order to affirm, modify, or reject  
aspects of the culture. This process was then applied to cultural issues in the church  
resulting in contextualized expressions of Christianity.  
From the etic perspective, proverbs were a window into the soul of the Builsa  
culture revealing aspects often hidden to outsiders. When this understanding was engaged  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. with Scripture, further aspects of Scripture were understood and applied. By using  
proverbs as oral art, the outsiders’ message was better appreciated and communicated.  
When comparing the values contained in Builsa proverbs to American values, differences  
were highlighted indicating potential areas of cultural misunderstanding.  
A contextualization process is described that incorporates the use of symbols,  
various genres, mother tongue Scripture, a widening hermeneutical community, the Holy  
Spirit, and an understanding of epistemology. Conclusions for the missionary community  
include\: appreciating oral literature in primary oral cultures, finding proper roles in the  
contextualization process, identifying cultural values via the window of proverbs, and  
tailoring discipleship training to incorporate significant aspects of orality.

# of Ghana, West Africa  
# Part I - Background Material  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **Chapter One - Searching for the Hippo**  
*“Yala nuru ale a nya waiik zuk pein ”*  
# \(The person at a distance sees the arrow in an antelope’s head\)  
\(Awein and Awangara 1990\:13\)  
A young girl once longed to see a hippopotamus in the river near her house  
\(Clapp 1996\:188\). One day, her parents informed her that a hippo was in the river and  
that she should hurry and see it. Excited, she ran to the riverbank looking for the hippo.  
Her enthusiasm quickly turned to disappointment - she could not find it. She searched up  
and down the bank but the hippo was nowhere to be seen. Finally, she scrambled on top  
of a big rock in order to get a good vantage point from which to view the entire river.  
Still, she could not see the hippo. Disappointed, she went home and told her parents how  
hard she looked but could not find the hippo. “You didn’t know it,” explained her  
parents, “but the very rock that you climbed on was the hippo. It was under your feet and  
you didn’t know it!” Often, the very things we search for are “under our feet.”  
Background of the Problem  
Many writers have observed Christianity’s lack of contextualization in Africa  
\(Bediako 2001a; Bevans 2002; Hiebert et al. 1999; Schreiter 1985; Turaki 2000;  
Whiteman 1997\). While the church in Africa is growing at a tremendous rate, the  
Scripture has not reached deeply into the worldview of the culture. Events like the 1994  
genocide in Rwanda, considered by many as one of the most Christian countries in Africa  
and the center of the famous East Africa revival, reveal the tragic consequences when the  
Scripture reaches only the surface level of culture but does not penetrate into the deeper  
# 1  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. worldview level. Contextualization then, is an urgent need since Christianity has yet to  
deal adequately with everyday concerns, let alone issues of life and death. Missionaries  
and national church leaders are searching up and down the river for tools and insights that  
will facilitate an engagement of the Scripture and culture. So far, there has largely been  
disappointment.  
Even the existing theological training has not often yielded the desired fruits of  
contextualization. During the 2001 all-Africa conference of the African Theological  
Fellowship held in Cote D’Ivoire, various members said that “the kind of theological  
formation that Africans receive in existing institutions, using existing patterns of training,  
does not equip for meeting the real needs and problems of life” \(Bediako 2001a\:32\). A  
Ghanaian pastor lamented to me, “After all of my training \[in Bible College\] was  
finished, I went back to the village and found that it did not fit!”1 His training did not  
address the cultural issues that he was facing in practical ministry in Ghana. Here is the  
mystery\: why has so much lip service been given to contextualization in Africa, yet there  
is still so little impact on the training of African pastors right up to today? Whiteman  
\(1997\:6\) observed this lack of practical contextualization and concluded, “I believe the  
only way through this maze is to discover the tools and perspectives of contextualization  
and then have the courage to implement them.” This research seeks to discover and  
experiment with one of those little-used tools - traditional proverbs.  
Our basic assumption is that, for a deep engagement of the culture and Scripture  
to occur, a closer look at cultural symbols is needed. Indigenous symbols can open  
windows that reveal aspects of the culture that are otherwise hidden. Local proverbs are  
an abundant and useful source of symbols that are often overlooked. Mbiti \(1969\:86\)  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. noted, “Proverbs are common ways of expressing religious ideas and feelings.” Proverbs,  
then, may serve as open windows into the worldview of the culture, thereby providing a  
good place to start in understanding the deeper aspects of culture. As this understanding  
of the culture is engaged with the Scripture in a hermeneutical community, critical  
contextualization can occur \(Hiebert 1987\). Proverbs may be one of the tools that God  
has placed in African cultures to facilitate contextualization - and they are right under  
our feet!  
African literary great, Chinua Achebe \(1959\:7\), noted, “Proverbs are the palm oil  
with which words are eaten.” While learning the Buli language of the Builsa people in  
Northern Ghana, I personally began to appreciate this oral art form as a powerful vehicle  
to communicate commonly held truth. This began a process where I started to collect  
Builsa proverbs, experiment with them in ministry, and reflect with other church leaders  
on how these proverbs could be useful in ministry among the Builsa people. I began to  
understand the potential of Mbiti’s \(1995\:55\) claim, “Proverbs...form a bridge between  
traditional African religiosity and biblical teaching.” I wondered how far this bridge  
would take us if we took proverbs seriously in discipling and theological training. Could  
we push this even further and change the metaphor from a bridge to a well? Would  
proverbs allow us to dig through the surface level of culture and help us reach down into  
the deeper, hidden worldview level? Would this well tap into a good source that would  
facilitate the engagement of Builsa culture with Scripture that would result in  
contextualization? Would it save some lives or even some entire societies from the  
disasters they fall into when their Christian understanding is superficial to their culture?  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 4  
Statement of the Problem  
While “Split-Level” Christianity is functioning in Builsa culture, large areas of  
the culture are not addressed by Scripture. The contextualization of Christianity in Builsa  
culture is then limited. How can this be corrected? What tools or perspectives can be  
found that will point the way forward for contextualization? What are the roles of  
missionaries and national church leaders/lay people in this process? The purpose of this  
research is to discover and describe how Builsa proverbs can be used in a hermeneutical  
community to help begin the process of contextualizing Christianity in Builsa culture.  
The focus is on a process that can be used to promote contextualization rather than a  
finished product.  
While the focus of this research is contextualization, several steps along the way  
are needed in order to get to the final goal.  
First, proverbs open an understanding of the traditional Builsa culture and religion  
that may otherwise be obscured. In order to determine how the analysis of proverbs by  
cultural insiders \(emic perspective\) may help them to understand and appreciate their  
own culture and Scripture, the first research question is posed, “What reflections are  
Builsa church leaders expressing of their own culture and Scripture as they focus on their  
proverbs?”  
Second, proverbs can be useful for those outside the culture looking in \(etic  
perspective\), as well as for those inside the culture. In order to determine how the  
analysis of proverbs by cross-cultural workers may help them understand another culture  
and Scripture, the second research question is posed, “What reflections are missionaries  
expressing of local culture and Scripture as they focus on proverbs?”  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. Third, a hermeneutical community can facilitate contextualization. To discover  
what process can be used to encourage the collection/study/analysis of proverbs, and the  
engagement of Scripture within a hermeneutical community via the use of proverbs, the  
third research question is posed, “What encourages the formation and on-going operation  
of a hermeneutical community and their engagement of Scripture with proverbs?”  
Fourth, when the Scripture and Builsa culture are engaged via proverbs, the  
resulting expression of Christianity will need to be communicated within Builsa culture.  
To observe evidence of contextualization as a result of focusing on proverbs, the fourth  
research question is posed, “How do Builsa respond as proverbs are used to communicate  
Christianity?”  
The above research questions helped to focus and guide the research. The  
following delimitations also help to sharpen the research focus.  
Delimitations and Limitations  
Folklore or oral literature for Africans was identified early as “a key to their ideas  
and a standard of their powers of thought” \(Theal 1886\[1970\]\:v\). The vast body of oral  
literature is an abundant source of cultural material; however, this study will only  
investigate the local genre of *yam wamgsima,* which can be glossed as proverbs. While  
proverbs, in themselves, do not provide the entire mosaic of a culture, they are one  
critical piece that is often neglected. This study will exemplify what can be done with  
other areas of oral literature.  
While oral literature, like proverbs, reflect certain aspects of culture, this study  
recognizes the limitations of language in communication. E.T. Hall \(1959\:45\) notes that  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 6  
humans use ten “Primary Message Systems” to communicate and only one of these  
involves language. As a result, proverbs can reveal significant aspects of culture, but  
much is also revealed by non-linguistic forms of communication.  
Hesselgrave \(1991\:327\) observed the abundance of proverbs across Africa\:  
“Thousands of proverbs and aphorisms form an indispensable part of daily  
communication throughout the cities and villages of Africa and other tribal areas.” For  
the purposes of this research, I will focus on the Builsa people in Northern Ghana, with  
supplementary material from other people groups in Northern Ghana and from African  
students studying at Asbury Theological Seminary.  
Honeck noted that there are several ways to view proverbs \(depending on the  
primary disciplines involved\), and this affects how the particular research is conducted.  
Honeck \(1997\:5\) concludes, “There is no overarching theory of proverbs. Instead,  
different views have emerged.” He describes seven major views \(see Table 1 on the  
following page\).  
The cognitive view regards proverbs as a “theoretical mental construct, and  
emphasizes the universal microcognitive, pancultural processes that underlie them”  
\(Honeck 1997\:43\). The emphasis is on finding what universal mental processes humans  
use to learn, understand, and apply proverbs. The proverbs are extracted from their  
cultural context in order to minimize cultural differences and discover universal  
principles. In contrast to the cognitive view, the cultural view considers proverbs to be  
“cultural linguistic products, created and used in social situations for social purposes...  
\[that\] largely entail the codification of important lessons in the culture” \(Honeck  
1997\:31\). Instead of minimizing the cultural context \(as in the cognitive view\), this is  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. emphasized in order to understand the meaning of proverbs, how they are used, and why  
they are used. This also includes the study of how proverbs originate, themes of proverb  
collections, and their diffusion across time and various cultures.  
Table 1. Summary of Seven Views of Proverbs \(Honeck 1997\:42\)

In the formal view, the focus is to “describe the linguistic and semantic-logic  
properties of proverbs” \(Honeck 1997\:11\). Proverbs are regarded as a unique language  
form that can be defined, classified, and analyzed resulting in taxonomies of various  
proverbs collections and characteristics that define proverbs. The literary view analyzes  
how proverbs are used in various genres \(e.g., prose, poetry, song\) by various authors  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 8  
through time \(e.g., Shakespeare, Dickinson, Achebe\). This view offers “an intriguing  
look at authors, their concerns and their times” \(Honeck 1997\:28\). The personal view of  
proverbs may also be called the phenomenological view. This is a more subjective study  
by people that have personal interests in proverbs and study them for various reasons  
\(e.g., personal use in conversation, attraction to proverbs, etc.\). Basically, this is “an  
egocentric and non-scientific view” \(Honeck 1997\:6\).  
The practical view recognizes that proverbs have pragmatic functions that  
“facilitate memory, teach, and persuade..Their practical uses draw on these same  
functions” \(Honeck 1997\:29\). Honeck lists several practical uses of proverbs to include\:  
treatment of substance abuse, psychotherapy, test, mental status, teach children abstract  
thinking, asses attitudes toward work and life, determine competency to stand trial,  
advertising, and intelligence testing. Finally, the religious view regards proverbs as  
expressions of religious wisdom that reflect how people should live their lives with each  
other. Religious texts \(e.g., Scripture\) are studied to understand the intended meaning or  
“how they fitted into the larger theological realm and helped to spread a religious  
message” \(Honeck 1997\:26\). Some researchers analyze the form and message of  
proverbs in wisdom literature, while others have compared proverbs between various  
religious traditions.  
This study is limited to the cultural and religious views. In these disciplines, I  
limited the research to explore how proverbs function as windows to understand Builsa  
culture, religion, and personal ethics, and how this helps to contextualize Christianity  
within that culture. The cultural view is helpful since it regards proverbs as oral literature  
that arises from a particular culture and functions as an integral part of that culture. The  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 9  
basic assumption is that the genre of proverbs is culturally familiar to the indigenous  
community. Field studies are used to determine what the proverbs portray of this culture  
and how they function within that community. The religious view of proverbs is also  
helpful since it recognizes that proverbs are packed with religious teaching and moral  
wisdom. In a primarily oral culture like the Builsa culture, some of these proverbs are  
“religious texts” that help to reveal the Builsa primal religion. By analyzing these texts, a  
view of Builsa religion is revealed that is often hidden.  
While the other approaches to the study of proverbs, such as the cognitive, formal,  
and literary, would prove interesting and perhaps even useful, this study is limited to the  
two approaches that appear to have the most missiological significance.  
An obvious limitation is that I am an outsider looking into the Builsa culture. My  
family and I first moved to Ghana, West Africa in 1992 as SIM missionaries and  
promptly started language learning. I learned the Builsa language called Buli, each of my  
family members has been given Buli names by the Builsa people, and my wife even  
delivered a baby there. While this has helped to sensitize us to the Builsa culture, we are  
still Anglo-Saxon Americans and not indigenous Builsa. My American culture, and my  
education based on the Enlightenment tradition, have limited my complete understanding  
of many proverbs and their implications. As a result, I want to let the Builsa voices be  
heard instead of only providing my opinions or having them “speak” through my  
worldview and limited framework. This should result in a more relevant and authentic  
picture of the Builsa context. This is particularly needful in light of critiques such as the  
following offered by Isichei \(1995\:7\),  
The underlying critique is that all Western analysis, however sympathetically  
intentioned, is, in Mudimbe’s words, an “invention” of Africa. It utilizes  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 10  
categories of thought, including “religion” that African cultures do not recognize.  
Far from encountering traditional religions with mutuality, it describes them from  
outside, in works to which most Africans have no access.  
A narrative style of writing will often be used to present the proverbs in contexts  
like the people employ them. The narratives will be interrupted, as necessary, for  
reflection and analysis \(this is similar to watching a video and then pausing for  
discussion\). The intent is that this will result in a presentation that demonstrates how  
proverbs are used in context, as well as how they engage the peoples’ emotions and  
memory. The goal is to engage and communicate with missionaries and nationals through  
the narrative, and also present scholarly research through the reflection and analysis. This  
goal should be achieved by combining narrative with analysis. Although this is not a  
typical dissertation style, it is more appropriate for the topic, and it demonstrates the  
power of narrative to communicate truth and understanding.  
Time is another limitation for this research. Referring to African proverbs, Mbiti  
\(1995\:58\) noted, “one person cannot collect them all, cannot analyze them all, cannot put  
them all into their context, and cannot use them all.” I lived in Ghana from January, 1992  
to April, 2001 with one furlough in between. I then returned to Ghana in January -  
March, 2004 for seven weeks of intense data collection. While this short time period in  
2004 was not ideal, it was adequate since I had already learned the Buli language and  
been active in ministry there. As a result, I was able to follow up on relationships and  
ministry settings with which I was familiar. In addition, friends in the SIM Ghana  
administration as well Father Kofi Lange \(with the Tamale Institute of Cross-Cultural  
Studies\) prearranged many meetings in order to make the most of my time in the field.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 11  
This saved me months of field time, thereby allowing my time in Ghana to be more  
productive.  
Another delimitation is that biblical proverbs were not prominent in this research.  
This may seem odd at first glance since proverbs were also an important genre in biblical  
times. Not only does the Old Testament contain an entire book devoted to proverbs, but  
the genre of proverbs is included in the New Testament as well. In addition, proverbs in  
Scripture often function to encourage reflection on practical living and to reinforce moral  
precepts. While it may seem logical that compatible genres would lead to fruitful  
discussions for contextualization, biblical proverbs are not featured in this research for  
the following reasons\:  
1\. The biblical book of Proverbs has not yet been translated into the Buli  
language. Consequently, when the Builsa people engage Scripture, they do not yet have  
ready access to the book of Proverbs.  
2\. When Builsa hear a *yam wamagsima* spoken, they immediately recognize it as  
such. While *yam wamagsima* can be roughly glossed as the English word “proverb,” non-  
indigenous proverbs, including biblical proverbs, do not affect a Builsa audience the  
same way. Y*am wamagsima* are unique expressions of Builsa culture that often invoke  
strong emotional responses among the Builsa; whereas, biblical \(and other\) proverbs do  
not typically create the same response. So, *yam wamagsima* are a distinct genre within  
the broader genre of proverbs and similar figures of speech. This will be discussed further  
in the next section and exemplified in subsequent chapters.  
3\. In the field research, I interjected some biblical proverbs to see how this may  
help the contextualization process. This did not seem to be particularly helpful, perhaps  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 12  
due to the reasons listed above. Additional research would need to explore this further,  
and this was beyond the scope of this study.  
Definitions  
The following definitions will clarify the discussion\:  
Oral Literature. Two East African scholars, Nandwas and Bukenya \(1983\:1\)  
defined oral literature as “those utterances, whether spoken, recited or sung, whose  
composition and performance exhibit to an appreciable degree the artistic characteristics  
of accurate observation, vivid imagination and ingenious expression.” Put more simply,  
Okpewho \(1992\:3-4\) states, “Oral literature ... simply means literature delivered by  
words of mouth”, and this represents the verbal aspect of folklore “which comprises  
riddles, puns, tongue-twisters, proverbs, recitations, chants, songs and stories.” Finnegan  
\(1970\) adds to this list drama, poems, dance, myths, and drum language. While some  
object to the use of this term since it ignores the distinctions between orality and literacy  
\(Ong 1982\:12\), others prefer the terms “oral literature” \(Finnegan 1970\), “oral tradition”  
\(Baldick 1990\:156\), or “orature” \(Thiong'o 1986\) over the formerly used term “folklore”  
due to the negative connotations often given by Europeans associated with the term  
“folklore.” Proverbs are a part of, and intimately connected to, the larger body of oral  
literature. Proverbs are often told in the context of stories or songs, either as the  
summation of the point or as an introduction to the performance. Like other oral  
literature, proverbs provide an aesthetic expression as an oral art form; therefore, the  
performance of the proverb carries the meaning, not just the words or logic of the  
utterance.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. Proverbs. Scholars have strived for years to provide an inclusive definition of  
proverbs. Wolfgang Meider \(Meider and Holmes 2000\:37\), a leading contemporary  
paremiologist, considered Archer Taylor’s classic text, *The Proverb* to be the “Bible of  
proverb scholarship.” Taylor \(1962 \[1931\]\:37\) concluded, “The definition of a proverb is  
too difficult to repay the undertaking... An incommunicable quality tells us this sentence  
is proverbial and one that is not.” In other words, a proverb is easily identified when  
spoken but hard to define. Shirley Arora \(1984\) notes that certain characteristics of  
proverbs often include traditionality, currency, repetition, certain grammatical or  
syntactical features, metaphor, semantic markers \(parallelism, paradox, irony, etc.\),  
lexical markers \(archaic words, etc.\), and phonic markers \(rhyme, meter, alliteration,  
etc.\). The more these are included in the phrase or saying, the more likely it is to be  
regarded as a proverb. Nonetheless, several have attempted to provide a working  
definition of a proverb. A proverb has been defined as “ ... a short, pithy saying that  
encodes the philosophical outlook, religious concepts and world-view of African society  
in a digestible form” \(Healey and Sybertz 2000\[1996\]\:34\). While proverbs may not  
portray the entire philosophical outlook, religious concepts and world-view, they most  
certainly reveal important aspects. Lord John Russell \(1792-1878\) offered the following  
definition, “A proverb is the wit of one, and the wisdom of many” \(Meider and Holmes  
2000\:96\).  
The Builsa categorize their oral literature differently than what is portrayed in  
English, as shown in the following table in which I have used glosses for the Buli words.  
Glosses are approximations but are not translations.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 14  
Table 2. Taxonomy of Builsa Oral Literature  
I ------------------------- 1 --------------- 1 -------------1 ----------1 ------------- 1 ---------------1  
*- Wamagsima Sunsuelima Watogma Konim Yiila Puusa Yuenga*  
\(riddles/proverbs\) \(stories \(stories\) \(legends\) \(songs\) \(greeting/ \(names\)  
with song\) prayers\)  
— *Yam-wamagsima*  
\(proverbs\)  
*— Wamagsima*  
\(riddles\)  
Ayaric \(1992\:12\) noted that the word “*wamagsima*” included both proverbs and  
riddles, which are differentiated from *sunsuelima* \(stories\), *korum* \(legends of early  
ancestors\), *puusa* \(greetings/prayers\) and various types of *yiila* \(songs\). In field research,  
I observed that several people used *watogma* to differentiate stories without a song from  
*sunsuelima* when referring to stories that had a song as a refrain. Several Builsa also  
noted that th*eyuenga* \(names\) were of special importance and could be used like  
proverbs. Upon further questioning, I observed that the Builsa further differentiated  
between *wamagsima* \(riddles\) and *yam-wamagsima* \(proverbs\). Kroger \(1992\:392\)  
defined *yam* as “sense, wisdom, reason, intellect, knowledge, cleverness, intelligence.”  
This research focuses on *yam wamagsima.*  
While a complete taxonomy of Builsa oral art is beyond the scope of this  
research, another classification may help the reader to appreciate the elaborate system  
that exists within the Builsa culture. This affirms Walter Ong’s \(1982\) observation that  
oral cultures tend to be elaborate in thought but this thought is organized and expressed in  
a way that is foreign to the literate mind. The art of speaking Buli \(*biik\)* can also be  
classified by the degree of obscurity intended for the general audience, as shown in the  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 15  
following diagram. Depending on the context, *yam wamagsima* can be used at all three  
levels of clarity and directness.  
*Biik* \(speech/conversation\)  
*Pieli* \(white or clear\) *Wiani* \(obscure\) *Sobili* \(black or dark\)  
Direct/Clear *\<r*---------------------------------------------------------\>Indirect/Less Clear  
Figure 1. Direct vs. Indirect Buli Speech Patterns  
*Biik-pieli* is used to speak very clearly and simply. This type of speech is used by  
a child or someone speaking to a child. An example is the proverb, **“Ba kan dak yauk a**  
**sak biiga”** \(They do not point to the elephant and show the child\).  
*Biik-wiani* is used to speak in a way that obscures the meaning so that some of the  
listeners will not understand the meaning \(even though they may understand every  
individual word spoken\). This can be used when adults do not want children to  
understand what is being discussed or to insult another person. An example is the  
proverb, *“Fi dan sak bulorik dueni deka, ku chum le faari fi ma*” **\(If you teach an ugly**  
**man how to marry women, in the future he will marry your mother\).** The meaning of  
this proverb will be discussed in Chapter 5. The point is that understanding and using this  
type of *biik* indicates that you understand the Builsa people and their culture. This  
elevates the speaker from being a child who uses *biik-pieli* to being a skilled adult who  
aptly uses *biik-wiani* in the appropriate context. In explaining the usage of *biik-wiani,* one  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 16  
Builsa explained that *Yezu* spoke this way. He spoke so that his enemies could not trap  
him; yet those who were truly interested could come back and learn more.  
*Biik-sobili* is used by the elderly to convey ancient wisdom. Often archaic words  
are used that most Builsa do not understand. An example is the proverb, “*Ba kan kali dai*  
*yeng a nakpeesa a su eyu iyd* ’ \(They do not sit in one day and harvest Fra-Fra potatoes  
to fill a leather bag\). The leather bag referred to is rarely used anymore and is not a part  
of routine vocabulary; hence, it obscures the meaning. A Builsa explained to me the  
usage of *biik-sobili* in this way, “If a boy wants to learn *biik-sobili,* you must sit with  
your father and ask him \[the meaning\]. If a child does not sit with his father, then the  
child will not know *biik-sobili.* If you ask the meaning though, the elder will want to  
know why you are asking the meaning. ..They fear that you may use the meaning to hurt  
them.”  
While the above characterizations are not exhaustive, they do begin to define and  
describe Builsa oral literature, particularly *yam wamagsima.* It should be noted that there  
is a detailed emic taxonomy that is every bit as complex as etic taxonomies.  
Builsa. The Builsa people \(also written in various literature as Bulsa, Bulisa, and  
Kanjargas\) comprise approximately 100,000 \(Kroger 2001\:30\) and predominately live in  
the Upper East Region of Ghana, West Africa, while many have also migrated to  
Southern Ghana and beyond. Their language is called Buli and their land/society is  
referred to as Buluk \(Akanling-Pare 1999\:38\). Buli is in the Gur language family under  
the Oti-Volta subgrouping, being most closely related to the Konni languge \(Naden  
1988\:17-18\). The Builsa are largely subsistence farmers living in scattered villages with  
the largest towns being Sandema \(in the North\) and Fumbisi \(in the South\). According to  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 17  
traditions and affirmed by the present Builsa paramount Chief, Naab Ayieta Azantilow  
IV, the earliest Builsa migrated from the Mamprusi people who live just South of the  
Builsa \(Barker 1986\:151\). Atuga was the Mamprusi Chiefs son who got in a quarrel and  
had to leave his home. He settled in what is now the Builsa area.  
Buli Scripture. After approximately 17 years of translation, the first printing of the  
Buli New Testament occurred in 1995 \(Ghana Institute of Linguistics\) under the title,  
*“Naawen Gbang Paalika ”* While the Ghana Institute of Linguistics, Literacy, and Bible  
Translation is still translating the Old Testament, only a few portions have been  
completed. These include the books of Ruth, Habakkak, and Jonah, as well as Ezekiel 18,  
and Genesis 1-9 \(Schaefer and Schaefer 2001\). At the time of this research, this  
represents the completed portions of written Scripture that the Builsa have available. Oral  
Scripture portions are available on cassette along with eight picture books by Gospel  
Recordings.  
Kwame Bediako \(1995\:60\) notes the importance of using mother tongue  
Scripture,  
The ability to hear in one’s own language and express in one’s own language  
one’s response to the message which one receives, must lie at the heart of all  
authentic religious encounter with the divine realm. Language itself becomes,  
then, not merely a social or a psychological phenomenon, but a theological one as  
well.  
While the use of the word “all” may be too strong a word, Bediako points out the  
critical importance of using the mother tongue for developing contextual theology. The  
mother tongue Scripture then, as “the authoritative, normative deposit given to us of the  
divine-human encounter, that lies at the heart of our faith,” is the hermeneutic of culture  
and tradition \(K. Bediako 2001b\:2\). This means that the Scripture, when translated in the  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 18  
Buli language, is the guideline to interpret all aspects of culture. Quarshie \(2002\:11\)  
concludes,  
If Africans are to make a unique contribution in any language-based enterprise  
such as Biblical Studies, they will have to draw inspiration from their mother  
tongue and therefore from their cultural consciousness. This confirms the far-  
reaching and challenging implications of doing Biblical Studies in the African  
context in mother-tongue Scriptures.  
The Buli New Testament and Buli Old Testament portions will be the interpreter  
of all Builsa culture and tradition, when used in the Builsa church hermeneutical  
community. This makes the Buli Scripture central to the discussions of culture.  
Culture. Culture is easier to observe by comparing various cultures than it is to  
define. There is no universally accepted definition of culture, though this concept was  
first introduced into the English language in 1871 by Edward B. Tylor \(Langness 1987  
\[1974\]\: 1\). Whiteman \(2001\) defines culture as “That complex array of ideas in a person’s  
mind which are expressed in the form of material artifacts and observable behavior.”  
Rynkiewich \(2003\) states that culture is, “A more or less integrated system of learned,  
shared and contested ways of thinking, valuing, and feeling that serves to adapt a people  
to their changing environment and provides a foundation for change.” From an  
anthropological perspective, the emphasis is on a holistic plan or blueprint for living  
carried in the minds of people that is learned and always adjusting to meet the needs and  
questions of people. The norms and values established guide the interactions between  
people, including communication patterns. These norms for communicating are “basic for  
contextualizing the Gospel and for constructing meaningful local theologies” \(Luzbetak  
1988\:157\). It is also important to note that culture is a set of learned ideas that are  
transmitted by a set of symbols that have meanings understood by the group. When  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. discussing communication in culture, Leeds-Hurwitz \(1993\:17\) defines culture as “a set  
of systems or codes of symbols and meanings” since the symbols “provide a structure for  
social actors, limiting possible choices to those culturally available,” and they are “the  
tools people use to convey meaning; these are the resource materials from which people  
choose to convey what meanings they wish.” While no single Buli word is used to  
translate “culture,” several Builsa indicated that the word *“koru* was useful when  
discussing the traditions and legends of the Builsa. Other Builsa used the term *“nyeboka ”*  
to refer to the way Builsa live, including their contemporary way of life.  
Builsa culture and religion. African religion has been described as “a way of life  
or life itself, where a distinction or separation is not made between religion and other  
areas of human existence” \(Magesa 1997\:25\). As a result of this close union, Dickson  
\(1984\:29\) noted, “African culture and religion are bound up together, so much so that the  
term culture...\[is\] an umbrella description which subsumes religion.” This close union of  
religion and culture is noteworthy since it indicates the complexity of understanding  
Builsa religion, as well as showing that tools for understanding the religion will be  
located in the culture \(e.g., proverbs\).  
Soul of Culture. Luzbetak identifies three levels whereby societies have organized  
their culture. The surface layer reveals the “meaningless forms, the ‘shapes,’ the symbols  
minus their meaning” \(Luzbetak 1988\:223\). In order to uncover the meaning behind these  
forms, the second layer of culture must be uncovered. The second layer is the inner  
function or system of meaning behind the forms. The third or deepest level is the basic  
psychology or soul of the culture \(1988\:223\). Valuable clues to understanding this level  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 20  
will be found in myths, rituals, folk tales, proverbs, etc. \(Luzbetak 1988\:251\). Luzbetak  
\(1988\:249-250\) describes this third level further,  
The unifying psychological tendency has been called by various names.. .genius,  
philosophy of life, mentality, psychology, inner logic, and Geist. Sumner spoke of  
ethos, while Sapir preferred the terms style and patterning. Benedict spoke of the  
main spring and patterns of culture.. .meant are the underlying notions, values,  
and motives; meant are the starting points of reasoning, reacting, and acting; and  
meant are the basic premises, emotionally charged attitudes, and goals. Such an  
underlying and basic set of starting points in reasoning, reacting, and acting tends  
to be consistent in itself and is so basic that it permeates the whole culture and  
gives a certain oneness. This is what is commonly referred to as a people’s  
“mentality,” “psychology,” or “soul.” It is what we call the third or deep level of  
culture.  
It is this third level of culture that is most difficult to identify and understand, even for  
those that are immersed in it and call it their own. This is the level that must be  
understood, however, in order for the critical engagement of the culture and Scripture to  
occur.  
Sign and Symbol. A sign has been defined as “as word, object, action, event,  
pattern, quality, relation, person, or concrete particular that serves as a vehicle for a  
conception of some perceived reality” \(Hiebert et al. 1999\:232\). In short, it is “something  
present that stands for something absent” \(Leeds-Hurwitz 1993\:6\) or something that  
points to something else. Signs help to “connect the world of exterior realities to inner  
mental worlds” \(Hiebert et al. 1999\:232\). A symbol is a subset of a sign. Peirce  
\(1955\[1940J\) called symbols unmotivated or arbitrary signs, meaning that they do not  
necessarily resemble the reality that they represent \(i.e., they are not obviously related\).  
Symbols are the basic building blocks that societies use to construct the mental worlds of  
reality in order to give order and meaning \(Hiebert et al. 1999\:234\). Burke \(1968\:3-24\)  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 21  
points out that humans not only use symbols, but they make them and misuse them. This  
implies that they are continually being transformed and recreated.  
Engagement of Scripture and Culture. More than just Western classical theology  
with African clothing, the engagement of Scripture and culture reveals both the image of  
God and the stain of sin in culture. Similarly, both the universal continuity and cultural  
applications of Scripture are revealed as the mother tongue Scripture is employed. Since  
this concept is essential to the research, it deserves further explanation, as given by  
African theologian, Kwame Bediako \(2001b\:2\),  
The engagement of gospel and culture means more than the altering of surface  
elements like music, dance and artifacts. It is also more than “spiritual salvation”,  
more than the saving of the spiritual part of us. It is also not about the  
demonisation of our culture. Rather, gospel and cultural engagement is about  
the conversion of cultures, the turning to Christ and turning over to Christ of all  
that is there in us, about us and round about us that has defined and shaped us  
when Jesus meets us, so that the elements of our cultural identity are brought  
within the orbit of discipleship.  
Contextual theology results as the mother tongue Scripture and the culture are  
fully engaged, thereby creating dialogue between the two. Both the Scripture and culture  
are more fully understood and appreciated in this process.  
Critical Contextualization. A process whereby the Scripture and culture are fully  
engaged has been called critical contextualization \(Hiebert 1987\). Hiebert et al \(1999\:22\)  
describe this as a four-step process that includes phenomenological analysis, ontological  
reflection, critical evaluation, and missiological transformation. Uncritical acceptance  
and total rejection of cultural beliefs and practices are two inappropriate responses to the  
interaction of Scripture and culture. Critical Contextualization is an attempt to bridge  
these two responses and provide a response to cultural issues that is Scripturally faithful  
as well as culturally relevant. Whiteman \(1997\:2\) notes, “Essentially, contextualization is  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 22  
concerned with how the Gospel and culture relate to one another across geographic space  
and down through time.” Contextualization includes the proper use of indigenous forms  
in the life of the church, the developing of contextual theology and the proper addressing  
of local needs by the church in order to transform society \(Gilliland 1989\:27\).  
Hermeneutical Community. Critical contextualization is best done by a  
community of Christians in dialogue to both initiate and affirm it. Gilliland \(1989\:317\)  
notes, “the goal of the critical method is to arrive at contextualized practices which have  
the consensus of the redeemed community .” When discussing the process of constructing  
local theologies, Schreiter describes the “community as the theologian.” He \(1985\:16\)  
states, “The community itself \[is\] the prime author of theology in local contexts. The  
Holy Spirit, working in and around the believing community, gives shape and expression  
to Christian experience.” While the critical role of the hermeneutical community has been  
widely affirmed, little has been written to discuss how this community is formed, how it  
functions, is led and motivated. When discussing indigenous theologies, Tienou  
\(1993\:249\) states, “How to get the faith community, the so-called grass-roots Christians,  
to be effective participants in theological decision-making still remains a challenge for  
the professional theologian today, and perhaps tomorrow.” While this community of  
believers locally and beyond is needed to properly engage the Scripture and culture, the  
process is not equally clear. Even though the church is a hermeneutical community and  
contextualization is done to a certain degree in the life of the church through the songs,  
prayers, testimonies, sermons, rituals, etc., this research aims to see how church leaders  
can more intentionally engage “excluded middle” issues.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. To promote a hermeneutical community, several church leaders were gathered to  
focus on proverbs and discuss their interaction with Scripture. Initially, twelve Builsa  
pastors from the SIM-related Bible Church of Africa \(BCA\) met from 2000-2001. These  
discussions were in Buli and I recorded my observations as a participant observer. This  
stimulated further interest in the use of proverbs in ministry such that church leaders from  
nearby people groups wanted to meet as well. This resulted in a “Proverbs and the  
Gospel” Workshop from February 20-23, 2001 at the Tamale Institute of Cross-Cultural  
Studies \(TICCS\) that included 45 participants from Chiana, Navrongo, Tumu,  
Yikpabango, Yagaba, Chereponi, Tamale, Yendi, Salaga, Bimbilla, Kpandai, Damongo,  
as well as the Builsa area. The leaders were from the BCA and the Roman Catholic  
churches. The discussions were in English and were printed for each of the people groups  
\(Lange and Moon 2001\). During the field research in 2004,1 met with both the BCA and  
TICCS groups again.  
Primal Religion. While Hiebert et al \(1999\:75\) use the term “folk religion” to  
refer to “the religious beliefs and practices of the common folk,” Turner \(1977\) rejects  
this designation and suggests the use of the term “primal religion.” Gillian Bediako  
\(2000\:12\) agrees with Turner that the term “primal” is “the best available to describe the  
varied, yet common religious traditions around the world from which the majority of the  
world’s Christians have come.” She adds that primal does not mean primitive. Walls  
\(1996\:121\) also affirms Turner’s preference for the term “primal” since it “helpfully  
underlines two features of the religions of the people indicated\: their historical anteriority  
and their basic, elemental status in human experience.” The term primal then goes  
beyond the term folk, since the use of the term primal religion acknowledges that this is  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 24  
largely a coherent system of faith that existed prior to Christianity and prepared them to  
understand and live out their Christian faith even today. This understanding helps one to  
take seriously the preparatory and present influence of primal religion in the African  
church as a potential soul-mate instead of an antagonist \(G. Bediako 2000\).  
My theological assumption is that God has revealed aspects of Himself to all  
cultures and people \(Romans 1, John 1\:9\). God has given to each person \(regardless of  
cultural or geographical location\) some light that testifies of Him. This grace is  
ministered to each person by the activity of the Holy Spirit, and it is meant to draw  
people to a new relationship with God through Jesus Christ. John Wesley describes this  
as the prevenient grace of God.  
Richardson \(1981\) has popularized the notion that God has placed a witness of  
Himself within each culture. He demonstrates how God’s general revelation \(which he  
calls the Melchizedek factor\) often prepares the way for people to understand and receive  
God’s special revelation \(the Abraham factor\). The Creator God has provided redemptive  
analogies within the culture that Christian witnesses can use to help explain the Good  
News of Jesus Christ. He also calls this “redemptive lore” since it “contributes to the  
redemption of a people solely by facilitating their understanding of what redemption  
means” \(1981\:59\). I regard some of the Builsa proverbs as “redemptive lore” that are  
evidence of the prevenient grace of God working within the Builsa culture.  
At the same time, the fall of Adam and Eve in Genesis introduced sin into the  
world. Consequently, all people and resulting cultures contain evidence of sin \(Romans  
3\:23\). Some of the Builsa proverbs also reflect the sinfulness of humankind. While  
missionaries have often worked to identify the sins in a host culture, my assumption is  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. that it is better for the Holy Spirit to reveal to a local hermeneutical community which  
aspects of culture are marred by sin and which are evidence of God’s prevenient grace, as  
they engage culture with Scripture and use the mother tongue Scripture as the interpreter  
of their culture and tradition. These assumptions undergird this research.  
So, when looking for perspectives and tools for contextualization, it may be best  
to look for evidence of God’s prevenient grace already active in Builsa culture. Perhaps  
some proverbs are huge rocks placed by God within Builsa culture - and they are right  
under our feet. They can be used in a hermeneutical community as a bridge to  
contextualize Christianity in Builsa culture. To understand how these rocks may help us  
to find the hippo of contextualization, a review of the relevant literature is first needed.  
1 This was a personal conversation with Joseph Otsin from Ghana, West Africa on June  
27, 2002.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **Chapter Two - Literature Review**  
“Wisdom is like a baobab tree, a single person’s hand cannot embrace it” \(Healey and  
Sybertz 2000\[1996\]\:17\)  
Overview  
To frame the discussion on how Builsa proverbs may be used to engage the  
culture with Scripture, a literature review is required in four major areas\: African Primal  
Religion, communication theory, religious symbols, and critical contextualization. A  
brief overview of each area will be followed by a more detailed review.  
First, it is important to understand religious belief systems in primal cultures in  
order to see how proverbs function in Builsa culture. A review of this literature highlights  
that an understanding of primal religion is an unfinished task of the church in Africa  
today. When looking closer at primal religion, one sees that Builsa primal religion is  
portrayed by various genres that provide different views of reality. One of the genres,  
proverbs, provides an important but often overlooked perspective for understanding  
Builsa primal religion. This frames the problem for us by revealing the need and  
opportunity for proverbs to help engage the Scripture and culture.  
Second, a constitutive model of communication theory is reviewed in order to  
understand how proverbs function in culture to help construct reality. In the constitutive  
model, symbols are a critical link to connect an inner reality \(e.g., the picture in your  
mind of a house\) to an external reality \(e.g., the physical house that you build\), and then  
help communicate this within culture. By viewing how proverbs function as important  
symbols in oral cultures, this highlights the usefulness of proverbs as a readily available  
26  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 27  
mnemonic device to help construct and communicate the inner concept of reality \(e.g.,  
the picture in your mind of what a house should be in that culture\).  
Third, religious symbols are helpful tools for understanding religious systems.  
While much research has focused on looking *at* proverbs \(to describe characteristics,  
origins, etc.\), the review of religious symbols demonstrates how proverbs can be looked  
*through* in order to provide a helpful window into the worldview. By connecting both the  
ideology and sensory levels, proverbs are powerful and prevalent open windows into  
primal religions and cultures. Once this window is opened, fresh areas of discovery into  
Builsa culture and religion emerge.  
Fourth, a review of the development of contextual theology displays how the  
cultural perspective gained by proverbs opens new avenues to engage the Scripture and  
culture. The recent history of African contextual theology points out the successes,  
shortfalls, and trends. By combining both the emic and etic perspectives in a  
hermeneutical community via the process of critical contextualization, a greater  
understanding and appreciation of both the Scripture and culture can result. Proverbs then  
become a catalyst to initiate and sustain a process of contextualization. This addresses a  
present and pressing need for contextualization in the Builsa church today.  
African Primal Religion  
An understanding of African primal religion is a helpful starting point for  
contextualization.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 28  
Religious Belief Systems  
Hiebert \(1994\:196\) provides a framework for the analysis of religious belief  
systems. This framework reveals that issues arising from primal religion are an  
“unfinished agenda” of the church in Africa \(Hiebert 1993\:258\). Heibert \(1994\:197\)  
explains that Western culture has a two-tiered view of reality, due to “the secularization  
of science and the mystification of religion.” On the first level, modern science deals with  
the empirical world to explain that which can be seen and experienced. This addresses  
problems of this world dealing with the natural order or the secular realm. On the second  
level, high religion is left to deal with other-world issues such as faith, miracles, inner  
feelings, and other sacred things.  
In contrast to the West, traditional African culture has a three-tiered view of  
reality. On the first level, folk science deals with the issues of this world that can be  
observed by the senses through the interactions of others. On the third level, high religion  
deals with the forces and beings in the unseen spiritual “other” world that occurred in  
other times. In between these two levels, primal religion addresses forces and beings in  
the unseen world that affect this world. Hiebert \(1994\:196\) calls this level “the excluded  
middle,” since it is “one of the greatest cultural gaps between Western people and many  
traditional religionists.”  
Whiteman \(2001\) illustrates this discussion with the diagram on the next page. At  
the top of the pyramid, the high religion deals with the ultimate issues in the unseen  
spiritual world \(e.g., where we come from or where we go when we die\). In practice, this  
is a small amount of the daily concerns of the average Builsa person. In Builsa culture,  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 29  
Abstract  
# A  
# Other World  
# High \\  
# Religion  
# Unseen  
# Primal Religion  
# This World  
# Seen  
# Folk Science  
Concrete  
# Figure 2. Three-Tiered View of Reality in Primal Cultures  
primal religion deals largely with unseen forces that act in this world \(e.g., who/what  
caused someone to be sick\). This covers a larger portion of the daily intimate  
activities in Builsa culture. The Builsa folk science covers an even larger amount of daily  
events that can be seen in this world \(e.g., how to plant crops, or catch animals\). For  
contextualization of Christianity in Builsa culture to occur, primal religion \(and the issues  
it addresses\) must be given a closer look and engaged with Scripture.  
While Builsa primal religion includes the high religion issues, it focuses largely  
on entities at work in this world that are beyond rational explanation since they are  
unseen or supernatural, such as ancestors, ghosts, spirits, witches, local gods or  
goddesses, etc. The key issues that are addressed are the causes of death and identity in  
life, well-being and misfortune, guidance for an uncertain future, and justice amidst evil  
\(Hiebert et al. 1999\:74\). Typically, Western missionaries did not provide spiritual  
answers to these issues, hence they were excluded from discussion. Since the Scripture  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 30  
\(or at least the way it was presented\) did not appear to be relevant to their questions,  
Africans deferred to local responses. While Christianity replaced the aspects of Builsa  
primal religion that addressed the concerns of high religion, it only minimally addressed  
the “excluded middle” level issues. As a result, Christianity operated largely on the high  
religion level to address ultimate issues, while primal religion operated largely at the  
middle level to address daily intimate issues. This has been called “Split-level  
Christianity” \(Bulatao 1992\) since Christianity and primal religion have operated  
concurrently on two distinct levels. This has severely limited the development of the  
church in Africa. Hiebert \(1993\:258\) summarizes, “It is clear today that folk religious  
beliefs and practices remain an unfinished agenda in the lives of young churches around  
the world.. .Nowhere is this more true than in Africa.”  
Gradually, missionaries and Africans are becoming aware of the need to  
understand and engage primal religion. Hiebert \(1993\:254\) notes, “There is a growing  
awareness among mission leaders that these issues arising out of folk religions will be  
central in missiology in the 21st century.” Missionaries are increasingly interested in  
understanding African primal religion but are limited in their understanding due to their  
own religious framework that excludes the middle level.  
Africans are also crying out for a further understanding of African primal religion.  
African theologian, Bediako \(1989\:59\), states, “There is probably no issue more crucial  
than the need to understand this heightened interest in the African pre-Christian religious  
tradition if Africa’s theologians are to be interpreted correctly and their achievement duly  
recognized.” Turaki \(2000\:281\) also affirms the need for understanding primal religion\:  
The most crucial theological issue at hand is that most African Christians lack  
sufficient knowledge of the Bible and its teaching on African traditional religions  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 31  
and cultures... .Even though the influence of the African traditional religions and  
cultures is profound in modem Africa, most African Christians have little  
knowledge of how this influence is exerted.  
Sanneh \(1983\) noted how African primal religion greatly affected the perception  
of Christianity within Africa and affected the organizational aspects of Christianity. He  
\(1983\:242\) demonstrates how African primal religion served to “deepen the sense of the  
religious potential of the message of the Bible,” and he concludes, “The issue is more  
than just academic interest. It has implications for pastoral care and counseling, and for  
developing a meaningful theology for the Church.”  
While both mission and church leaders in Africa agree that this “excluded  
middle” must be understood, this is often easier said than done. E.T. Hall \(1959\:29-30\)  
explains the difficulty in understanding culture, “Culture hides much more than it reveals,  
and strangely enough what it hides, it hides most effectively from its own participants.”  
As a result, methods and perspectives are needed to understand primal religion for both  
missionaries from the outside looking in and nationals from the inside looking at their  
own culture. Turaki \(2000\:281\) affirms the need for the African church to better  
understand African primal religion,  
The church in Africa seems not to have a good knowledge of the traditional  
African religious beliefs, religious practices, general religious and cultural life,  
and traditional theological reasons for adhering to the traditional religions and  
cultures....This background is essential if we are going to formulate a biblical  
theology that can address adequately the African traditional religions and cultures.  
Furthermore, this background is necessary to help African Christians sort out  
traditional religious beliefs, practices, and behavior that are in conflict with their  
Christian faith.  
Even though the need to understand African primal religion is clear, the way  
forward to do this is not equally clear. Some have described African primal religions and  
philosophies \(Magesa 1997; Mbiti 1969\) but the issue of how Scripture engages this is  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 32  
yet to be settled. Additional perspectives and processes are needed that can facilitate this  
engagement. The role of various genres provides some needed perspective to better  
understand primal religion, and it shows why the study of proverbs can fill a needed gap  
to move forward.  
Different Genres  
Language can be divided into different genres or types. Each genre has its own  
unique perspective that describes reality for people within a culture. Bakhtin1 explains,  
“Certain features of language take on the specific flavor of a given genre\: they knit  
together with specific points of view, specific approaches, forms of thinking, nuances and  
accents characteristic of the given genre” \(Holquist 1981\:289\). As a result, Bakhtin  
continues, each genre provides “a specific way of visualizing a given part of reality”  
since they each combine “specific blindnesses and insights” \(Morson and Emerson  
1990\:275-276\). While language unifies a people and their perspective of reality, genres  
also serve to stratify language and culture such that each different genre provide a  
different perspective \(Holquist 1981\:429\). When taken together, a more complete picture  
of the cultural view of reality is given.  
The different genre together form a set of blueprints \(Hiebert 1999\:76-81\) or  
mental maps \(Spradley 1979\:7\) that provide a “guide for acting and for interpreting our  
experience.” Each drawing in the set of blueprints “purports to give us true information,  
but only about some part of reality” \(Hiebert 1999\:80\). Korzybski \(1933\) introduced the  
metaphor that the verbal world is related to the extensional world \(that which is not  
directly experienced by the senses, but only heard about or learned\) as a map does to the  
territory it is supposed to represent. Verbal words, then, are symbols or mental maps that  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 33  
people carry in their heads. Various genres provide the material to form various maps of  
the same territory \(e.g., one map shows roads while another shows population density and  
another shows political boundaries, etc.\). These genres combine to provide a more  
complete picture of African culture and primal religion. Bailey \(1995\:201\) notes, “Most  
cultures exhibit a rich repertoire of genres, offering a wide range of particularized visions  
of reality,” and African culture is no exception. If one were to put a magnifying glass on  
African primal religion in order to get a closer look, numerous genres would be revealed,  
with each providing valuable perspectives on African culture and primal religion \(see  
Figure 3 on the next page\).  
Various genres have been studied in African culture to include\: rituals, myths,  
stories, songs, poems, prayers, dance, drama, riddles, and proverbs. A few genres will be  
noted in passing to illustrate the research conducted, but these works will not be engaged  
or discussed at length since it is beyond the scope of this research. The purpose of this  
discussion is to briefly describe some of the seminal and contemporary research on  
various African genres, and demonstrate how research on the genre of proverbs to  
promote contextualization has been largely overlooked.  
Turner provides details of rituals \(1995 \[1969\]\), \(1967a\), \(1967b\), while Eliade  
focuses on myths \(1959\), \(1998\[1963\]\). Progressive Vision \(Willis 2002\) recently  
produced a CD ROM set that discusses how storytelling is effective in understanding a  
worldview and communicating in cross-cultural ministry. Steffen \(1996\:22\) also  
discussed the role of storytelling, and he encouraged the collection of proverbs in order to  
understand the values and worldview of a people prior to selecting stories to tell. In many  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 34

Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 35  
of these works, proverbs are mentioned in passing but they are not fully explored or given  
serious attention.  
Hatcher \(2001\:475\) notes that poetry and songs are “significant vehicles for  
interaction and communion with God” since “the images, narratives, and melody that  
poetry put to song go beyond mere cognitive expression and involve the affective and  
volitional dimensions of people. Songs communicate a complexity of experience that  
cannot be expressed through abstract language.” Hayward \(1994\) demonstrates how  
contextualization can occur through epic poetry such as *The Heliand\:The Saxon Gospel*  
\(Murphy 1992\), whereby the life of Christ was sung in terms that were meaningful to the  
ninth century Saxons. King \(2004\) affirms this view and contends that the study of  
enthnomusicology can make a significant contribution to missiology. Klem \(1982\)  
demonstrated that singing helped the Yoruba of Nigeria to remember large portions of the  
book of Hebrews in a manner that was emotionally rich. Krabill \(1995\:v\) studied the  
hymns of the Harrist Church among the Dida in Ivory Coast and found that these hymns  
transmitted much of the church’s theology orally since 1913 “with little if any alteration  
throughout the years in worship contexts up until the present day.” This coincides with  
Tienou’s opinion that contextual theology has been occurring to some degree in Africa  
but it has not been widely recognized since it has been in the form of songs, prayers, and  
counseling."  
The prayers and praises of Afua Kuma \(1980\) express imagery of Jesus that is  
unique to the Akan worldview, such that Laryea \(2000\:58\) concluded from her grassroots  
theologizing, “the Akan folkloric tradition has the potential of shaping the liturgy and the  
worship of the Church, as well as defining the parameters of theological discourse.”  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 36  
Various genres uniquely communicate in ways that others miss; therefore, an  
understanding of each genre is needed to understand the wider picture of reality. Most of  
the research has focused on an individual genre, largely to the exclusion of others.  
Richardson \(1981\), discusses the value of “redemptive lore” as a way to help  
people understand and respond to Jesus Christ. He refers to the value of proverbs \(81,  
86\), oral tradition \(43, 58\), poetry \(22\), folk dance \(98\), as well as numerous songs and  
stories. Largely, he focused on the cognitive value of this oral literature, but he was  
particularly focused on a “one time” type of experience for the indigenous community to  
the neglect of the daily expressions of life \(such as proverbs that are used every day in  
traditional African culture\). Steffen \(1993\:220\) notes how Richardson was looking  
exclusively for *the* key redemptive analogy for evangelism, whereas “a number of  
redemptive analogies exist within any people group that are appropriate not only for  
evangelism, but also discipleship.”  
Hunter \(2000\:70\) noted the Celtic evangelism in the fifth - ninth centuries was  
effective, in part, since “Christianity gave them outlets for expressing their constructive  
emotions through indigenous oratory, storytelling, poetry, music, dance, drama, etc. in  
God’s service.” Finnegan \(1970\) analyzed the overall oral nature of African oral  
literature to include poetry, songs, rhymes, narratives, proverbs, riddles, drama, and drum  
language but she did not deal with the use of oral literature in contextualization.  
Anyidoho et al \(1983\) discuss linguistic aspects of various folklore such as proverbs,  
riddles, stories, etc. When describing how culture is learned, Samovar et al \(1998\:39\)  
state, “We learn our culture through proverbs.” Subsequently, they describe how culture  
is also learned from folk tales, legends, myths, art, and mass media. Hughes \(1984\)  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. advocated that actual beliefs \(those that influence behavior and action\) instead of  
religious traditions and banked beliefs \(thoughts remembered but not affecting daily  
action\) must be understood in order to begin to understand a culture and to develop  
contextual theology. He \(1984\:251-252\) recommended, “Beliefs they hold about the  
universe and how to live in it are often found in their folk tales and their proverbs.” Like  
others, these works did not expand on how proverbs can be useful in promoting  
contextual theology.  
Oral literature genres are often combined. Commenting on the Lugbara culture,  
Dalforo \(1996\:64\) noted how a proverb often formed the conclusion of a story. Vlach  
\(1973\) observed that folk tales often illustrate proverbs or proverbs are merely a  
summary of the longer folk tale. Yankah \(1983a\:88\) described the close relationship  
between proverbs and riddles; “what both proverb and riddle require of the audience is an  
act of interpretation in working out terms of the comparison.” The interrelationship of the  
various genres again suggests the need to explore each genre in order to have a more  
complete view of reality in that culture.  
Each genre communicates and nuances different views of reality since they each  
allow us to comprehend certain aspects while hiding other aspects. Each of these genres  
must be understood if one is to understand African primal religion, just as each plan sheet  
is needed to understand how to construct a building. While several of the above genres  
have been widely studied, the genre of proverbs has been less studied and understood.  
The result is that gaps exist in our view of certain aspects of African culture and primal  
religion. This research focuses on the genre of proverbs, and demonstrates how the  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 38  
serious study of Builsa proverbs can fill in some of those gaps, thereby facilitating  
contextualization. Proverbs, then, can be critical to promoting contextualization.  
Genre of Proverbs  
Proverbs are a critical genre to help understand Builsa culture and primal religion.  
Van Rheenen \(1991\:39\) discusses how “oral cultures, which are prevalent in Third World  
societies, are proverb-oriented.. proverbs and myths are significantly helpful in  
deciphering animistic worldviews.” He then adds, “To learn as much of the new culture  
as possible, a new cross-cultural worker must learn the oral forms of his adopted people,  
in particular proverbs and myths” \(1991\:44\). Seidensticker \(1987\:8\) adds, “Proverbs are a  
compact treatise on the values of culture,” but again no details are given concerning what  
process can be followed. John Pobee \(Nussbaum 1996-98\) explained that “proverbs are a  
vital and important mode of communicating and key to penetrating the worldview of  
Africans.” Pobee \(1979\:75-77\) goes a step further than many others by illustrating the  
natural revelation of God in Akan culture with various proverbs. Kudadjie \(1996\:14\)  
affirms that “The collective thought, beliefs, and values of an African people can be  
discerned from their proverbs,” and he gives examples of the use of proverbs in  
preaching. These are good steps but they do not go far enough to explain to the Builsa  
church how they can use proverbs to begin developing contextual theology.  
Perhaps the value of proverbs to Africans is best illustrated by Samuel Adjei  
Crowther, who was considered “one of the greatest African church leaders in the  
nineteenth century” \(Hildebrandt 1996\:103\). In a letter to Henry Venn in 1862  
concerning the tragic loss of his papers in a house fire, Crowther \(Hastings 1994\:279\)  
wrote  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 39  
My collection of words and proverbs in Yoruba, of eleven years’ constant  
observations since the publication of the last edition of my Yoruba vocabulary,  
were also completely destroyed. The loss of those is greater to me than anything  
else, in as much as it cannot be recovered with money nor can I easily recall to  
memory all the collections I had made during my travels at Rabba and through  
Yoruba country, in which places I kept my ears open to every word to catch what  
I had not then secured.  
While Africans have placed great value on their proverbs, unfortunately, this was  
not widely shared by missionaries in the past. For example, Nussbaum \(1996-  
98\:Introduction\) observed, “In some extreme cases such as Liberia and Swaziland,  
missionaries took such a negative view that to this day there is a virtual taboo on the use  
of any proverb in Christian preaching.” A notable exception is Johannes Christaller  
\(Christaller 1879\) who compiled 3600 Twi proverbs in Ghana that were later translated  
by Lange \(Christaller 2000\[1879\]\). The significance of this work is noted by the  
following praise given by Sanneh \(1993\:82-83\),  
One missionary linguist who left a permanent mark on the African vernacular  
scene was Johannes Christaller, a Basel missionary of German origin who arrived  
in Ghana in the 1840s .... One of the fruits of his labour was an impressive  
compilation in 1870 of some 3600 Twi proverbs and idioms that provides an  
invaluable and unique insight into the Akan worldview.  
Only recently, missionaries have begun to appreciate the value of proverbs in  
Africa. Two five-day international conferences were held in 1995 to discuss African  
proverbs. In Mozambique, the “Proverbs and African Christianity” conference was held  
\(March, 1995\), while the consultation entitled, “Embracing the Baobob\: The African  
Proverb in the 21st Century” was held in South Africa \(October, 1995\). The proceedings  
from both are recorded on a CD ROM \(Nussbaum 1996-98 .Record 14/78\). Both  
conferences “raised the profile of the African proverb” \(Nussbaum 1996-98 \:Record  
18/78\), and they increased the networking of historians, theologians, philosophers, and  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 40  
cultural researchers in African proverbs research.  
The African Proverbs Project CD was a major step forward in proverbs research.  
It contains over 25,000 proverbs from at least 27 different languages, along with  
bibliographies, and research studies. For example, in the CD, Joyce Penfield \(1996\:1024\)  
notes, “African proverbs are the sacred texts of African Traditional Religion and thus are  
a paradigm for understanding sacred tradition.” This claim indicates how the study of  
proverbs can be critical to understanding primal religion. Following the African proverbs  
project, regional centers were initiated in South African, Ghana, Kenya, and Cote d’  
Ivoire for data collection and research promotion. Even still, the African Proverbs Project  
did not provide practical tools or processes for churches and missionaries to follow in  
order to use proverbs in encouraging contextual theologizing.  
A web site \(www.afriprov.org\) now is also devoted to African proverbs, stories,  
and sayings that includes daily proverbs along with a “Proverb of the month” that  
provides an explanation of the meaning and biblical application. Missionaries and  
nationals maintain the site. The Sukuma Research Committee in Tanzania also met to  
collect and reflect on proverbs and other oral literature. This has resulted in the  
articulation of an African narrative theology \(Healey and Sybertz 2000\[1996\]\). While this  
is helpful to demonstrate an example of African narrative theology, little mention is made  
of the process that was followed to arrive at this theology. This is critical if contextual  
theology is to be done in various cultures. More will be said on this later.  
Section Summary  
A better understanding of Builsa primal religion is needed in order to deal with  
“Split-Level Christianity;” otherwise, large aspects of Builsa culture and primal religion  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 41  
will remain untouched by Scripture. Various genres exist within cultures, and these  
different genres provide different views, values, and feelings about reality, just as each  
blueprint reveals and filters various aspects of the entire set. The genre of proverbs  
provides an important view to facilitate an understanding of Builsa primal religion. If  
proverbs are neglected, then an important blueprint revealing the culture is missing that  
no other genre can fully replace.  
A survey of research on African Primal Religion indicates that, while other genres  
have been more thoroughly studied, the genre of proverbs has often been neglected. This  
has left a gap in our understanding of Builsa culture and primal religion. Only recently,  
proverbs are being given more than a passing glance. This research will demonstrate how  
proverbs can fill a vital gap, resulting in the contextualization of Christianity among the  
Builsa people.  
How the genre of proverbs is constructed and used in communication in Builsa  
culture is the topic of the next section.  
Communication Theory
