Communication theory can help explain how proverbs function in Builsa culture  
as symbols. Symbols help to construct reality and communicate this within a culture.  
They then serve as a link to connect the inner view of reality with the external reality.  
Communication Defined/Delimited  
It is difficult to find a single unified definition of communication. Dance and  
Larson \(1972\) reviewed the literature over 30 years ago and identified 126 different  
definitions. Since that time, several definitions have been added and the field of  
communications has significantly broadened. Noting the close relationship between  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 42  
understanding culture and the process of communication, Hall \(1959\:217\) notes, “Culture  
is communication and communication is culture.” Littlejohn \(2002\:3\) notes, “Much  
disagreement exists about what constitutes an adequate theory of communication” since  
“communication is one of the most pervasive, important, and complex aspects of human  
life.” He advocates looking at all of the theories and then selecting those which are most  
helpful for a particular study.  
Transmission model. Communication has been commonly defined as the  
“transmission or exchange of ideas” \(Craig 2001\:125\). This transmission model “is  
conceptualized as a process in which meaning, packaged in symbolic messages like  
bananas in crates, are transported from sender to receiver” \(Craig 2001\:125\) with  
interference in between. While this description may be criticized as a caricature, it serves  
to highlight the main elements of the transmission model\: sender \(source\), message, and  
receiver. Aristotle \(Kennedy 1991\) is the early classical advocate of the Source-Message-  
Receiver transmission model, where he dealt with the ethos of the speaker, the logos of  
the message, and the pathos of the receivers.  
Recently, the transmission model has been critiqued by others who claim that  
communication is more complex than previously thought. For example, Relevance theory  
shows that context is as important as text. Instead of transmitting information between  
people, Relevance Theory sees communication “as a matter of enlarging mutual cognitive  
environments, not of duplicating thoughts” \(Sperber and Wilson 1995\:193\). In this view,  
people selectively pay attention to stimuli based upon what they consider to be relevant.  
Hill \(2003\) found Relevance Theory helped explain how cross-cultural communication  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 43  
was often laden with contextual mismatches, which required contextual adjustment  
materials. This insight is not apparent using the transmission model.  
This transmission model is helpful to explain why some proverbs are more readily  
appreciated than other proverbs in particular audiences with particular speakers. I have  
heard Builsa say that people will not take you seriously if you do not know and use  
proverbs in speech. The proverbs carry forward the authority of the ancestors, which  
gives weight to the ethos of the messenger. The use of proverbs also ties into the deeper  
aspects of cultural understanding such that a connection is made with the pathos of the  
Builsa audience. One Builsa said, “When you speak in proverbs, it is like you are singing  
a song that I want to hear. You can sing that song all day long and I will listen.” While  
these aspects of proverbs are helpful, the focus of this research is contextualization of  
Christianity. For this focus, another communication model may be more helpful.  
Constitutive model. Some communication theorists are now favoring a  
“constitutive model” as an alternative to the classical transmission model. Craig  
\(2001\:125\) notes,  
The constitutive model posits instead that the elements of communication, rather  
than being fixed in advance, are reflexively constituted within the act of  
communication itself. Communication is defined as an ongoing process that  
symbolically forms and re-forms our personal identities, our social relations, our  
common world of meaningful objects and events, our ideas and feelings, and our  
routine ways of expressing these socially constructed realities.. .The field of  
communication expands to include all aspects of the creation and negotiation of  
meaning in society.  
The constitutive model of communication recognizes that communication often does  
more than send a message; something is often constructed in the process. Rynkiewich  
\(2002\:315\) notes that culture is contingent upon “historical and present regional and  
global flows of objects, images, ideas, and people” such that culture is constructed and  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 44  
contested in the communication process. The constitutive model of communication then  
addresses the construction of reality within a culture. This may provide clues into how  
proverbs function and are constructed/modified in the Builsa culture, and it offers a more  
dynamic view of Builsa culture instead of merely a static view. This is extremely useful  
in determining how proverbs may help facilitate the construction of Builsa Christian  
theology.  
While the transmission model may help to explain why particular proverbs are  
effective in certain situations, the constitutive model may give greater insight into how  
Builsa Christian reality can be constructed, thereby encouraging contextualization. For  
this reason, the constitutive model of communication will be followed in this literature  
review.  
Carey \(1975\:10\) proposed that “communication is a symbolic process whereby  
reality is produced, maintained, repaired, and transformed.” This definition proposes that  
the construction of reality and the use of symbols are central to communication. It also  
incorporates the fact that communication is a highly social process that includes feelings  
and values as well as ideas. In addition, it leaves room to explain why communication  
does not always achieve the intended goal but it is continually negotiated and  
constructed.  
Social Construction  
John Locke \(1959 \[1690\]\) proposed that meanings are in people, not in words,  
since words have no natural meaning in themselves. People use words to reflect and  
interface with reality. In order to understand how meaning is created, several theories  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 45  
have been proposed. It will be shown that proverbs are critical links in this process of  
constructing reality within the Builsa culture.  
Berger and Luckman \(1966\) posited that humans construct society and then  
society constructs humans \(see Figure 4 on the next page\). As the society constructs  
reality, proverbs help form the sum total of “what everybody knows” about a social  
world... \[via\] an assemblage of maxims, morals, proverbial nuggets of wisdom, values and  
beliefs, myths,” etc. This view of reality becomes “the framework within which anything  
not yet known will come to be known in the future” \(Berger and Luckman 1966\:66\). This  
helps to shape what reality is and what it should look like. Any deviation from this is a  
departure from reality. Farrell \(2001\:723\) affirms, “truisms, maxims, and conventions of  
social knowledge are not only ‘materially’ true; they also routinely reaffirm the  
cultural... routine. In other words, they become a sort of normative resource for propping  
up the social system as we know it.” In other words, reality is socially constructed and  
maintained, with proverbs being an important vehicle for the deployment of symbols in  
that process.  
In the process of enculturation, this normative inner view of reality is passed on  
by symbols. The symbols help to reaffirm the view of reality and to “legitimate” it. Carey  
\(1989\:30\) adds, “We first produce the world by symbolic work and then take up  
residence in the world we have produced.” Leeds-Hurwitz \(1993\:33\) adds, “By extension  
we are prone to forgetting our own role in creating the social world, forgetting equally  
that *we have the power to change it* \[italics mine\].” This is an important point since we  
are ultimately looking for ways to construct a Builsa Christian worldview. By using  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 46  
# Words forming thought and vice versa  
Figure 4\: Humans Construct Society; Society then Constructs Humans \(Hayakwa and  
Hayakwa 1990\[1941\]\:78\).  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 47  
symbols \(such as proverbs\) as building blocks, then the Christian community has power  
to help change their view of reality.  
Fisk \(1987\:4\) summarizes this view of communication,  
The point is that “reality” is already encoded, or rather the only way we can  
perceive and make sense of reality is by the codes of our culture. There may be  
an objective, empiricist reality out there, but there is no universal objective way of  
perceiving and making sense of it. What passes for reality in any culture is the  
product of that culture’s codes, so “reality” is always already encoded, it is never  
“raw.”  
While he does not specifically discuss symbols, the context is clear. Leeds-Hurwitz  
\(1993\:154\) discusses how symbols are the basic unit of culture that combine to form  
common codes that then combine to interpret and create culture.  
To construct reality, language \(the most common symbol system\) plays a crucial  
role. Within the Buli language, proverbs are considered “sweet talk” that is highly  
esteemed and appreciated \(Moon 2004\). As such, they play a vital role in the construction  
of the Builsa view of reality.  
Language  
Before discussing symbols in general, the critical role of language must be  
addressed. Spradley \(1979\:17\) affirmed the theoretical value of symbolic interactionism  
and noted how language helps construct reality, “Language is more than a means of  
communication about reality\: it is a tool for constructing reality. Different languages  
create and express different realities. They categorize experience in different ways.  
They provide alternative patterns for customary ways of thinking and proceeding.” He  
advocates ethnographic interviewing as a tool to uncover how the symbols within  
language are used to construct reality.  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 48  
Edward Sapir and Benjamin Lee Whorf \(1921\) developed their famous hypothesis  
to describe how people of different languages actually live in different worlds. In a  
classic passage, Sapir \(1958 \[1929\]\:69\) stated,  
Human beings do not live in the objective world alone, nor alone in the world of  
social activity as ordinarily understood, but are very much at the mercy of the  
particular language which has become the medium of expression for their society.  
It is quite an illusion to imagine that one adjusts to reality essentially without the  
use of language and that language is merely an incidental means of solving  
specific problems of communication or reflection. The fact of the matter is that  
the 'real world' is to a large extent unconsciously built upon the language habits of  
the group. No two languages are ever sufficiently similar to be considered as  
representing the same social reality. The worlds in which different societies live  
are distinct worlds, not merely the same world with different labels attached... We  
see and hear and otherwise experience very largely as we do because the language  
habits of our community predispose certain choices of interpretation.  
In other words, “The language a person internalizes affects the way he perceives the  
world around him” \(Langness 1987 \[1974\]\: 106\). Thiong’o \(1986\) applied this hypothesis  
to the African context when describing how the African mind was colonized. He noted  
that, during colonialization, “Language was the means of the spiritual subjugation”  
\(Thiong'o 1986\:9\); therefore he stresses that Africans still need to decolonize their minds  
and regain their African identity by recapturing their own languages. He \(Thiong'o  
1986\:15\) forcefully states that language  
is an image-forming agent in the mind of a child. Our whole conception of  
ourselves as a people, individually and collectively, is based on those pictures  
and images which may or may not correctly correspond to the actual reality of the  
struggles with nature and nurture which produced them in the first place.  
Thiong’o \(1986\:15-16\) then ties the communication process to the social construction of  
reality,  
Language as communication and as culture are then products of each other.  
Communication creates culture\: culture is a means of communication. Language  
carries culture, and culture carries, particularly through orature \[oral literature\]  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 49  
and literature, the entire body of values by which we come to perceive ourselves  
and our place in the world.  
Mucherera also observes how language powerfully shapes the inner view of  
reality within a culture. When discussing the affect of colonialism and the required use of  
the English language, Mucherera \(2001\:28\) is sympathetic to Thiong’o, as he states,  
“One of the most noticeable signs of what this author sees as cultural colonialism in the  
present Zimbabwe is the use of the English language.” Other Africans are also  
rediscovering the critical role of language and how this relates to identity. Quarshie  
\(2002\:11\) affirms, “When people abandon their languages, they give up on more than  
simply words; they deny to a certain degree their very identity... .Africans will have to  
draw inspiration from their mother tongue and therefore from their cultural  
consciousness.”  
The role of the vernacular language in constructing the inner view of reality has  
also been noted by African theologians. Pobee \(1979\:23\) emphasizes, “Ideally, African  
theologies should be in the vernacular. Language is more than syntax and morphology; it  
is the vehicle for assuming the weight of a culture. Therefore, this attempt to construct an  
African theology in the English language is the second best.” Sanneh has long stressed  
how vernacular Scriptures and Christian materials have strengthened the church in  
Africa. He \(1989\:207\) points out the relation between the vernacular language and the  
releasing of inner creativity,  
It is instructive that cultural creativity in much of Africa and elsewhere coincides  
almost exactly with developed interest in the vernacular and with a willingness to  
do things differently from the way they have been done. As a force, translatability  
endows persons and societies with the reason for change and the language with  
which to affect it.  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 50  
If the Builsa are to be empowered to contextualize Christianity in their culture,  
then this must be done in their own language. Builsa proverbs are creative expressions of  
the Builsa people that are unique to their own mother tongue. When discussing Builsa  
proverbs, the Builsa are on their “own turf’ since they are using their own language and  
reasoning processes. As such, the appropriate use of proverbs promotes additional  
creativity and opens further worlds of discovery. When tapped into, this creative force  
can encourage contextualization.  
To further understand why language is such a powerful component for  
constructing a culture’s inner view of reality, a study of symbols and signs should be  
helpful.  
Signs and Symbols  
Signs and symbols have been studied in various ways, leading to different  
implications and applications. I will briefly trace the literature and illustrate the major  
positions. These positions are summarized in Figure 5 on the next page. This will  
demonstrate how symbols do more than point to an inner constructed view of reality  
within a culture; they also point to an external reality as people dialogue within a  
community.  
Ferdinand de Saussure \(1983\[1916J\) was a pioneer in semiotics \(the study of sign  
systems\). He divided signs into inner mental images and outer experiencable forms. This  
meant that meaning was totally constructed in a culture and was only in the mind. Taken  
to its logical conclusion, this leads to cognitive relativism that is not helpful in our pursuit  
of how Scripture can be the interpreter of culture. Ogden and Richards \(1946\:1-23\) did  
not accept this dyadic nature of signs. Rather, they felt that signs had a triadic nature.  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 51  
*Inner Mental Image Reality Mental Image \(Signification\)*  
*Outer Form Symbol*  
*Signifier \(Sign\) Reality \(Signified\)*  
*1\. D e Saussere*  
*\(Instrumentalist*  
*epistemology\)*  
*2\. Naive Realist*  
*epistemology*  
*3\. Pierce, Hiebert \(Critical*  
*Realist*  
*epistemology\)*  
*Thought \(Reference\)*  
*4\. Ogden/Richards*  
*Word \(Symbol\) Thing \(Referent\)*  
*Meaning*  
*5\. Siran*  
*\(regarding proverbs\)*  
*SigniTication Value*  
*External Reality*  
*6\. Dialogue in Community*  
*Person 1*  
*Person 2*  
*thought 1 thought 2*  
*Symbol*  
Figure 5\: Models of Signs/Symbols and Associated Epistemologies  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** **5 2**  
They created the semantic triangle to show how symbols \(particularly words\) connect to  
an external referent, as well as in internal thought. The word \(as a symbol\) refers to an  
external thing \(referent\) and attaches thought \(reference\).  
Charles Pierce \(1955\[1940\]\), a contemporary of de Saussure, also viewed the  
triadic nature of signs to include\: signifier \(Sign\) - Mental Image \(Signification\) - and  
Reality \(Signified\). In this view, signs help to connect the inner thought world to the  
outward realities that exist. This affirms the existence of an external objective reality and  
does not leave the inner meaning totally subjective in the mind. Hiebert \(1999\) finds this  
position more attractive from an evangelical standpoint. Instead of being totally  
subjective, Hiebert \(1999\:72-73\) notes that this triadic model “holds that all knowledge  
has both objective and subjective dimensions to it.” He uses this model to advocate an  
epistemology of critical realism, as opposed to instrumentalism \(implied by de Saussure’s  
model of signs\), or naive realism \(signs are exact representations of reality\).  
Siran \(1993\) applied symbolic theory to proverbs. Though using different  
terminology \(signification, value, and meaning\), he identified three aspects of proverbs\:  
literal text that can be translated \(signification\), literal meaning in light of social and  
historical context \(value\), and symbolic or connotative meaning \(meaning\). By regarding  
proverbs as symbols, fresh areas of cultural discovery emerge. For example, Awedoba  
\(2000\:68\) used this approach with 266 Kasena111 proverbs with slight modifications, since  
he felt that “Kasena proverbs are mostly symbols.” Howell \(1997\), an anthropologist who  
has studied and written about the language and culture of the Kasena for over 20 years,  
commented that her reading of Awedoba’s work \(who himself is a Kasena\) on proverbs  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 53  
revealed deep insights into Kasena culture and religion that she had never known  
before.lv  
While each of the theories of symbols described above contain some truth to  
them, they do not sufficiently incorporate the aspects of dialogue or performance within a  
community, perhaps due to the fact that these theories arose from Western cultures that  
were very individualistic in orientation. When viewed through the eyes of cultures where  
identity comes from belonging to the community, one sees how symbols are constructs of  
the community through dialogue and not merely constructed by individuals.  
In the constitutive model of communication, symbols construct something in the  
process of communication. Symbols are a critical building block to connect the inner  
reality with the external reality for people. In dialogue together, person A and person B  
use symbols to communicate. The traffic is two-way since symbols are both used and  
received. In the process, the symbols are affirmed, modified, or rejected in this dialogue.  
As a result, the symbols construct reality by connecting an external reality to an inner  
reality, even though the inner reality of person A \(thought 1\) and the inner reality of  
person B \(thought 2\) do not *exactly* correspond to each other, or to the external reality for  
that matter. The point is that the symbols are continually negotiated through dialogue  
within community since they are never exact representations. This concept will be useful  
as we seek to understand how proverbs function as a symbol set to negotiate meaning  
through dialogue in community. This opens the door for proverbs to help construct a  
Builsa Christian worldview.  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 54  
Oral Cultures  
Proverbs, functioning as symbols, are especially important in oral cultures where  
discussions must be remembered in order to be useful. Ong \(1982\) emphasizes that oral  
cultures rely upon formulaic expressions and mnemonic patterns in order for people to  
remember what was said. Proverbs are particularly useful in oral cultures since they are  
often short, pithy statements that are easy to remember. McLuhan \(1964\) emphasizes that  
the primary channel or medium of communication changes the way people perceive  
themselves. His catch phrase was “The medium is the message,” and he emphasized the  
changes in thinking from oral to written to electronic media \(especially television\). These  
distinctives have often been overlooked by missionaries from highly literate cultures.  
Kennedy \(2001\) discusses how the cross cultural study of rhetoric allows  
comparisons between cultures that helps identify features in one culture that may not  
exist in another. In societies without writing, he notes \(2001\:138\), “there is much  
repetition in most traditional speeches and little explicit logical reasoning, though  
references to myth, legend, history, and proverbs may supply examples of what should be  
done in support of the speaker’s proposition.” He \(2001\:142\) adds,  
A complex chain of logical argument is not a feature of persuasion in nonliterate  
societies. Argument from examples - mythological, historical, experiential, or  
analogical - has probably been the most common form of reasoning all over the  
world, and proverbs are a basis of demonstration in many cultures, though often  
universal propositions are not articulated.  
This indicates one reason that those from a literate background may overlook the value  
that oral cultures place upon proverbs. The logic and repetition of proverbs is highly  
valued in oral cultures but it is not generally appreciated in literate cultures.  
The place of proverbs then is not a peripheral one in oral cultures; rather, proverbs  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** **55**  
function as a powerful symbol set in oral cultures to facilitate the connection of inner  
reality to external reality by providing formulaic or mnemonic expressions to aid recall.  
According to Barrett \(1997\), the non-literates and semi-literates in the world probably  
outnumber literates, while Klem \(1995\) notes that this percentage is higher \(over 75%\) in  
Africa. If the majority of the world values a symbol system that includes the genre of  
proverbs, then proverbs should no longer be overlooked for use in contextualization.  
Section Summary  
In a constitutive model of communication, proverbs function as important  
symbols to help construct the culture’s inner view of reality. Within a culture, their inner  
view of reality is constructed, maintained, and communicated to others by the use of  
symbols. As shown in Figure 6 on the next page, symbols connect the external reality to  
the inner realities of those in dialogue, even though the correspondence between them is  
not exact. Language is a key component since it powerfully shapes how people view and  
express this concept of reality. Although often overlooked by outsiders, the language of  
proverbs are highly valued and appreciated in Builsa culture as the epitome of “sweet  
talk.” Proverbs are also particularly important in oral cultures since they provide a readily  
available mnemonic device to help construct, communicate, and perpetuate the inner  
concept of reality. Proverbs, then, function as a valued and available symbol system that  
can help Builsa construct a theology that is fully Builsa and fully Christian. This begins  
to fill the gap between the need for contextualization and tools/perspectives to do so. In  
order to facilitate the contextualization of Christianity in Builsa culture, Builsa proverbs  
are a good place to start.  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 56  
# External Reality  
Inner Concept of Reality 1 Inner Concept of Reality 2  
SYMBOLS link two worlds in a constitutive process  
Figure 6\: Symbols Help Construct and Communicate Reality  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 57  
An understanding of religious symbols will further describe how proverbs may reveal  
African primal religion and culture.  
Religious Symbols  
Proverbs can function as religious symbols that are windows revealing the Builsa  
worldview. Mbiti \(1969\:1\) begins his seminal work on African religions and  
philosophies,  
Africans are notoriously religious, and each people has its own religious system  
with a set of beliefs and practices. Religion permeates into all the departments of  
life so fully that it is not easy or possible always to isolate it. A study of these  
religious systems is, therefore, ultimately a study of the peoples themselves in all  
the complexities of both traditional and modern life.  
Since “there is no distinction between the sacred and the secular, between the religious  
and the non-religious” \(Mbiti 1969\:2\), religious symbols will be found in the daily life of  
Africans. Proverbs function as religious symbols that deal with the complexities of  
African life, thereby serving as a window for one to look through and see the soul of the  
culture.  
Look *Through*  
Several paremiologists have done extensive studies by only looking *at* proverbs.  
Taylor’s classic text, “The Proverb” \(1962 \[1931\]\) looked *at* proverbs to discover their  
origin, content, style, etc. Meider \(1993\) also looked *at* proverbs to examine the changes,  
the practice of borrowing across cultures, the flavor of regional oral literature, etc. While  
this is helpful, it does not go far enough to facilitate contextualization of Christianity in  
Builsa culture.  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** **58**  
In addition to looking *at* proverbs, one can also look *through* proverbs. Shea  
\(1987\:107\) comments, “Images are not so much what we see as what we see through.”  
The focus is on seeing behind the proverbs to understand what they reveal of the culture  
and people. This is consistent with how McLaren \(2002\:57\) advocates looking at the life  
of Jesus, “God communicated his message, not *in spite* of Jesus’ humanity, but *through*  
*it, because o f it. “* \(emphasis in original\).  
In the enlightenment paradigm, looking *at* something was considered more  
scientific and exact. In a postmodern age, this is being questioned and alternative views  
are being explored to look *through* it. Bosch \(1991\:353\) noted,  
Metaphor, symbol, ritual, sign, and myth, long maligned by those interested only  
in “exact” expressions of rationality, are today being rehabilitated.. .they not only  
touch the mind and its conceptions, and evoke action with a purpose, but compel  
the heart.  
This alternative view can lead to breakthroughs as noted by Richardson  
\(1981\:99\). “Missionary breakthroughs among the Karen, Kachin, and Lahu tribes came  
*through* their respective folk religions, rather than in spite of them.”  
This view of looking *through* is perhaps best summarized by the following  
metaphor of a window by Warfield. “A glass window stands before us. We raise our eyes  
and see the glass; we note its quality, and observe its defects; we speculate on its  
composition. Or we look straight through it on the great prospect of land and sea and sky  
beyond” \(cited in Gire 1996\:15\). Instead of looking *at* proverbs and describing their  
origins, style, changes, and regional variations, proverbs can be looked *through* to see the  
worldview that they reveal.  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** **5 9**  
Windows into Worldview  
Hiebert et al \(1999\:247\) note, “At the center of a culture are its sacred symbols,”  
and “the study of their sacred symbols, in particular, provides a window on a people’s  
understanding of ultimate reality” \(1999\:252\). Ellwood \(1983\:100\) also emphasizes,  
“Even the plainest symbols can open up a virtual universe of transcendent feeling and  
meaning for those who understand. They are magic portals into the other world where the  
truth of ones’ religion is visible, felt, and far overshadows the inconsistent ordinary.”  
Religious symbols then can open the meaning of a religious system that is otherwise  
hidden from plain view. In searching for new tools and perspectives for contextualization,  
religious symbols can potentially open levels of reality that have otherwise been hidden.  
In a primal culture, Eliade \(1959\:129-130\) notes that the supreme God is not  
usually considered to be active; therefore, “the celestial sacred remains active through  
symbolism” since “it is through symbols that the world becomes transparent, is able to  
show the transcendent.” This underscores how the religious symbols become windows to  
keep primal cultures in touch with the “real reality,” i.e., the sacred.  
Geertz \(1973\:90\) defined religion as “a system of symbols. . .”, which he describes  
as providing a model *o f* reality \(a gloss or explanation of what reality is\) and a model *for*  
reality \(a template for what reality should be\) \(1973\:92-94\). While Geertz’s definition  
falls short, since it does not recognize the existence of the supernatural and the supreme  
God, his definition is helpful since it encourages the analysis of religious symbols in  
order to understand the religious system. Zahniser \(1997\) advocates using those symbols  
in traditional religious societies that connect ultimate reliable meaning and intimate  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 60  
relevant daily issues. The question is\: Which symbols are most useful to start with as  
windows that reveal the worldview of the culture?  
Open Windows  
The various genres discussed previously could be potential windows to look  
through to see the culture. Several have identified the prevalence and value of proverbs.  
The following are just a few. In oral cultures, Hiebert et al \(1999\:252\) note that people  
often “use images, masks, dress, art, dramas, stories, rituals, songs, chants, proverbs,  
riddles...to symbolize their beliefs”. Schreiter \(1985\) identifies art, folklore, ritual, etc. as  
“culture texts” that can be read to understand the culture. Mbiti \(1969\:86\) lamented the  
lack of study concerning proverbs and observed, “Proverbs are common ways of  
expressing religious ideas and feelings... It is in proverbs that we find the remains of the  
oldest forms of African religious and philosophical wisdom.” Nkansah-Obrempong  
\(2002\:38\) affirmed this view as he noted, “ In these \[Akan\] proverbs, therefore, one can  
see the wisdom and soul of the Akan people.” These authors affirm that proverbs can be  
religious symbols to be looked *through* instead of just looked *at.* Unfortunately, there are  
only a few examples of how this can be done.  
Ghanaian philosopher and author, Kwame Gyekye \(1996\), exemplifies how Akan  
proverbs can be analyzed in order to highlight the meaning and extract the African values  
embedded within. He grouped the proverbs and analyzed their content in order to give a  
broad understanding of the African worldview. Gyekye \(1995\) also utilized proverbs in  
order to construct a moral philosophy of the Akan people. In doing so, he attempted to  
describe a general African worldview and philosophy. While this is helpful, there is no  
engagement with Scripture to help interpret this worldview and philosophy.  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 61  
When advocating the use of proverbs in evangelism and theological reflection,  
Gibbs \(2000\:189\) also stated that proverbs “provide a window into people’s worldview  
and store of moral values.” He then added \(2000\:187-188\), “Literal language is limited in  
its capacity to express religious and aesthetic concepts. Symbolic language has greater  
potential for creating links between the known and the unknown or unthought.” While  
recognizing the potential of proverbs to express religious concepts, he still does not  
provide examples as to how this can be performed. He does, however, recognize that  
proverbs, functioning as religious symbols, can express more than literal language. A  
look at the property of symbols to form links or bridges will be helpful in describing how  
proverbs can express both cognitive and sensory experiences. This will move us another  
step closer to realizing the potency of proverbs in Builsa culture.  
Symbols Bridge  
Victor Turner found that symbols polarized meaning between two poles\: sensory  
and ideology. Turner \(1967a\:28\) explained these terms, “At the sensory pole are  
concentrated those significata that may be expected to arouse desires and feelings; at the  
ideological pole one finds an arrangement of norms and values that guide and control  
persons as members of social groups.” Hiebert et al \(1999\:247\) discuss sacred symbols  
using somewhat different terms, but conveying a similar meaning, by noting that  
they integrate and give expression to a people’s worldview - the mental picture  
they have of the way things in reality actually are \(the cognitive dimension\); the  
tone, character, and aesthetic quality of their life \(the affective dimension\); and  
the moral standards that set their ideals and governs their relationships \(the  
evaluative dimensions\).  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 62  
Proverbs, functioning as religious symbols, powerfully link both feelings and ideas. This  
has largely been overlooked by most researchers; instead, they have focused on studying  
either the sensory or the ideology poles but not both together.  
Several studies focus largely on the didactic and normative value of proverbs  
\(Christensen 1958; Shelton 1963\), and a few on the aesthetic value of proverbs \(Boadi  
1972; Yankah 1983b\). Only a few focus on both \(Nwachukwu-Agbada 1994; Patnaik  
1982\). Both poles are significant in forming “deep symbols” \(Farley 1996\). According to  
Farley \(1996\:3\), “deep symbols have at least the following four features\: normativity,  
enchantment, fallibility \(relativity and corruptibility\), and location in a master narrative.”  
Normativity includes ideals and norms. Enchantment refers to mystery, novelty, right  
brain, “the way finite reality participated in sacred power, the infinite creativity” \(Farley  
1996\:5\), or the emotions. Fallibility refers to the fact that they were created in community  
in a certain historical age that may change; also, they can corrupt those with power by  
maintaining their privilege. This has implications for both culture change and power  
abuse/prejudice in the Builsa culture \(to be discussed in the next section\). Many proverbs  
fit the characteristics of deep symbols, which again helps to highlight their importance in  
Builsa culture.  
Since symbols combine both the ideology and senses, they are powerful windows  
to look through and experience in order to understand Builsa culture and religion.  
Proverbs, functioning as religious symbols, serve as open windows, giving invaluable  
perspectives into worldviews.  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 63  
Section Summary  
Religious symbols open levels of reality to experience Builsa religion and culture  
that would otherwise be hidden. When proverbs are looked *through* instead of merely  
looked *at,* they can function as religious symbols. This then opens windows into  
understanding and experiencing Builsa religion and culture that would otherwise be  
closed. Since proverbs are so prevalent in Builsa culture, they become windows that are  
open every day. Once opened, these windows invite fresh areas of discovery.  
Since symbols combine both the sensory and ideological aspects, they affect both  
the head and the heart. Proverbs, functioning as symbols, can facilitate contextualization  
that reaches both the head and heart of the Builsa. As deep symbols, proverbs reach deep  
within culture and carry the potential to touch the deeper aspects of the Builsa worldview.  
That is the level that is necessary to reach in order to overcome “split-level Christianity.”  
Proverbs have the potential to address significant aspects of “split-level  
Christianity.” While many concur with the powerful potential of proverbs to do this, none  
give details as to how this can be done. This research fills this gap. Before  
contextualization can occur, however, the Scripture must engage these deeper aspects of  
Builsa culture. That process is the topic of our next discussion.  
Contextualization in Africa  
Contextualization occurs as the mother tongue Scripture and culture are engaged,  
leading to a deeper understanding and appreciation of both. This facilitates a contextual  
understanding and communication of Christian truth. While contextualization is highly  
discussed, a survey of previous efforts reveals that there are few results to show for this  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 64  
effort. New tools and perspectives are needed - proverbs can be a catalyst to initiate the  
way forward. The neglect of symbols, such as proverbs, has closed windows that need to  
be opened.  
Neglecting Symbols  
Since symbols are valuable windows into the soul of a culture, the neglect of them  
overlooks the indigenous inner view of reality. In theology, this hampers the  
understanding of Christian truth, as previously noted by several African theologians.  
Yankah \(1982\) found that Africans trained in the West tended not to use proverbs  
frequently, which then put them at a disadvantage when returning home where proverbial  
wisdom is held in high esteem. To understand how the neglect of African symbols such  
as proverbs affect the understanding of Christian truth, a brief history of the recent  
development of African contextual theology is helpful.  
Recent History  
Ritchie \(1993\) traced the development of African contextual theology in four  
phases\: rejection, adaptation, incarnation, and emerging theologies. In the first phase  
\(Rejection\), with few exceptions, African traditional beliefs, practices, and religion were  
considered evil and of no value to Christianity. For example, in the 1910 Edinburgh  
World Missionary Conference \(World 1910\), it was felt that African traditional religion  
contained no preparation for Christianity. As a result, the attitude was that the  
missionaries must start over with a clean slate *\(tabula rasa*\) and start to teach universal  
Christianity. It was not realized at the time that the “universal Christianity” was actually a  
Western contextualized expression of Christianity.  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 65  
Roland Allen started to write about the self-supporting church \(1962\[1912\]\), but  
his thoughts largely concentrated on the relationship between the church to the mission  
and did not consider the development of contextual theology. In 1926, Edwin Smith  
wrote The Golden Stool, which suggested that “Christianity should be presented as not  
antagonistic to, but the fulfillment of, African culture, religion, and tradition.” \(Hastings  
1994\:553-554\). In 1934, the first Catholic clergy to be entirely staffed by Africans with  
no missionary control was in Buganda, but “its clergy were meticulously faithful to the  
“Statutes” regulating the details of their daily life, drawn up on missionary authority”  
\(Hastings 1994\:571-575\). This was necessary in order to survive the experiment amidst  
colonialism and missionary hesitancy. Hence, contextual theology was not a topic under  
discussion.  
Contextualization received a boost with the publication of Placide Tempels’ book,  
Bantu Philosophy \(Tempels 1959\). This was originally published in French in 1945, and  
later translated into English. Parratt \(1995\:10\) notes,  
It was the first attempt to come to grips, other than in a purely descriptive way,  
with African thought forms, and to treat them both analytically and as a coherent  
system. It also set out to show the importance of the study of language and oral  
lore, as well as the accepted methods of ethnology, in bringing to light religious  
and philosophical ideas. In particular, Tempels’ identification of “life-force” as  
the ground of Bantu ontology has contributed to our understanding of African  
thought in general and has provided a base from which several of his successors  
worked.  
Tempels’ work was important in that it started to take African philosophy seriously. His  
work was followed by Catholic African priests, Kagame, Lulufiiabo, and Mulago. Parratt  
\(1995\:10\) notes that Kagame’s work, La Philosphie Bantu Rwandaise de l’Etre “made  
wide-ranging use of linguistics and oral tradition.” While these efforts began to  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** **6 6**  
understand African philosophy as a coherent system of thought, there was no discussion  
of developing a contextual theology.  
In the second phase \(Adaptation\), for the most part, certain outward forms began  
to be adapted to the local culture. In 1963, the All Africa Conference of Churches was  
founded in Kampala. The focus here was on modifying local forms \(e.g., music, dress,  
and involving African clergy\) but this was still done within the framework of Western  
theology. It merely became Western theology with African clothes. Vatican II started to  
look again at theology and culture. Isichei \(1995\:327\) noted, “Vatican II encouraged a  
contextual theology, and a more positive approach to other faith traditions.” The 1968  
Kinshasa Colloquium is a notable example. “Its conclusions were epitomized in  
Mulago’s affirmation that there could be no universal theology, and that it was no longer  
possible to put the clock back and deny to African theologians the freedom to elaborate a  
Christian theology that would not be foreign to the African milieu, tradition of thought,  
mentality, and culture” \(Parratt 1995\:11\). While this affirmation was not fully realized, it  
was prophetic concerning the upcoming trend in the development of contextual  
theologies.  
Ritchie \(1993\) describes the third phase of African contextual theology in the  
1970s as Incarnation. At that time, a host of conferences were convened to include the  
All Africa Council of Churches \(AACC\), the WCC, the Lutheran World Federation, the  
Ecumenical Association of African Theologians and the Ecumenical Association for  
Third World Theologians \(EATWOT\). In 1974, the AACC called for a moratorium of  
Western theologians in Africa. This was significant in that it showed how far Africa had  
come. They had trained theologians who were not content with Western expressions of  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 67  
Christianity; thus, they wanted space to theologize without Western domination. Africans  
began to research African traditional religion and become aware of their religio-cultural  
roots \(Ritchie 1993\). In addition, the EATWOT conference in 1976 was convened since  
the Third World churches did not want to be defined by the rich, the Westerners, and the  
Whites. This was a clear backlash against the Western global theology, and it set the  
stage for the further development of contextual theologies.  
In the fourth phase \(Emerging Theologies\), much is still unknown. There is not a  
single paradigm and it is still emerging \(Ritchie 1993\) since “African theology is in the  
midst of its birth pangs” \(Penoukou 1991\:28\). The word “theologies” is plural since this  
recognizes the different cultural contexts that exist in Africa. The current trends seem to  
be that Africans are striving to address cultural issues as a community of believers in the  
mother tongue. They are using African symbols, themes, oral literature, etc. and their  
own mother tongue categories for theologizing.  
John Pobee is a good example. His approach “will involve the collection,  
analysis, and elucidation of oral literature - myths, prayers, proverbs, and so on. The raw  
materials of African theology thus are biblical-theological on the one hand, and the oral  
traditions of African religion on the other” \(Parratt 1995\:49\). Proverbs then are valuable  
indigenous resources for African theologians to work with in developing contextual  
theologies. Parratt \(1995\:50\) comments on Pobee,  
One outstanding feature of his book is his adroit use of Akan proverbs and  
sayings, by which he attempts to bring out the folk wisdom of Akan  
religion...few have so painstakingly delved in religion and moral oral traditions  
as Pobee does here. In this he has opened up new possibilities for exploring  
common ground between African religions and the Christian faith.  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** **6 8**  
Another example of contextual theologizing is Fashole-Luke. Parratt \(1995\:44-  
45\) observes that “Fashole-Luke recommends a wider use of anthropological findings  
and a thorough study of oral traditions in the analysis of African religions, with a view to  
their use in Christian theology.” Nkansah-Abrenpong has also advocated the use of  
cultural symbols, proverbs, and metaphors for developing African contextual theology.  
He notes \(Nkansah-Obrempong 2002\:38\), “The use of cultural symbols, proverbs and  
metaphors for theology not only involves the attempt to relate theology to culture, but  
also requires a critical reflection on the appropriate means of expression used in theology  
itself.” Proverbs and other indigenous means of expressing theology are being explored,  
but this is still in the early stages of development.  
Other African theologians who are actively expressing African theologies that are  
distinct from Western theology include\: Kwesi Dickson \(1984\), Kwame Bediako \(1995\),  
John Mbiti \(1969\), J.N.K. Mugambi \(1989\), and Mercy Amba Oduyoye \(1986\), to name  
a few. African women are also beginning to contribute their perspectives \(Fabella and  
Oduyoye 1988; Nasimiyu-Wasike 1991\). Isichei \(1995\:311\) adds that South Africa alone  
in the continent has looked to liberation theology due to its struggle with apartheid. She  
notes, “In 1985, 150 signatories produced the *Kairos Document.* It stands in the tradition  
of Liberation Theology.. .It emphasizes the ‘preferential option for the poor’ and those  
biblical passages that proclaim social justice, and denounce oppression, such as the book  
of Amos and the Magnificat.” Isichei \(1995\:331\) suggests that, while Liberation  
Theology has had an impact in South Africa, “the main concern elsewhere is  
inculturation, the incarnation of Christianity in African culture.” She speculates  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 69  
\(1995\:333\), “Perhaps the most successful incarnation of Christianity in African cultural  
life is found in the hymns and liturgies of the prophetic churches, and in art ”  
This brief survey has shown how far the development of contextual theology has  
come in Africa. From the early rejection of African symbols to the recent  
experimentation with symbols, songs, rituals, proverbs, etc., there is now much hope that  
the universal church will hear more from the African church. The understanding and  
application of African symbols like proverbs may be a helpful step forward in this  
process. There is a new openness to the use of proverbs but a clear process to use has not  
yet emerged - the search continues.  
Wav Forward in Contextual Theology  
In searching for a contemporary paradigm for mission, Bosch \(1991\:431\) suggests  
that the best models of contextual theology should hold together theory and praxis, while  
also incorporating *poiesis.* Stackhouse \(1988\:85\) defines *poiesis* as “an imaginative  
creation or representation of evocative images.” Bosch notes that this includes the  
elements of “beauty, the rich resources of symbol, piety, worship, love, awe, and  
mystery.” Bosch \(1991\:431\) adds, “Only too often , in the tug-of-war between the  
priority of truth and the priority of justice, this dimension gets lost.” Bosch advocates the  
use of symbols in theology \(since this has largely been ignored or misunderstood in the  
past\), which cracks open the door to explore proverbs and other oral literature;  
unfortunately, he does not provide any concrete process that can facilitate contextual  
theology.  
Others are also starting to grasp the need to understand and apply symbols in  
contextual theology. Hiebert, Shaw, and Tienou \(1999\:254\) comment, “Contextualization  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 70  
of the gospel in other cultures must begin at the level of signs.” Schreiter \(1985\:28\) also  
affirmed,  
Ideally, for a genuinely contextual theology, the theological process should begin  
with the opening of culture, that long and careful listening to a culture to discover  
its principal values, needs, interests, directions, and symbols. Only in this way  
can the configurations of a culture become apparent of themselves, without  
simply responding to other kinds of needs extrinsic to the culture.  
Schreiter \(1985\:29\) advocates analyzing the cultural symbols in order to find the activity  
of Christ in the culture long before the arrival of the missionary. This approach seeks to  
develop a local theology as the local hermeneutical community “listen\[s\] to its culture in  
order to complement its past experience of Christ. It must be able to recognize the signs  
of Christ’s presence in its midst.” While this theory is sound, it is often easier said than  
done. Again, no one has come forth with a clear approach to analyzing symbols, such as  
proverbs in culture, in order to find the presence of Christ. Still, the yearning to do so  
exists in Africa.  
The plea for discovering and using symbols in African contextual theology is  
poignantly summarized by Afagbegee’s poem, “Cry of an African Christian” \(2001\:66\),  
where he issues “a plea for understanding and sensitivity towards his symbols,” and he  
utters “a groan for patient-love for his mode of expressing himself.” The emotion of this  
poem illustrates the huge gap between the desire for the use of local symbols in  
theologizing and its neglect in practice. A process must be discovered that will fill this  
gap. The use of African communication forms points us in the right direction.  
Local Communication Forms  
Schreiter notes how the forms used to communicate theology are largely  
dependent upon cultural patterns. He observes \(Schreiter 1985\:31\),  
**R eproduced with perm ission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without perm ission.** 71  
Not only is it a matter of how meaning is organized in a culture, but also how it is  
to be communicated.... We cannot presume written texts - with all they in turn  
assume about organization - as the sole form for communicating cultural meaning,  
and therefore theology. Perhaps more African theology will be done via proverbs,  
which are important in communication in sub-Saharan cultures.  
When searching for local communication forms, Schreiter turns to proverbs as a possible  
way forward. In a similar way, Kraft emphasizes that communication must be receptor-  
oriented, whereby the communicator uses local communication forms \(devices\). When  
searching for appropriate African communication forms, he notes \(1979\:161\),  
To relate the message to the real life of one's hearers, the communicator must  
learn to employ the communicational devices familiar to the receptor in ways to  
which the receptor is responsive. Credible messages start from where the receptor  
is. In many cultures this involves the knowledge and use of a variety of proverbs,  
aphorisms, and tales of various kinds. Such literary devices form important  
vehicles within their communicational system.  
Again, proverbs emerge as a communication form that is receptor-oriented in many  
cultures. An appropriate form gives credibility to the seriousness of the message.  
Taber \(1978\:67\) also recognizes the importance of using local communication  
forms, “Indigenous theology must use literary forms and genres that are culturally  
appropriate to the formulation of the most serious religious discourse. This may be  
poetry, or mythology, or some other narrative form, or a ‘wisdom’ type of text; or it may  
find appropriate philosophical forms within the culture.” Proverbs are a type of wisdom  
literature that Builsa appreciate and recognize as serious discourse, as previously noted.  
When used to communicate theology, proverbs are a valuable form of communication.  
When discussing the form that theology should take, Bevans \(2002\:17\) adds,  
“When we begin to take culture and culture change seriously, theological content is not  
the only thing affected. The form of theology comes under the influence of such *loci*, as  
well. In an African culture, for example, the best form of theologizing might be  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 72  
collecting, creating, or reflecting on proverbs.” Again, proverbs are recognized as a form  
of theologizing that may be the most appropriate for the African context. If many authors  
recognize the importance of using proverbs for an appropriate communication form in  
Africa, why is there so little usage of proverbs in contextualization?  
To answer this question, Nussbaum \(2000\) analyzes the wisdom paradigms of  
Western cultures against African cultures and concluded that proverbs, as a form of  
ancient wisdom, are highly regarded in African cultures, but this is often overlooked by  
Western cultures who favor a wisdom paradigm of propositional knowledge. When  
relating this to theology, Schreiter \(1985\:80\) adds that some cultures look upon theology  
as wisdom \(*sapientia*\), whereas others look upon theology as sure knowledge *\{scientia\).*  
He then concludes \(Schreiter 1985\:77\), “Perhaps theology in African villages could best  
be expressed in proverbs rather than in Bantu philosophy.” In short, proverbs may be  
used more in theologizing if the African wisdom paradigm is appreciated and  
encouraged.  
Clearly, appropriate cultural symbols are essential in developing contextual  
theology. Builsa proverbs, functioning as symbols, are important resources for  
theologizing. In addition to the content, proverbs are a communication form that is highly  
esteemed and appreciated in Builsa culture. As a result, they can be an appropriate form  
to help communicate Builsa theology. In order for this to occur, however, there must be  
an engagement of both the cultural proverbs and the Scripture.  
Critical Engagement of Scripture and Culture.  
Developing local theologies is easier said than done. Several writers have  
suggested models to do this. One distinguishing factor is often the starting place for  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **73**  
initiating contextual theology. Schreiter advocates starting with the study of the symbols  
in the culture, as discussed above. He then recommends \(1985\:29-30\), “From the analysis  
of the culture comes the emergence of what we shall call culture texts, in which are  
contained themes that in turn are the cultural nucleus around which a local theology  
develops.” He notes that the themes that emerge will be determined by either a current  
and often urgent need in a culture, or the larger patterns that determine how things are  
done in a culture. While Schreiter provides a step in the right direction, this process is  
still vague and lacking specific directions to make the process more workable.  
Bevans \(2002\:xvi\) describes six models of Contextual Theology, each using the  
elements of the past \(Scripture and tradition\) combined with the present context \(culture,  
individual/social experience, social location, and social change\). He terms these models\:  
Translation, Anthropological, Praxis, Transcendental, Synthetic, and Countercultural.  
The synthetic model is similar to Schreiter’s method. Bevans \(2002\:92-93\) explains,  
One begins with listening to culture for basic patterns and structures, analyzing  
culture in order to discover its basic system of symbols. Out of such a ‘thick  
description’ will emerge basic themes for the local theology. At the same time,  
however, these themes need to be in dialogue with the basic themes in gospel and  
tradition, which are normative inasmuch as they are acknowledged by the  
community as relatively successful local theologies in times past and in varying  
cultures.  
Again, the theory is sound but the process to accomplish this is still not clear. Due to this  
ambiguity, it would be difficult for grass-roots theologizing to blossom. There are still  
more questions than answers.  
Sedmark \(2002\:119\) discusses working with proverbs to develop Tittle  
theologies’ \(“theologies made for a particular situation, taking particular circumstances  
into account, using local questions and concerns, local stories and examples as their  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 74  
starting points”\)- He notes \(2002\:150-151\), “proverbs help us access the situation of a  
local culture”; the wisdom contained in the proverbs is then useful as the local  
hermeneutical community will “reflect on this wisdom in the light of gospel values.”  
While this acknowledgement of the validity of proverbs in theologizing is helpful, it  
appears to undervalue the potential for proverbs and other oral literature in the process of  
developing contextual theology. While it is a step in the right direction, it does not go far  
enough.  
Another interesting experiment with proverbs is Nkansah-Obrempong’s use of  
proverbs, cultural symbols, and metaphors to develop what he calls “visual theology.” He  
explains \(Nkansah-Obrempong 2002\:38\),  
The Akan religio-cultural heritage - especially the beliefs, values, views about  
reality, humans and the world, that are embedded and expressed in cultural  
symbols, proverbs and metaphors - provides important and critical materials for  
theology.... These materials are visual in the sense that they have the ability to  
create mental pictures through which we conceptualize reality.  
He uses these visual images from the Akan culture to interact with Scripture in order to  
develop a fresh understanding into the nature and character of Nyame \(God\), using Akan  
idioms and “affirm biblical faith from within \[the\] Akan religious experience” \(Nkansah-  
Obrempong 2002\:46\). This provides new possibilities for theologizing but it is still just  
emerging. Like the other models, there are few details that describe a process that can be  
used to facilitate contextualization.  
Hiebert et al \(1999\:21-29\) attempted to clarify the process by providing a four  
step process called critical contextualization that attempts to engage the Scripture with  
cultural beliefs and practices. Gilliland \(1989\:317\) summarizes the process of critical  
contextualization,  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 75  
Critical contextualization is carried out through an exegesis of the culture as one  
exercise and a fresh study of corresponding biblical themes as another. With the  
cultural and biblical information in hand, these two sources are critically reviewed  
with the objective of making a new response, which is culturally authentic and  
biblically appropriate.  
The first step in critical contextualization is phenomenological analysis, where the  
local culture is studied during which judgment is suspended. The second step is  
ontological critique, where the truth claims of different values and beliefs are tested by  
Scripture and objective reality. The third step is critical evaluation, where the church  
evaluates and makes decisions concerning the cultural practices and beliefs in light of the  
new information gained. The last step is missiological transformation, where specific  
missiological answers and responses are formulated. The process of critical  
contextualization provides practical steps to engage Scripture with cultural issues. This  
was developed to deal with specific cultural problems, however, and does not address  
how routine theologizing can be done. This process was not written with the use of  
proverbs to engage Scripture and culture in mind.  
By applying Heibert’s critical contextualization to oral literature \(with an  
emphasis on proverbs\), the Sukuma Research Committee \(Healey and Sybertz  
2000\[1996\]\) developed a seven step process to collect and interpret African oral literature  
in Tanzania. This resulted in an African narrative theology. These steps are\: 1\) Collect  
the original Sukuma proverb, 2\) Record the context including the history, meaning, and  
use of the proverb, 3\) Choose a theme of the proverb, 4\) Record similar proverbs to form  
proverb clusters, 5\) Explore biblical parallels and connections, 6\) Choose a Christian  
teaching based on the theme of the proverb, and 7\) Make suggestions for use in religious  
education. This is a helpful application of critical contextualization using oral literature.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 76  
While the end result provides a good example of how oral literature like proverbs can  
facilitate contextualization, there are few details provided as to the process that was used  
and can be reproduced in other locations. In particular, it is not clear how the  
hermeneutical community was formed, how it functioned, and what encouraged or  
discouraged the ongoing operation. In addition, few details were provided concerning  
step two\: “Record the context including the history, meaning, and use of the proverb.”  
This aspect has the potential to dig below the surface of the proverb and arrive at the  
cultural values, attitudes, and feelings; however, we are not told how this was discovered  
and how the discussions proceeded. In addition, some of the multiple meanings and uses  
of the proverbs are not discussed. Additional details are needed to flesh out the process.  
This research in the Builsa area seeks to fill in some of these missing details.  
By using a hermeneutical community, multiple voices and perspectives are heard.  
Both etic and emic perspectives are valuable in encouraging contextualization.  
Etic View  
Tarr \(1994\) demonstrates that the use of West African proverbs and parables can  
help increase one’s understanding of Scripture. While Westerners engage their culture  
with their own mother tongue Scripture, they will understand some Christian truth claims,  
but not all \(see Figure 7 on the next page\). Some Christian truth is obscured from  
Western eyes since contemporary Western culture is far removed from the cultures  
during the times of the biblical authors. When outsiders, such as Western missionaries,  
view African culture through the window of African proverbs, then a greater  
understanding of African culture and religion is obtained. This can then be engaged with  
Scripture providing increased depth, and understanding of Christian truth. Tarr calls this a  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 77  
**r\*s-K«m ""TV m +•&»■\$.**  
Outsider Mother  
Tongue Scripture  
African Mother  
Tongue Scripture  
African Primal Culture  
Outsider  
Figure 7\: Etic Perspective\: Critical Engagement of Scripture with Culture  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. “double image” since both Western and African cultures provide double insight into  
Scripture through the lenses of both cultures.  
Newbigin \(1986\:26\) affirms that Westerners should revisit their own theology more  
critically when he notes that Non-Western Christians can “help Western Christians make  
a Christian analysis of Western culture. Syncretism is a greater peril for Western than for  
African or Indian Christians, and less often recognizable for what it is!” While he may be  
overstating the case, his point is valid\: all cultures need to critically evaluate their  
own theology in order to break free from ethnocentrism. Missionaries are not exempt.  
The etic view offered through local symbols is essential for outside missionaries, as noted  
by Hiebert et al \(1999\:253\)\:  
The study of symbols, their meaning, and their uses in different contexts is  
essential for missionaries to understand the worldview underlying a culture. This  
must be learned and explicated to be useful in ministry.... Effective  
communication requires learning the sign systems people use in everyday life.  
Proverbs, functioning as symbols, can provide an etic perspective. While the etic  
\(outsider\) view is important, the emic \(insider\) view is equally important, if not more so.  
Emic View  
The emic view is also critical to facilitate the contextualization of Christianity in  
Builsa culture. The Builsa will need to critique their proverbs as the proverbs reflect back  
to the Builsa a view of their own culture. Pobee \(1979\:78\) notes, “While we affirm what  
is good in African culture, we also wish to warn against accepting everything African *en*  
*masse* and as useful in the process of adaptation. Some elements may have to be modified  
or even rejected.” The proverbs then become a mirror to reflect back an image of the  
culture that can be engaged with the Scripture, leading to a deeper understanding and  
appreciation of Christian truth \(see Figure 8 on the next page\).  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **79**  
African Mother  
Tongue Scripture  
African Primal Culture  
Figure 8\: Emic Perspective\: Critical Engagement of Scripture with Culture  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **8 0**  
Nkansah-Obrempong \(2002\:38\) noted how proverbs serve as mirrors of culture,  
“Akan proverbs are encapsulations of the accumulated wisdom and experiences of past  
generations of the Akan and constitute an authentic *mirror* \[emphasis mine\] of the mind  
and philosophy of the Akan People.” This emic view gained via proverbs provides a  
valuable perspective for authentic contextualization.  
In his monumental work on Twi proverbs in the nineteenth century, Christaller  
\(2000\[1879\]\:preface\) exhorted,  
May this collection \[of proverbs\] give a new stimulus to the diligent gathering of  
folklore and to the increasing cultivation of native literature. May those Africans  
who are enjoying the benefit of a Christian education make the best of this  
privilege, but let them not despise the sparks of truth entrusted to and preserved  
by their own people and let them not forget that *by entering into their way of*  
*thinking and by acknowledging what is good and expounding what is wrong they*  
*will gain the more access to the hearts and minds o f their less favored countrymen*  
\[italics mine\],  
Christaller observed how proverbs can provide a valuable emic perspective,  
which can then be critically evaluated by Scripture. This critical evaluation of proverbs,  
using the mother tongue Scripture as the interpreter of all culture, may be resisted by  
well-intentioned people. Some may consider that culture should be totally rejected and  
replaced with “Christian culture.” Nussbaum \(1996-98Introduction\) observes,  
Christian leaders have not yet gone very far toward integrating traditional  
proverbs with theology in the quest for a genuine African Christian identity. In  
some extreme cases such as Liberia and Swaziland, missionaries took such a  
negative view that to this day there is a virtual taboo on the use of any proverb in  
Christian preaching. Even in places like Ghana and Zambia where the encounter  
was more favorable, proverbs have not become one of the basic components of  
theological education. African theological education has been copied from the  
West.  
On the other extreme, others may resist the critical evaluation of proverbs by  
considering them sacred and unchangeable. In light of the previous discussion on  
symbols, it should be remembered that proverbs are symbols that are continuously  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 81  
constructed and contested in society. While these symbols link an inner concept of reality  
to an external reality, their representation is never exact; therefore, the symbols are  
continually negotiated, deconstructed, and reconstructed through dialogue in community.  
If this is not done, then the symbols die out.  
When discussing deep symbols \(also called “words of power”\), Farley \(1996\:1\)  
comments, “Words of power, that is, deep and enduring symbols that shape the values of  
a society and guide the life of faith, morality, and action, are subject to powerful forces of  
discreditation and even disenchantenment...we must find either ways to recover their  
power or live without them.” Farley further explains that deep symbols need to be  
continually reinterpreted in order to survive and remain relevant.  
To be more specific, Farley \(1996\:8\) describes how deep symbols can persist in  
culture, even though they may be harmful\:  
As instruments of corrupted power, deep symbols can mirror the society’s  
stratification of privilege. All actual societies privilege some members over  
others\: citizens over slaves, gentry over plain folk, males over females, majorities  
over minorities. And no society in history has ever been able to keep these  
arrangements from affecting its deep symbols. Thus, the deep symbols can be so  
framed as to advance the privileged members and suppress the voice of the  
unprivileged. They still may function as deep values and ideals, but in those ideals  
lurk racism, the disenfranchising of women, the maintenance of social policies  
that favor an existing social elite. For these reasons, the religious community  
must never pretend that its own deep symbols float above history in a world of  
ideal meanings. It must never maintain a passive, uncritical relation to its deep  
symbols.  
As a case in point, Schipper \(1985\:23-27\) shows that proverbs and other literary  
genre such as myths are used by the dominant power group to maintain the status quo.  
She observes that most proverbs are attributed to men and are used by men to maintain  
power over women. This is an important critique and shows how the proverbs must be  
critically evaluated instead of passively accepted.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. Leeds Hurwitz \(1993\:159-170\) reminds us that signs and symbols can undergo  
several changes throughout their lifetime. Some changes include\: layering \(new meanings  
become added on\), reinterpretation \(new meanings replace old meanings\), transformation  
\(substituting a new sign for an old one\), revival \(new life is given by a later generation\),  
assimilation \(a sign is acquired from another culture\), appropriation \(a sign is taken from  
another culture and is given a new use in another culture, giving it new meaning in the  
process\), and syncretism \(signs are integrated to form a “joint creation of a new set of  
meanings”\). This adds further support to the concept that symbols such as proverbs can  
i  
be critiqued and changed. This is important since contextualization of Christianity in  
Builsa culture can occur as the proverbs are critiqued and changed via Scripture.  
Section Summary  
A review of the recent history of African contextual theology reveals the  
shortfalls, trends, and recent progress. A clear way forward to promote contextualization  
is still lacking. While symbols were largely ignored in the past since they were not exact  
or scientific, the analysis of cultural symbols is a recommended starting place by several  
adherents of contextual theology. Proverbs, functioning as a symbol system, can be a  
useful starting point for contextualization. In addition to content, proverbs are an  
appropriate form to communicate theology in many African cultures.  
While the process of critical contextualization was not originally designed for the  
analysis of proverbs, it can be adapted to do so. As the cultural perspective gained by  
proverbs is critically engaged with Scripture, new avenues for contextualization may be  
realized. While some are starting to do this, these efforts are still very young and lacking  
in detail. This research attempts to fill in many of the details. By combining both the emic  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 83  
and etic perspectives in a hermeneutical community via the process of critical  
contextualization, a greater understanding and appreciation of both the Scripture and  
culture can result. Proverbs then become a catalyst to initiate and sustain  
contextualization. This addresses a present and pressing need in the Builsa church today.  
Before discussing how the research data addresses this need, the theoretical  
framework that will be used to interpret this data must be outlined. This is the topic of the  
next chapter.  
1 Bakhtin’s works were originally in Russian, which were later translated into English.  
The translators acknowledged that Bakhtin’s writing style was difficult to translate but  
they did their best with English concepts. That is why quotes from Bakhtin are cited  
differently by different translators.  
" This was a conversation the author had with Dr. Tienou during the Trinity Consultation  
on Missiology on June 22, 2004.  
III The Kasena people are neighbors of the Builsa. The Kasena and Builsa have  
intermarried from their early origins until the present time \(Howell 1997\:22-23\), and  
there are many similarities between the two cultures.

field research in March, 2004.
