# “A tune is made meaningful by lyrics; a point is elucidated by analogies and idioms”  
\(Ethiopian proverb\) \(Ayele\)  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **Chapter Three - Preparing to Run into the Open Field**  
*“Suom a dan kan nya bu dek chagiya, bu kan nyini kampuuring ninga*”  
# \(When a rabbit does not see itself well, it will not go out into an open field\)  
\(Awein and Awangara 1990\:4\)  
Introduction  
A rabbit knows that it is important to be well prepared prior to entering the open  
field; otherwise, it will end up with undesirable results \(on the dinner table\). The  
researcher must also have a well-prepared theoretical framework prior to collecting field  
research in order to guide the proper collection and interpretation of the data.  
Theoretical Framework  
The theoretical framework upon which the data are evaluated is founded on four  
major categories\: African primal religious systems \(particularly Hiebert’s framework for  
religious belief systems and Bakhtin’s genre theory\), the constitutive model of  
communication, religious symbols \(particularly Victor Turner’s bipolarization of  
symbols\), and Hiebert’s critical contextualization model. The combination of these  
approaches provides a useful framework to evaluate the potential of proverbs to facilitate  
contextualization in the Builsa church.  
84  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **85**  
Overview  
Hiebert’s three-tiered view of religious belief systems reveals that responses to  
issues arising from primal religion are an urgent need for the church in Africa. In order to  
understand primal religion better, Bakhtin’s genre theory explains that various genres in  
culture highlight certain areas of culture and hide other aspects. Proverbs are an important  
genre that provide valuable insight into understanding Builsa primal religion and culture.  
To understand how proverbs are useful in constructing theology, the constitutive model  
of communication suggests that symbols, such as proverbs, help construct reality in the  
communication process. Proverbs, functioning as religious symbols, can facilitate the  
construction of Builsa Christian theology and ethics. When looking through proverbs like  
windows, aspects of Builsa primal religion and culture are revealed. By adapting  
Hiebert’s critical contextualization model, this understanding gained by Builsa proverbs  
can be engaged with the Buli Scripture, resulting in contextualized expressions of  
Christianity. Builsa proverbs then, provide the Builsa church with a potent tool and  
perspective to facilitate the contextualization of Christianity in Builsa culture.  
African Primal Religion  
Hiebert’s \(1994\:196\) framework of religious belief systems helps me understand  
how large areas of Builsa culture are not addressed by Scripture. While Christianity  
addressed issues at the top level \(High Religion\) and Builsa culture provided answers at  
the lower level \(folk science\), the middle level \(primal religion\) was often excluded or  
ignored when Christianity was presented by missionaries. As a result, issues in the  
“excluded middle level” were addressed with traditional answers given by Builsa primal  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 86  
religion since Scripture did not appear to be relevant to these issues. This has led to “split  
level Christianity,” whereby Christianity addresses some areas of life \(High religion\) and  
Builsa primal religion addresses other areas of life \(middle level\).  
This is analogous to building a house. Missionaries assumed that Christianity  
could address the top level issues \(similar to the roof of the house\) and leave the lower  
level folk science issues \(similar to the foundation of the house\) alone. Unfortunately,  
this left a gap since there were no walls to support the house. The walls are the middle  
level issues, and Builsa primal religion filled that gap in order to keep the house standing.  
As a result, Christianity and Builsa primal religion operate together to address different  
issues or levels.  
While this “split-level Christianity” has functioned in Builsa culture, it does not  
allow the Scripture to address large areas of life. The contextualization of Christianity in  
Builsa culture is then limited. How can this be corrected? What tools or perspectives can  
be found that will point the way forward for contextualization? What are the roles of  
missionaries and national church leaders/lay people in this process? This study attempts  
to address these questions.  
While Hiebert’s framework of religious belief systems cogently identifies a need  
in the Builsa church, it does not provide a response to this need. Bakhtin’s genre theory  
can be combined with Heibert’s framework to move us forward. Bakhtin \(Morson and  
Emerson 1990\:275-276\) explains that each genre provides “a specific way of visualizing  
a given part of reality” since they each combine “specific blindnesses and insights.”  
A better understanding of Builsa primal religion can be obtained by examining the  
different genres within Builsa culture. For example, proverbs reveal critical aspects of  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. Builsa primal religion that other genres do not disclose \(see Figure 3 in chapter 2\). This  
can be likened to a set of plans for a building, where each plan sheet highlights and hides  
particular aspects for particular tasks to be performed \(see Figure 9 on the next page\). For  
example, the foundation plan describes how the concrete footers will be poured for the  
masons to do their job. The framing plan then instructs the carpenters where to build the  
walls. The roofing plan then instructs the roofers where to install roofing shingles and  
ventilation. While each plan sheet provides sufficient information for the particular job to  
be done, only by combining the entire set of plans will the building be adequately  
described and constructed. Likewise, the various genres in Builsa culture provide  
important and unique views that the others miss, whereas the combined set of genres  
more fully describe Builsa primal religion.  
While some genres have been more widely researched such as myth, story, song,  
ritual, ceremony, etc., the genre of proverbs has often been overlooked. Some thought  
that the genre of proverbs was not critical to understanding primal religion or culture,  
while others feared that a serious study of proverbs may cause them to backslide from  
Christianity .1 What would occur if the genre of proverbs were seriously studied by the  
church? What insights into Builsa culture and Scripture would this provide both  
missionaries and the national church? How could these insights help to promote  
contextualization? These are important questions that this study attempts to address, but  
another model is also needed to show how the genre of proverbs can be an important  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 88  
*Roofing Plan*  
*First Floor Plan*  
*Foundation Plan*  
Figure 9\: Various Plan Sheets Highlight and Hide Unique Perspectives  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **8 9**  
building block for constructing Builsa contextual theology. For this, we look to the  
constitutive model of communication.  
Communication Theory  
While Hiebert’s framework for religious belief systems identified the need for the  
Scripture to engage “middle level issues” in primal religion, and Bakhtin’s genre theory  
points us to look at various genres like proverbs to understand primal religion and  
culture, the constitutive model of communication takes us one step further toward the  
goal of constructing a Builsa Christian worldview. In this model, communication does  
more than pass on a message to a receiver; rather, something may be constructed in the  
process. In the constitutive model, Carey \(1975\:10\) proposed that “communication is a  
symbolic process whereby reality is produced, maintained, repaired, and transformed.”  
When looking at communication in this way, the door is opened to look at how the Builsa  
have constructed the shared inner reality via their symbols. This then prepares the way  
for Builsa to look for symbols that can be useful in reconstructing reality that is both  
Builsa and Christian.  
Our task, then, is to find symbols that are powerful building blocks within Builsa  
culture. Once these are identified, then they can be helpful tools for constructing  
contextual theology. Of course, the Buli language is the most prevalent and pervasive  
symbol system of the Builsa people. Within the Buli language, what symbols exist that  
may contain both the image of God and the problem of sin? What symbols contain deep  
cultural values and serve as windows into the Builsa worldview? The constitutive model  
of communication does not address these questions; however, when the study of religious  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **9 0**  
symbols is combined with the constitutive communication model, we see how Builsa  
proverbs can be important tools for contextualization, thereby releasing fresh discoveries.  
Religious Symbols  
When searching for potent symbols to use in constructing theology, religious  
symbols are promising since they provide windows into the soul of a culture. Victor  
Turner’s polarization of symbols identified how symbols bridge both the ideology \(norms  
and values\) and the sensory \(emotions and desires\) poles. Powerful symbols then will be  
those that attach both the deepest ideology of the Builsa culture with the deepest  
emotions.  
Builsa proverbs emerge as religious symbols that do both - they reveal the deep  
norms and values of the Builsa people, and they have an aesthetic function that reaches to  
the deepest senses and becomes “sweet talk.” By combining Turner’s bipolarization of  
symbols with the constitutive model of communication, proverbs are regarded as  
religious symbols that can be potent tools in helping to construct reality for the Builsa  
people. This theoretical framework describes how the proverbs will be understood and  
analyzed in this research - as religious symbols that serve as windows into the soul of the  
Builsa culture. As a result, the proverbs will be studied, as religious symbols, to see what  
they reveal of Builsa culture and primal religion to missionaries and nationals. I will also  
look through this framework to describe how the Builsa respond when proverbs are used  
to communicate Christianity.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **91**  
The contextualization of Christianity in Builsa culture does not result until the  
Buli Scripture interprets Builsa culture. To take this next step, we come to Hiebert’s  
model of critical contextualization.  
Critical Contextualization  
Hiebert developed the model of critical contextualization, largely to deal with  
cultural problems in the church. His four step process encourages the church to  
understand a cultural issue and then critique it with Scripture. This process includes a  
phenomenological analysis, ontological reflections, critical evaluation, and missiological  
transformations.  
This model can be adapted to deal with proverbs and the cultural issues that  
proverbs reveal. Once Builsa proverbs, functioning as religious symbols, become  
windows to reveal aspects of Builsa culture, this understanding can then be critiqued by  
Scripture \(in the Buli mother tongue\). As a result, the proverbs may become building  
blocks to construct contextual theology.  
This adaptation of Hiebert’s critical contextualization model helps form a  
theoretical framework to address the following questions\: What process can be used in  
Builsa culture to promote contextualization via proverbs? How does this hermeneutical  
community start and operate? Who are the important “construction workers,” and what  
does this process look like? These details have not been adequately described yet in the  
existing literature.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **9 2**  
Section Summary  
Hiebert’s analytical framework for religious belief systems helps us see that there  
are many “excluded middle” issues that exist in the Builsa culture that are not addressed  
by Scripture. Bakhtin’s genre theory helps to identify proverbs as a critical, but often  
overlooked, genre that offers unique insight into Builsa culture and primal religion. The  
constitutive model of communication helps us understand how the Builsa construct and  
reconstruct reality via symbols in the language, particularly proverbs. Turner’s  
bipolarization theory of symbols helps identify how proverbs can function as a powerful  
religious symbol set. They are bridges to connect both the deep Builsa ideology and  
sensory poles. They also serve as windows into the soul of the culture. Hiebert’s theory  
of critical contextualization can be adapted for the use of proverbs within a hermeneutical  
community in order to facilitate the construction of theology that is both Builsa and  
Christian.  
Now that I have described the theoretical framework upon which the data is  
analyzed and interpreted, the next section describes how this data was obtained and  
analyzed.  
Data Collection  
The data was collected over three different time periods from 1992-2004.  
Time Periods  
1992-2001\. My family and I lived in Ghana from January, 1992 to April, 2001  
with one furlough in between. During this time, I learned the Buli language and lived in  
the villages of Sandema and Fumbisi. I traveled extensively throughout the Builsa area  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **93**  
and collected research data on the use of proverbs in the Builsa area. I collected over 600  
proverbs along with English translations and suggested meanings. I also recorded my  
research notes in field books. Four lengthy discussions with church leaders were recorded  
on cassette tapes.  
2002\. From February, 2002 to May, 2002,1 met with African students from  
Asbury Theological Seminary. We discussed the use of proverbs and other oral literature  
to help promote the growth of an indigenous church and to facilitate contextual theology.  
The discussions were recorded on cassette tape, and a final session was captured on video  
cassette. This research was supplemental material to help inform the more intensive field  
research that I conducted in 2004.  
2004\. 1 conducted intensive field research in Ghana from January 26, 2004 to  
March 14, 2004. The research focus during this time period was twofold\: to follow up on  
church leaders with whom I met previously concerning the use of proverbs in their  
ministry, and to collect new data in the Builsa churches and communities. Having already  
learned the Buli language and formed friendships with Builsas previously, I was able to  
return to the Builsa area and do intensive research almost immediately. This data  
collection formed the main source of data for this research, while the previous periods  
were supplemental \(but necessary\) background data that guided the 2004 research period.  
I recorded extensive field notes in journals, as well as captured some events on cassette  
tapes, video recordings, and photographs. Various methods were used to collect the data.  
Methods of Data Collection  
Three methods were used to collect data such that each method strengthened the  
others.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. Participant-Observation. As a member of the BCA church and resident of the  
Builsa community, I observed Builsas using proverbs in various contexts, using their own  
mother tongue. I attended various community and church events and reflected on what I  
observed in field notes. These observations were recorded in field notes during the day,  
such that more complete observations and notes were fleshed out in detail at night in  
larger journals. These were unstaged events that occurred naturally as part of the life of  
the church and community. During the 2004 research, I participated in ten church events  
that included approximately 445 people \(including a funeral and water well dedication, in  
addition to regular church services in the Builsa area and one service in the capitol of  
Accra\). Prior to the 2004 research, a discussion with the *teng nyono* and church leaders  
was tape recorded and later transcribed in Buli and translated into English. In these group  
settings, people were free to come and go as they pleased. This allowed a wide range of  
people from different backgrounds to participate as they pleased.  
Group Discussions. I met with Builsa church leaders to discuss Builsa proverbs  
and then engage them with Scripture. We had 10 discussions that usually consisted of 8-  
12 people and lasted for 3-4 hours. I facilitated the meetings usually, but I largely listened  
and recorded observations. The church leaders’ discussion groups formed the  
hermeneutical community for the Builsa BCA church. During the 2004 research, I had  
group meetings with approximately 145 people \(including church leaders’ discussions,  
seminars, a class at a Bible Training Centre, and youth meetings\). Some of these  
discussions were tape recorded and later transcribed. The Proverbs Seminar proceedings  
were tape recorded, transcribed in English, and then portions were printed. In addition,  
the discussions with African students at Asbury Theological Seminary were tape  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **95**  
recorded and 1 session was video taped. Each student then submitted a paper of their  
reflections at the end. The group settings encouraged open discussions and benefited by  
the synergy of the group dynamics to extend the discussions much broader than an  
individual interview.  
Intensive Interviews. I did follow up in-depth interviews with several individuals  
to uncover more specific answers to questions raised in the other contexts. Usually, the  
interviews lasted 2 hours and were conducted in the Buli language. I looked for good  
informants who were\: thoroughly enculturated, currently involved in the use of proverbs,  
had adequate time to meet with me, and were nonanalytical \(Spradley 1979\:46\). I  
approached the interviews “as a series of friendly conversations into which the researcher  
slowly introduces new elements to assist informants to respond as informants" Spradley  
\(1979\:58\). This was helpful since I was already friends with most of the informants;  
therefore, the interviews were more friendly visits than drill sessions. By analyzing the  
responses to questions in initial interviews, new questions and relationships were  
identified for further exploration in subsequent interviews.  
During the 2004 research, I conducted 48 one-on-one private interviews. This  
allowed more private and intimate discussions to occur. I also interviewed 20 cross-  
cultural workers \(both expatriate and Ghanaians\) who were using proverbs in ministry.  
Three of these interviews were conducted by email, while the others were done face to  
face. The individual interviews helped verify the results in the group meetings and the  
participant-observations in order to increase the reliability of the data.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **9 6**  
Measures to Promote Reliability  
In order to strengthen the reliability of the data, multiple data collection methods  
\(as described above\) and multiple data sources were used. The data sources varied in  
their geopraphical locations, formal education levels, and ministry experiences.  
Locations. During the 2004 research, the data were obtained from various  
locations. This included 13 different villages \(8 Builsa, 2 Mamprusi, 1 Konni, and 2  
Kasena/Nankani people groups\). A seminar was also held in the city of Tamale \(a  
Dagomba city in the Northern Region\). In addition, supplementary data was obtained in  
Wilmore, KY with several African students at Asbury Theological Seminary.  
Formal Education Levels. The African participants had various levels of  
education to include\: \(1\) strictly oral learners, \(2\) recently literate in their Mother tongue,  
and \(3\) literate in both their Mother tongue and English. Some had never been to formal  
school while others had completed university training.  
Ministry Experiences. The ministry experiences were also varied to include\: \(1\)  
village lay leaders, \(2\) district chairpersons, and \(3\) national missionaries. The cross-  
cultural workers studied were missionaries in Ghana from the countries of Ghana, United  
States, Australia, England, and South Korea.  
By varying the contexts, education levels, experience, and countries of origin, the  
reliability of the responses to the guiding questions was increased. My long-term  
involvement in the Builsa area also helped to increase the reliability. As the data was  
collected, the analysis was an ongoing process.  
Analysis of Data  
The data were continually analyzed throughout the research process, as follows.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **9 7**  
Overview. During the initial data collection, I was looking for common responses  
from participants to the guiding questions. After a short time in the village, I observed the  
response, or lack thereof, of Builsa believers to cases that arose involving the Scripture  
and Builsa culture. I then used these cases in subsequent interviews to see how they  
would respond to the same cases. Common reactions were noted and used in subsequent  
interviews. I then introduced the use of proverbs to see if this would benefit the  
engagement of the Scripture with culture.  
Process. The process gradually progressed in the following manner.  
1\. Initially, the discussions were open-ended and I looked for responses related to  
the established research questions.  
a. What reflections are Builsa church leaders expressing of their own  
culture and Scriptures as they focus on their proverbs?  
b. What reflections are missionaries expressing of local culture and  
Scripture as they focus on proverbs?  
c. What encourages the formation and ongoing operation of a  
hermeneutical community and their engagement of Scripture with  
proverbs?  
d. How do Builsa respond as proverbs are used to communicate  
Christianity?  
While there was no interview schedule, I carefully observed and noted  
common responses that addressed these guiding questions. Much of the initial  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **9 8**  
2\. 3. 4. research was to observe the changes that occurred as the Builsa church began  
using proverbs more readily in ministry.  
Within a short period of time, several case studies arose dealing with  
“excluded middle” issues. As church leaders discussed these cases, I observed  
two common responses - either total rejection of the cultural practice in question  
or total acceptance of it. There was little evidence of critical evaluation and  
engagement with Scripture. I recorded the cases and the responses by various  
church leaders.  
In follow-up individual interviews, I revisited the cases. I started with one of the  
“less complicated” cases and asked for their responses. I.took notes during the  
ensuing conversation. I then presented to them a proverb and asked them to  
critique it. This was generally a proverb that was more widely understood; hence,  
it was easier to analyze. I then asked them to critique the root meanings of this  
proverb with the Buli Scripture. Again, I took notes on the ensuing conversation.  
The proverb discussed was not related to the case; rather, I was looking for a  
reproducible process. We then discussed the case again, and I asked them to use  
the same process that was just used to critically evaluate the proverb. I noted the  
difference in responses to the case between the first discussion \(before the proverb  
was addressed\) and the second discussion \(after the proverb was addressed\).  
As the interview progressed, I then repeated the same procedure in step 3, but I  
used a more detailed and involved case, as well as a more complicated and less  
familiar proverb. I observed how they used the process of analyzing proverbs to  
help them deal with the cases.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **9 9**  
5\. Large group discussions were also conducted, and I followed the procedure  
described in steps 3 and 4 above. The large group discussions opened the way for  
a deeper discussion of broader issues. As new issues and cases were brought to  
light, this furthered the discussion into areas that were previously undisclosed to  
me, even though I lived among the Builsa for years prior to this. Many times, I  
felt as though I was not sure where the conversation would head or end. This  
exploration of new territory, though, proved to be most helpful in dealing with  
deeper cultural issues that were usually not addressed in church settings.  
Missionaries who used proverbs in their ministry in Africa were also observed  
and interviewed, in similar manner as the Builsa above. I met with them in Ghana, as  
well as interviewed some via email and personal visits.  
Computer Program. The Mother Tongue Oral Literature, Version 1.0 database  
program" was also used to analyze the actual proverbs discussed in order to fully explore  
the meaning of the proverbs. Some of the proverbs are concise summaries of stories;  
therefore, the stories were also entered into the database to help interpret the proverbs.  
Composite People. When I finished collecting the field data, I reviewed all of the  
field notes for common themes, responses, reactions, or lack of responses. This helped  
me to form a “composite person” who typified the observations. From this, I developed  
characters to form a story that accurately portrayed the Builsa story. All of the events that  
happen to the composite person actually occurred - they were merely spread out over  
several people and not just the one person. The advantage of forming this “composite  
person” over just using case studies from different people is that the reader can follow the  
development of one character throughout the narrative.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. For example, Immanuel represents a Builsa convert to Christianity who is young  
and sincere in his faith. He struggles with “excluded middle” issues and how he can be  
fully Christian as well as fully Builsa. We come to understand and feel Immanuel’s  
struggles as we see his story unfold before us. While all of the events described did not  
actually happen to one person named Immanuel, all of the events that are narrated  
actually occurred to several young sincere converts who are striving to be fully Christian  
as well as fully Builsa. The intent is to follow the advice of Strauss and Corbin \(1998\:56\),  
“If we do it correctly, then we are not speaking for our participants but rather are  
enabling them to speak in voices that are clearly understood and representative.” The  
various settings provided a broad base upon which to draw out common responses from  
representative voices.  
Other composite people in the narrative included\: Pastor David \(a graduate from a  
Bible College that was taught in English in a city removed from the Builsa area\), Pastor  
Kofi \(a graduate from a vernacular Bible Training Centre that was taught in his mother  
tongue\), and Missionary Joe \(A Western missionary who has come to live and minister in  
Ghana\). Joe is a composite character based on missionaries that I interviewed and  
observed \(including myself\).The composite people are based on participant-observations,  
interviews, and group discussions with the following number of people who typified  
these characters\: 41 people for Immanuel, 26 people for Pastor David, 91 people for  
Immanuel, and 20 people for Missionary Joe.  
Since Builsa stories and proverbs are often intimately connected, it seems  
appropriate that the presentation of the proverbs research should be in a narrative format.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 101  
Rationale for Narrative Format  
I chose to present the research results in narrative format \(with periodic pauses for  
reflection and analysis\) for the following reasons\:  
1\. The narrative genre is closely related to the genre of proverbs; thus, narrative is  
an appropriate form for this research. In this way, I want to practice what I am preaching.  
I am trying to model that contextual theology in oral cultures like the Builsa may be  
better presented in formats other than propositional, philosophical statements. In addition,  
while I have limited this research to proverbs, it is hoped that other oral literature genres  
will be researched in a similar manner, and I would like to encourage others to consider  
alternate genres for presentation.  
2\. There is precedence for the narrative genre being used in scholarly  
anthropological writing. Dimen \(1977\), as well as Spradley and McDonough \(1973\)  
encouraged the analysis of narrative in order to stimulate anthropological reflection.  
Victor Turner \(1995 \[1969\]\) also presented pages of narrative with periodic commentary  
for reflection and analysis. This added to the authenticity of the writing. Geertz’s \(1973\)  
“thick description” in writings such as “Deep Play\: Notes on the Balinese Cockfight”  
contain narrative that is very engaging as well as insightful. He recreates the context and  
action in which it took place so that the readers participate in the original context, as  
much as possible. This creates an authentic presentation for the reader, while still  
combined with analysis and reflection. These authors have shown that a narrative  
presentation can be combined with reflection and analysis in a way that is scholarly as  
well as engaging.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 102  
3 .1 believe that this format will be more attractive to African readers. I have  
already received interest from Africans \(and missionaries ministering in Africa\) in favor  
of this format. Narrative theology is gaining an audience in Africa, as evidenced by  
attempts such as “Toward an African Narrative Theology” \(Healey and Sybertz  
2000\[1996\]\), and the popularity of the chronological Bible Storying method. Others are  
advocating a narrative rather than propositional presentation since this involves people in  
the learning process \(Dent 2004; Morton 2004\). Healey and Nussbaum \(2 leading  
parameoligists/authors with extensive ministry experience in Africa\) were adamant that I  
should use the narrative format since they feel that this is a “way forward” in African  
contextual theology111.  
4\. One of the major gaps in contextualization is that there are few details  
concerning how the process can work including the starting, operating, and functioning of  
the hermeneutical community. While much of the existing theory is sound, there are few  
practical examples with details to point the way forward. By presenting the research  
results in a narrative format, many of these details will become clearer as the characters  
portray them in a grass-roots discussion. It is hoped that readers will identify with one or  
more of the characters in the narrative in order to help them picture themselves doing  
contextual theology.  
Narrative Format  
The narrative is developed around four major parts, each part addressing the  
research questions, as follows\:  
Chapter 4 = An Insider’s Perspective. The perspectives of Africans are described as they  
use proverbs and reflect on their usage. This part summarizes the answers to research  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 103  
question one\: What reflections do Builsa express of their culture and Scripture as they  
focus on their proverbs?  
Chapter 5 = An Outsider’s Perspective. The perspectives of cross-cultural workers are  
described as they use proverbs in ministry in Africa, and then they reflect on this usage.  
This part summarizes the answers to research question two\: What reflections do  
missionaries express o f Builsa culture and Scripture as they focus on proverbs?  
Chapter 6 = Engagement of Builsa Culture with Scripture. The engagement of Scripture  
with Builsa culture via proverbs is described. This results in contextualized expressions  
of the Christianity. This part summarizes the answers to research question four\: How do  
Builsa respond as proverbs are used to communicate Christianity?  
Chapter 7 = Process for Replication. A process is described that facilitates the use of  
proverbs in engaging the Scripture with culture in critical contextualization within a  
hermeneutical community. This part summarizes the answers to research question three\:  
What encourages the formation and on-going operation of a hermeneutical community  
and their engagement o f Scripture with proverbs?  
Now that the methods for data collection and interpretation are described, as well  
as the theoretical foundation given for the research, I am finally prepared to present the  
data in a narrative format. The rabbit is now prepared to run in the open field.  
1 In a conversation with an African seminary student at a prominent school in the U.S.,  
the student informed me that he liked proverbs but he was afraid to express too much of  
an interest in them in class. Upon further questioning, he disclosed his fear that others  
would think he was backsliding into African primal religion. Another PhD student who  
attended a school in Germany wrote his dissertation and included over 100 Indian  
proverbs. His advisor requested that they all be removed since they were not suitable for  
an academic paper.

II Short term missionary, Gerry Lynn, and I wrote this computer database program to  
enter the proverbs with their meanings, appropriate contexts, themes, and appropriate  
Bible references. The data can be entered in English as well as the mother tongue  
language. This program is a database application from the Microsoft Access program.  
This program allows the proverbs to be grouped by themes, words, and biblical passages.  
It also allows the entry of songs and stories in order to combine the analyses with these  
other aspects of oral literature.  
III In several personal discussions with these men, both strongly urged me to use the  
narrative format for the dissertation.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **Chapter 4 - An Insider’s Perspective**  
*“Ba kan de korum a mini biiga*” **\(They do not eat \[consume/destroy\] culture and leave**  
# nothing behind for the children\).  
A Tragedy Occurs  
*Tragedy struck Immanuel’s house. His 18 year old son was sick fo r several weeks*  
*but now his death hit his father hard. This was his only son... and now he was gone. He*  
*called the church to come and help him do the burial and funeral. They gathered at*  
*Immanuel’s house throughout the night and brought comfort to Immanuel and his family*  
*as they buried his son. Pastor Kofi conducted the funeral ceremony and also stayed with*  
*Immanuel until the next day.*  
*Immanuel had built his own house a few years ago. His family had all become*  
*Christians and they wanted to have a Christian household. As the head o f the house,*  
*Immanuel could make the decisions concerning the funeral; otherwise, the traditional*  
*Builsa elders would have conducted the burial andfuneral. Immanuel knew that this*  
*would include traditional animal sacrifices to the ancestors. Since he was a Christian*  
*now, Immanuel knew that he did not need those sacrifices to help him.* Yezu Krista *was*  
*the sacrifice that* Naawen *had providedfor the Builsa.*  
*“Our fathers have said,* Ti mabiik dan bo cham zuk, fi kan de teng chain ya” *\(If*  
*your relative is in the top o f the sheanut tree, then you do not need to eat the sheanuts*  
*that have fallen to the ground\), Immanuel explained to people.* “Yezu *is the relative who*  
*has risen from the grave and now sits at the right hand* o/'Naawen. *Since he is at the top*  
*o f the tree, I can pray to him and he will provide what I need When others offer*  
105  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 106  
*sacrifices to the ancestors, they are just gathering the sheanuts that have fallen to the*  
*ground- everyone knows that these sheanuts are not nearly as good as the ones at the*  
*top o f the tree.* ” *This made good sense and it helped Immanuel to put his confidence in*  
Yezu, *the one who sits at the top o f the sheanut tree.*  
*After the funeral, Immanuel was confronted by the Builsa village elders, “We*  
*need to collect some dirt from the grave and take it home. ”*  
*Immanuel was not surprised. He knew the importance o f funeral rights in* Buluk -  
*this was a serious matter fo r the whole community.*  
*“What will you do with the dirt? ” Immanuel asked*  
*“We need to take it to the* bano, " *the elders explained, “so that he can reveal the*  
*reason that your son has died. ”*  
*Immanuel was unsure what to do. Should he refuse to give them the dirt or should*  
*he comply with their request? This matter was never previously discussed in the church.*  
*He did not know what to do.*  
*“I will need to talk to my church leaders before I can allow you to collect the dirt.*  
*The elders have said,* “Ba kan kali dai yeng a nak kpeesa a sue yui ya ” *\(They cannot sit*  
*in \[just\] one day and harvest Fra-fra potatoes to fill up a bag\). ”*  
*This put off the decision fo r a few more days until Immanuel could consult with*  
*Pastor David. The elders accepted this and agreed to sit with him another day.*  
*Pastor David arrived shortly thereafter. As Immanuel explained the whole*  
*situation to him, the pastor patiently listened.*  
*“You need to refuse the elders and not allow them to collect the dirt. Don ’tyou*  
*see, ” Pastor David warned, “that they will go to the* bano *and he will say that the death*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 107  
*has come about since the boy followed the path* o/'Yezu Krista? *This will only bring*  
*about more trouble fo r the church. You had better refuse them now! ”*  
*Immanuel nodded his head in approval. “That is what I have been thinking of*  
*doing all along, ” he replied. “Thanks fo r your help. ”*  
*Pastor David was not sure himself if his advice would help or not. These types of*  
*questions relating the Scripture to the Builsa culture were never discussed in the training*  
*that he received at Bible school. He knew that following the path* o/’Yezu Krista *was a*  
*better way than trying to reach* Naawen *through the ancestors. As a result, he turned his*  
*back on anything related to the ancestors since he considered it a trap to cause him to*  
*backslide into Builsa primal religion. Even still, he wondered if there was anything good*  
*from the ancestors that may be helpful. Do Builsa have to reject everything from the*  
*ancestors in order to be fully Christian? Can Builsa retain certain aspects o f culture*  
*without compromising Scripture?*  
*While contemplating this struggle between the Scripture and Builsa culture,*  
*Pastor Kofi arrived to greet the funeral and offer his condolences. After some time,*  
*Immanuel discussed the matter o f the ‘dirt from the grave ’ with him, and Immanuel asked*  
*fo r his advice.*  
*Pastor Kofi was not sure how to respond either. He tried to recall what he was*  
*taught in a vernacular Bible training center but this issue was never discussed. After*  
*some thought, Pastor Kofi replied, “I think that you should allow the elders to come and*  
*collect the dirt. You are not the one who will go to the* bano, *and you will not be the one*  
*to bring the sacrifices. You can just allow them to follow the path o f the ancestors and*  
*you continue on the path o f* Yezu. *I f you totally refuse their request, then this will end up*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 108  
*in a fight. It is better to accept their request fo r now. This will then be over soon and we*  
*can just continue on following* Yezu *as usual.* ”  
*Immanuel was confused. He respected Pastor Kofi and his advice made sense to*  
*him; however, this was the exact opposite o f what Pastor David advised. “The pastors*  
*have been to Bible training and they should know how to advise us, ” Immanuel thought,*  
*“How can one advise me to reject the elders ’ request and the other advise me to accept*  
*the elders ’ request? Perhaps Christianity does not deal with these questions so I should*  
*just go along with the elders ’ request? On the other hand, perhaps I should refuse since*  
*this may weaken my faith in* Yezu *and cause others to fa ll away. ”*  
*Immanuel was still confused. This issue was just too hard to figure out. This type*  
*o f issue was never preached about or taught in church so he had no preparation to know*  
*how to deal with it. I f the pastors could not agree on a Christian response, then how*  
*could he?*  
This encounter reveals the types of struggles that Builsa Christians experience.  
While they are sincere in their faith, they are uncertain how Scripture would help them to  
respond to the “middle level issues” that they face. Immanuel represents a typical Builsa  
Christian. While he is not an actual person, his responses and actions were drawn from  
several actual Builsa; therefore, he is a composite person representing many actual cases.  
The narrative also portrays common responses that Builsa Christians often give  
concerning issues relating to Builsa culture. Often times, the quick answer is just to reject  
the culture and have nothing to do with the ancestors and their ways. After all, this may  
cause people to fall back into African primal religion. Some even say that the entire  
Builsa culture is full of demonic activity and it must all be cleansed. If one tries to remain  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **10 9**  
in Builsa culture then even the elders have a proverb that says, *“Ft jii fi nyuvuri a dengsi*  
**\(You carry your life on your head without using your hands to support it\).”** It is just  
too dangerous. Pastor David typifies this response. While Pastor David is not an actual  
person, he is a composite person representing typical responses of many actual cases.  
The other extreme is to accept the culture and just go along with it. Instead of  
creating a fight with traditional elders and authority figures, just let them follow their  
own way while Christians follow their own way. The issue will blow over after awhile  
and then the Christians can get back to “life as normal.” One small issue will not destroy  
the whole church. Even the elders have advised, *“Dai yeng chala kan nakpuuk a basi*  
**\(One day’s running will not cause an abortion\).”** Since the church does not discuss  
many of these issues, it seems that these are not important aspects of Christianity. It is  
easier just to follow the culture in these areas that are not clear. Pastor Kofi represents  
this position. Once again, while he is not an actual person, he represents many actual  
cases where Builsa responded this way.  
Immanuel’s tragedy is an example of what Hiebert has called “The excluded  
middle,” since it is “one of the greatest cultural gaps between Western people and many  
traditional religionists” \(Hiebert 1994\:196\). The excluded middle deals with issues  
arising from primal religion that include entities at work in this world that are beyond  
rational explanation since they are unseen or supernatural, such as ancestors, ghosts,  
spirits, witches, local gods or goddesses, etc. The key issues that this level addresses are  
the causes of death and identity in life, well-being and misfortune, guidance for an  
uncertain future, and justice amidst evil \(Hiebert et al. 1999\:74\). All of these issues are  
involved in this case to some degree. Unfortunately, typical Western missionaries and  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 110  
Bible training did not provide spiritual answers to these issues; hence they were excluded  
from discussion. Since the Scripture did not appear to be relevant to these issues,  
Africans referred to the local responses to these issues, resulting in “Split-level  
Christianity” \(Bulatao 1992\). The two belief systems of high religion \(Christianity\) and  
primal religion \(Builsa traditional religion\) were maintained side by side with each one  
addressing different issues. This has severely limited the development of the church in  
Africa. Hiebert \(1993\:258\) summarizes, “It is clear today that folk religious beliefs and  
practices remain an unfinished agenda in the lives of young churches around the  
world.. .Nowhere is this more true than in Africa.”  
Africans are also crying out for a further understanding of African primal religion.  
African theologian, Bediako \(1989\:59\), states, “There is probably no issue more crucial  
than the need to understand this heightened interest in the African pre-Christian religious  
tradition if Africa’s theologians are to be interpreted correctly and their achievement duly  
recognized.” Turaki \(2000\:281\) also affirms the need for understanding primal religion,  
The most crucial theological issue at hand is that most African Christians lack  
sufficient knowledge of the Bible and its teaching on African traditional religions  
and cultures... .Even though the influence of the African traditional religions and  
cultures is profound in modem Africa, most African Christians have little  
knowledge of how this influence is exerted.  
The tragedy is that Immanuel’s dilemma is not an isolated case; rather, it is  
representative of the many primal religion issues that Builsa face. Since the Builsa are not  
accustomed to engaging Buli Scripture with the Builsa culture, it is just too difficult a hill  
to climb. As a result, they often fall back on the two responses of either total acceptance  
\(as in Pastor Kofi\) or total rejection \(as in Pastor David\). There is very little engagement  
of the Scripture with Builsa culture since the process is just too difficult.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. I l l  
The difficulty described above is similar to learning to ride a bicycle. In the  
beginning, they are taught to ride on smooth, flat ground until they get used to the  
motion. After practice, they are ready to take on the challenge of climbing a hill. If they  
were taught to ride the bike by going up the hill initially, then the challenge would be too  
great and they would likely give up - it is just too difficult.  
To ask Immanuel to engage Buli Scripture with the issue of the “dirt from his  
son’s grave” is similar to asking him to learn to ride a bike by going up a hill. He has not  
been taught the process of developing contextual theology, and he has not had practice  
applying it on easier issues. When this difficult issue is then given to him, he is not  
prepared. Pastors Kofi and David have been taught the Scripture, but they have also not  
been taught how to ride the bike of contextual theology. As a result, this is just too  
difficult a hill to climb. The easy and familiar responses are either total rejection or total  
acceptance.  
Clearly, the Builsa need to learn how to ride the bike of contextual theology. If  
they can get used to riding this bike on easy ground dealing with issues that they can  
tackle more readily, then they can later be ready to deal with the difficult cultural issues  
that emerge. Where is this easy ground? For the Builsa, their own proverbs are familiar,  
easy ground. They love to use proverbs in conversation and reason with them. The Builsa  
can reflect on their own proverbs and dig out the roots to find the cultural values. When  
using the Buli Scripture as the interpreter to find the roots that are proper for a Christian  
and those that were not, they are engaging Scripture with Builsa culture on easy ground.  
Once they are used to riding the bike, then they can climb the uphill challenge of  
reflecting on the ‘dirt from the grave’ issue.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 112  
Let us return to the story to see how this unfolded.  
Learning to Ride the Bicycle  
*Immanuel was still confused. He didn* 7 *know whether he should accept or reject*  
*the elders ’ request. He wanted to do the right thing to please* Naawen *and still be on*  
*good terms with his neighbors.*  
*When pastor Kofi invited him to a meeting at the church to discuss some*  
*proverbs, he was happy to accept. “I always enjoy hearing some new proverbs, ”*  
*Immanuel thought. Proverbs were like entertainment to him and this meeting may help to*  
*get his mind off o f his immediate problems, he reasoned.*  
*The next day, Immanuel joined the meeting as they were discussing the proverb,*  
“Buuk ale ku bisa kan ga Yagaba kinla” *\(A goat and its children do not go to \[the*  
*village of\] Yagaba without a reason\). Immanuel laughed when he heard it and he*  
*remembered that last time that he heard this proverb.*  
*“What does this proverb mean? ” the discussion leader asked.*  
*“I f someone has to take a goat and its children to the market in Yagaba, that*  
*means there is a serious matter at home that has forced them to do this, ” Pastor David*  
*responded.*  
*“True. They say,* ‘Jakpak kan chali kapiokapo kinla’ *\(The old man does not run*  
*in the valley without a reason\) ”, responded another church leader as he smiled widely.*  
*“Yes. When you see a pregnant goat fo r sale in the market, then you know there is*  
*a pregnant problem at home! ” Everyone laughed and nodded their heads in agreement.*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 113  
*Immanuel recalled something else. He then added, “There is more to the proverb*  
*than that. I remember that someone used this proverb to insult a person who had moved*  
*into our village and was having some problems. ’’*  
*“Didyou know the person who moved into the village? ’’ asked Pastor Kofi.*  
*“No, but I could tell that he was having problems with his neighbors and people*  
*were talking about him. ”*  
*“They wanted to know why he left his former home and came to live in the village,*  
*didn’t they? ”*  
*Immanuel knew that some o f them understood the meaning so he added, “You are*  
*correct. They said that no one leaves their home and comes to live in another area unless*  
*there was some problem in their home area. He was probably troublesome and they*  
*drove him away; that is why he had to come live here. Some think that he was a thief and*  
*was about to be discovered so he had to hide here. ”*  
*“Maybe his wife was accused o f being a* sakpak *and they had to run to your*  
*village fo r safety. ”*  
*Some awkward smiles flashed across the room since pastors were often*  
*uncomfortable talking about witchcraft. Witchcraft accusations periodically surfaced and*  
*sometimes they were church members who were accused. Each case was different and no*  
*uniform way fo r the church to deal with these accusations had yet been found.*  
*“Do you think that she is a* sakpak? ” *one person asked.*  
*“I can’t answer that, but I know that they have not lived in our village long and*  
*they are starting to cause problems. People are wondering about them and that is why*  
*this proverb was spoken about them, ” Immanuel replied.*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 114  
*Several issues were surfacing but the discussion leader wanted to bring the*  
*discussion on track by interjecting, “It helps to understand the various pieces o f this*  
*proverb. Now we have to use* Naawen Wani *to help us know what pieces o f the proverb*  
*are* magsi *and what parts o f the proverbs are not* magsi. *I f we find the pieces that are*  
magsi, *then we can accept these pieces as true and affirm them in our church. When we*  
*fin d the pieces that are not* magsi, *then we should reject these pieces and explain to our*  
*churches how* Naawen Wani *corrects this. In this way, we will not just reject the whole*  
*proverb as being all evil and we will not just accept the whole proverb as being all good.*  
*Rather, we will pick out the pieces that are not* magsi *and refuse them. Then, we will*  
*accept the pieces that are* magsi *and affirm them in church. ”*  
*Pastor Kofi smiled, “You mean that we will pick the bones out o f the fish and set*  
*them aside. Then, we can continue to eat the rest o f the meat. ”*  
*The discussion leader was pleased with this insight, “Very good. I f you reject the*  
*whole fish because o f the bones, then you miss out on the meat and go hungry. I f you eat*  
*all o f the meat and ignore the bones, then you also will have problems with the bones in*  
*your mouth. It is better to pick out the bones and then enjoy the meal. ”*  
*“How do we know which are the bones are which are the meat? ” Immanuel*  
*asked. He liked what he heard, but he was not sure how to do this.*  
*“Think about* Naawen Wani *and try to remember what portions talk about these*  
*things. Use your Buli New Testament or the Gospel Recordings picture books to help*  
*you, ” answered the discussion leader. He handed out some copies o f the Gospel*  
*Recordings picture books that summarize the Bible in eight books using pictures that are*  
*easy to remember. Each o f the books has a cassette tape that narrates the story. These*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 115  
*picture books and cassettes have been used fo r several years so many o f the church*  
*members and all o f the leaders are familiar with them'. They are helpful in teaching as*  
*well as personal devotion and evangelism \(see photo 4 in the Appendix\).*  
*“It is true that there is usually a meaning behind things that occur, ” Pastor Kofi*  
*started. “I f there is a serious problem, then it will require serious measures to correct it.*  
*Isn ’t that why* Yezu *had to come and die fo r us? "*  
*“Yes, all o f us have sinned and the payment fo r sin is death. That is why* Yezu *had*  
*to come and be a sacrifice fo r us, ” added Pastor David.*  
*“The elders say,* \*Ba kan gering wusuum kpalinsa ale kingkanga’ *\(They cannot*  
*separate the fighting o f horses with millet stalks\). A serious matter will require serious*  
*means to solve it. ”*  
*“It is also true that sin will be discovered. Remember Achan’s sin was found out* -  
*see this picture... so were Ananias and Sapphira in Acts 5. ”*  
*“Yes,* \*Ku dan puak, ku le nyung’ *\(When it is rotten, it will smell\). Eventually the*  
*sin ofpeople will be rotten and others will smell it. It can not stay hidden forever.* ”  
*The discussion leader affirmed, “Those are all pieces o f the proverb that* Naawen  
Wani *affirms so we can accept those pieces. Are there any other pieces that* Naawen  
Wani *would want us to reject? ”*  
*The group was quiet fo r a moment as they reflected on the meaning o f the proverb*  
*that was discussed, as well as the relevant Scripture portions that they remembered.*  
*Pastor Kofi pondered, “While it is true that there is a reason fo r things that*  
*happen, is it true that there is always a bad reason behind it? I f someone came to my*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 116  
*village, must I assume that he had to come here because he was a troublesome person*  
*and he was forced to come? Maybe he came fo r a good reason.* ”  
*“Look at this picture o f Job. ” Pastor David pointed to the Gospel Recordings*  
*booklet. “He had a lot o f bad things happen to him and his friends made the mistake o f*  
*thinking that there was a bad reason behind it all. In the end, they were proven wrong - it*  
*was* Naawen *who was behind it all along. ”*  
*“I see what you are saying, ” responded Immanuel. “We should not be too quick*  
*to throw the first stone and judge someone since we may not understand the issues*  
*involved. Only* Naawen *can understand the whole story. ”*  
*The discussion leader added, “It seems that this is a piece o f the proverb’s*  
*meaning that we can reject. There is not always a bad reason behind every difficult*  
*problem. We should not be too quick to judge.* “Kilima, kilima kan jogi nyain’ *\(Hurry,*  
*hurry doesn’t miss ‘nyain ’ \[the sound o f a breaking pot\]\).* ”  
*Everyone laughed since they knew that quick decisions and judgments often lead*  
*to problems \(a clay pot will easily break if one is in a hurry and hastily knocks the pot\).*  
*“We make a mistake if we accept the whole proverb since there are some pieces*  
*that are not* magsi, ” *he continued. “We also make a mistake if we reject the whole*  
*proverb since there are some pieces that are* magsi. *By taking the time to understand the*  
*meaning o f the proverb, we can then fin d the pieces that are* magsi *by using* Naawen  
Wani *as the teacher. ’’*  
*All agreed that this was helpful. This process was not easy but it was something*  
*that they all enjoyed and it was something that could be done. It helped the Builsa church*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 117  
*leaders to engage their culture with Scripture. The proverb then was a small fishhook*  
*that helped to catch a bigger fish - contextualization.*  
*Immanuel was now pondering his own situation back home. When he returned*  
*home, he would have to respond to the elders. What would he say? He wondered if this*  
*same process that they just did with a proverb could be used to help guide his decision*  
*concerning the dirt from the grave. Immanuel was not sure if it was safe to discuss this*  
*topic but he was beginning to feel that perhaps this process would be helpful.*  
*That was the real tragedy that Immanuel felt most keenly. He did not know how to*  
*engage deep heart-felt cultural issues with the Scripture. He was not even sure if others*  
*knew how to do this any better, or if it was acceptable to discuss these issues in the*  
*church. He reasoned, though, that since they took the proverb and its cultural meaning*  
*seriously, they will likely take his situation seriously. In addition, he enjoyed gaining*  
*insight into Builsa culture and Scripture by focusing on the proverb; perhaps, this would*  
*happen as they focused on his dilemma. There was hope fo r him to deal with his tragedy.*  
Before proceeding with Immanuel’s story, this encounter reveals several insights.  
The value of proverbs is easily overlooked by Westerners who come from cultures with  
high literacy. In primary oral cultures like the Builsa culture, however, proverbs retain a  
prominent position. When 108 Builsa were asked why they speak in proverbs, over one  
quarter responded, “This is the type of speech we like. It brings laughter and enjoyment  
into the conversation” \(Moon 2000\). Communication in Africa is an art form\: how you  
speak is just as important as what you say. Ong observed, “Oral cultures indeed produce  
powerful and beautiful verbal performances of high artistic value and human worth”  
\(1982\:14\). This artistic value can be easily overlooked by missionaries. A Nigerian pastor  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **118**  
once told me, “If one does not speak in proverbs, he will not carry people with him for  
long - they will walk for a short way only and then leave him. If he speaks in proverbs,  
however, the people will not be able to close their ears!” Immanuel was drawn to the  
proverbs meeting since it was entertaining. This became a hook for him to engage the  
Scripture with his culture.  
It should also be noted that the logic pattern of primary oral cultures is different  
than that of cultures with high literacy. Ong \(1982\) shows that oral cultures tend to be  
elaborate in thought but this thought is organized and expressed in a way that is foreign to  
the literate mind. Ong notes, “More than any other single invention, writing has  
transformed human consciousness” \(1982\:78\). Overall, oral thinking is formulaic and  
structured in proverbs and other set expressions. Notice how the meaning of the first  
proverb was described by giving another proverb. Serious thought is intertwined with  
memory systems and these mnemonics, such as proverbs and stories, form the basis of  
thought itself. Ong continues, “You know what you can recall... In a primary oral  
culture, to solve effectively the problem of retaining and retrieving carefully articulated  
thought, you have to do your thinking in mnemonic patterns, shaped for ready oral  
recurrence” \(1982\:33-34\). The discussion of the proverb was stitched together by several  
other proverbs that could be readily recalled. This logic is different than the linear  
propositional logic that literate cultures tend to prefer.  
The logic of the Buli word *“magsi”* deserves some explanation. When I first  
discussed with some of the church leaders about critiquing proverbs, I discussed how we  
wanted to accept the “*wensie*” \(truth\) and reject the “*venta*” \(falsehood\). One pastor  
observantly approached me later and suggested that perhaps we should find other terms  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **11 9**  
to use. These terms were too harsh and it pushed them back into rigid categories of full  
acceptance or full rejection. This was the opposite of what we were trying to accomplish.  
As a result, he suggested the term *“magsi”* This word means “to be proper, fitting,  
suitable, or right.” This is an appropriate term since it suggests a redirection or  
adjustment to cultural issues but does not force the decision into black and white  
categories of rejection or acceptance. This term will be used throughout the narrative  
since it is an appropriate Builsa way to describe how we can approach cultural issues -  
we want to discover what is proper, fitting, suitable, or right in the Builsa culture from  
God’s perspective, as revealed in Scripture.  
It is also interesting to observe how the proverbs discussion opened up a door to  
talk about a wide variety of cultural issues. Notice how the topic of witchcraft was  
brought into the discussion. While this is an ever-present reality in the Builsa area, it is  
not often discussed in the church. By taking the proverbs seriously, it opened the way for  
them to freely discuss cultural roots that are often not addressed. This prepared the  
ground for contextualization. Also notice how the proverbs discussion encouraged  
Immanuel to bring forth his heart-felt tragedy for discussion in the church. He was  
beginning to feel that it was safe to discuss this issue. While this heartfelt issue was  
foremost in his mind, it would often not be addressed in church. It would be part of the  
“excluded middle” that Scripture seems not to address. After the proverbs discussion,  
however, Immanuel observed how Scripture addressed the meaning of the first proverb.  
This encouraged him to think that perhaps Scripture will speak to his issue, like it did  
with the proverb. Immanuel was starting to learn how to ride the bike. If he persists and  
rides this bike enough on ‘easy ground,’ then he will be able to tackle the difficult hills.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 120  
This will take some time and practice, but a crucial step is to get on the bike for the first  
time. Immanuel has taken that first bike ride - now it is time to practice.  
Practicing to Ride the Bicycle  
*Before the group was ready to discuss a difficult issue like Immanuel’s tragedy,*  
*the discussion leader felt that it would be helpful to discuss another proverb. The*  
*meaning o f this proverb may be a bit harder to uncover and describe. This exercise*  
*would help the group later to meet more difficult issues like Immanuel’s.*  
*The discussion leader presented the proverb,* “Fi dan sak bulorik dueni deka, ku  
chum le faari fi m a” *\(Ifyou teach an ugly man how to marry women, in the future he*  
*will marry your mother\). The entire room burst into laughter. The imagery was painfully*  
*funny.*  
*“I don’t think that we should discuss that proverb. It is not good and it will not*  
*help our discussion, ” responded Pastor David.*  
*Several others nodded their heads in agreement. Pastor Kofi added, “Sometimes,*  
*these proverbs are used to insult certain people. They can also be used to hide things*  
*from some people since the meaning is not clear. We should be careful not to use*  
*proverbs like this. In addition, there are some proverbs that use language that would be*  
*embarrassing to use in church! ”*  
*Sensing how the group was uncomfortable at discussing this proverb, the*  
*discussion leader pointed out, “Yes, it is true that we need to be careful how, when, and*  
*with whom we use proverbs. On the other hand, this proverb exists in our culture fo r a*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 121  
*reason. It points out some issue or problem that is a part o f how we Builsa think and live.*  
*Do you see that tree over there? What do you see?”*  
*"I see big limbs and some baobob fruit on the ends o f the limbs, ” answered*  
*Immanuel.*  
*“I see the leaves and birds resting in there, ” replied another.*  
*The discussion leader continued, "Good. That huge tree, leaves, fruits, and birds*  
*are all the things that you can see. The tree only stands strong, though, because o f the*  
*roots that you do not see. I f there were no roots, then the tree would not be there. The*  
*tree is there because o f the roots. In the same way, this proverb is there because o f some*  
*roots in our culture. We should not dismiss it too quickly. Let us take the time to*  
*understand the proverb well so that we can understand the roots in our culture that are*  
*allowing this tree to stand. ”*  
*“I see what you are saying. You are right that there are some roots in our culture*  
*that we do not like to talk about. How do we uncover these roots so that we can see*  
*them? ” replied Pastor Kofi.*  
*"We need to dig down to the roots by asking some questions. This is hard work,*  
*just like it is hard to dig up the roots o f the Baobob tree. L et’s ask questions like\:*  
*'Who says this proverb and to whom do they say it? ’*  
*‘When does this proverb come into conversation? What happens just before it that brings*  
*this proverb forth ’*  
*'Where is this proverb spoken* - *in the market, funeral, in the house, at the Chief’s court?*  
*‘Why do they say this proverb? ’*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 122  
*Immanuel couldn 't stand it any longer and he had to jump in to answer some o f*  
*the questions. “I ’ll tell you who says this proverb. You say this to a person when you help*  
*them, but then they turn around and do something bad back to you. I had a friend who*  
*called me to come and help him on the farm. I agreed and helped him all day. Later, I*  
*called him to come andfarm fo r me, but he never showed up. I struggled hard to get the*  
*farming done. I did good to him but he repaid me with unkindness; therefore, I used this*  
*proverb to describe how he treated me.* ”  
*Pastor Kofi added, “I had a similar experience. The elders say,* Ti dan pa poning  
a poni nuru zuk, wa chum le pa takaribaling a poni fi zuk’ *\(Ifyou take a pair o f scissors*  
*and cut someone’s hair, in the future he will take a piece o f broken pot and cut your*  
*hair\). A friend o f mine was out offood one year and I helped the family by giving them*  
*some food. Another year, my crop did not do well and I asked that friend for help. He*  
*totally forgot about the past help that I gave him and he refused my request fo r help. ”*  
*“Why does that happen? ” the discussion leader asked the group.*  
*“I think that it is greed, ’’ replied one pastor.*  
*“That is a root in our culture, isn’t it? Does* Naawen Wani *talk about this? ”*  
*asked the discussion leader.*  
*“Yes. Galatians 5 says that greed is from the heart ofpeople. This proverb*  
*reveals our sinful nature.* Naawen *warns us that those who practice greed will not see*  
Naawen*’s kingdom. Greed can destroy our community and our church, ” replied*  
*Immanuel.*  
*“True, this proverb is telling us to not have mercy on others since they may*  
*return evil to us. I f I have mercy on an ugly man and help him, then he may cause*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 123  
*problems fo r me by marrying my mother. How would my father like that! ” replied Pastor*  
*Kofi. Everyone laughed and clapped.*  
*Pastor David added, “In Scripture, we see that* Naawen *had mercy on us;*  
*therefore, we should show mercy to others. You never know how* Naawen *can use that*  
*person. Think about the conversion o f Saul.* Naawen *turned him around to be a blessing*  
*to the church. ”*  
*“I think that this proverb is used because someone has been offended or been*  
*mistreated. As a result, they want to get back at that person. As we are discussing it here,*  
*I remember* Naawen Wani *tells us not to repay evilfor evil. We should repay evil with*  
*good, ” Immanuel added.*  
*The discussion leader pulled it together fo r them, “We are discussing some roots*  
*in our culture that Scripture rejects. We should not withhold mercy from others; rather,*  
*we need to show mercy as* Naawen *has done fo r us. Remember what* Yezu *has done for*  
*us - then act that way to others. Another root that we discussed was greed. We need to*  
*warn our people about greed since it can destroy our church and community. This may*  
*help the church members understand why it is important to bring their tithe to church.*  
*We also talked about how to deal with those who mistreat us. Does that ever happen in*  
*the church? How should we respond as church leaders? Instead o f rejecting those who*  
*persecute us, we should repay evil with good. ”*  
*“But wait... I f I try to not be greedy and give all o f my food to others and help*  
*everyone, then I may not have enough fo r myfamily to eat, ” responded Immanuel.*  
*Pastor Kofi had a pensive look, “Mmm. This is not easy to follow. The person that*  
*offends you may be very troublesome. There may be a reason that this person is this way*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 124  
*and they need to feel the consequences o f their actions. The elders say,* Ti dan nya diok a  
bo nyiamu po a cheng, fa yieri; fi dan nya nuru a bo nyiamu po a cheng, basi ’ *\(If you see*  
*wood floating by in the water, you pull it out; I f you see a person floating by in the*  
*water, leave them\). ’’Many burst out in laughter.*  
*“Can you explain that one to us? ” asked the discussion leader.*  
*“Well... A story goes like this. A man made a promise to the river shrine that he*  
*would bring an animal sacrifice to the river if it helped him to get wealthy. Later on, the*  
*man got wealthy but he did not fulfill his promise to the river by bringing the sacrifice.*  
*One day, while he was near the river, it pulled him into the water to pay the debt.*  
*Another person saw him floating by calling fo r help. I f this person pulled the man out o f*  
*the water, then the debt to the river would still not be paid back. The man who owed the*  
*debt would then push the person who rescued him into the river to pay back the debt that*  
*he owed. The rescuer then meant to do a good thing to the man, but he would be repaid*  
*with evil. It would have been better if the rescuer let the man float by. I f it was a piece o f*  
*wood, however, he could use it in his house and it would not push him in the water;*  
*therefore, he should pull the wood out o f the water. Do you understand now? ’’*  
*The fo g was slowly beginning to clear. It touched some deep feelings in the*  
*process. Each o f the pastors could think o f other instances where they meant to do*  
*something goodfor someone but it turned out that they were not appreciated and the*  
*person turned against the pastor.*  
*Pastor David noted, “This proverb talks about how to deal with difficult people. It*  
*implies that we should be careful with who we help and how we do it.* Naawen Wani *also*  
*warns us to be careful with people. We should not throw our pearls to swine. We should*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 125  
*be wise as serpents but harmless as doves. We know that we should not repay evil with*  
*evil, but we should also be careful and wise how we help people. ”*  
*The discussion leader added, “Yes, I think that is one root that we can accept.*  
*When I think about* Yezu *though, he did not leave us in the water. We were like that man*  
*who was drowning in the water. Because o f our sins, we were going to die.* Yezu *saw us*  
*floating in the river and he pulled us out o f the water. What did people do to him? They*  
*pushed him in the water so that he would die fo r us! He KNEW that people were going to*  
*push him in the water by sending him to the cross; yet, he still pulled us out o f the river. ”*  
*A somber hush fell on the room like the settling o f dew in the early morning. “I*  
*never thought about that before. He knew that people would hurt him fo r his good deeds,*  
*yet, he did it anyway. That is powerful love, isn’t it? ” spoke Immanuel.*  
*“It sure is,* ” *responded Pastor Kofi, “It was not easy for* Yezu *to reach in the*  
*water and grab us out, particularly since he knew that we would push him in. Yet, he*  
*obeyed* Naawen - *that is why we see* Naawen*’s blessings today. We can help others see*  
Naawen*’s blessings today in* Buluk *by following what* Yezu *did. ”*  
*“I have to be honest though. Sometimes, a small misunderstanding happens in the*  
*church and the church members get angry with me, ” one pastor confided “They forget*  
*all o f the good that I have done and only remember the thing that made them angry. As a*  
*result, they start talking about getting a new pastor. I heard a proverb in Tamale the*  
*other day that said, ‘Ifyo u only consider the smoke, you will put out the fire.* ’ *They*  
*forget the benefit o f the fire and only think about the irritation o f the smoke; therefore,*  
*they want to get rid o f the pastor and put out the fire.* ”  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 126  
*The mood in the room now changed. Each pastor could identify with this struggle.*  
*It was not easy being a pastor. The pastors struggled to care fo r the people because they*  
*loved* Naawen. *There was no salary from the mission and they receivedjust a small*  
*collection from the church. There was often rejection from other Builsa because o f their*  
*faith. When church members complain, it was very discouraging and the pastors, at*  
*times, consider quitting. They tried to do good and help the ugly man to learn how to*  
*marry, but this kindness was occasionally returned by complaints against the pastor - it*  
*felt like an ugly man just married the pastor's mother!*  
*This created a long discussion about how to deal with misunderstandings in the*  
*church. They all agreed that leaders can expect problems to come at times even though*  
*the leader has not done wrong, citing the example o f Moses. They then discussed*  
*Matthew 18 to deal quickly with misunderstandings so that they do not grow into big*  
*problems. I f not addressed quickly, then gossip will destroy the pastor’s reputation,*  
*which will put out the fire.*  
*After this long discussion, the discussion leader asked, “We all know how it feels*  
*to be misunderstood and to have our good intentions repaid with evil. Do you think that*  
*missionaries sometimes feel this way? ”*  
*There was a long pause. The pastors remembered how they complained about the*  
*missionaries at times. They would talk about them behind their backs. Pastor David*  
*finally broke the silence, “I had never thought o f that before. We all know that they make*  
*mistakes at times that become like smoke in our eyes. When that happens, we forget all o f*  
*the good that they have done fo r us and we think about putting out the fire. It would be*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 127  
*better fo r us to discuss the problem with them so that we can manage the smoke and still*  
*enjoy the fire. ”*  
*Pastor Kofi added, “This is true. I do not think that any o f the missionaries have*  
*meant to do us harm. They have done many good things to help us. Their intentions were*  
*good, but they have made mistakes since they did not understand our culture and they*  
*were also human beings with their own weaknesses. When we complain and gossip about*  
*them instead o f discussing the root issues with them, they may feel like the ugly man has*  
*married their mother.* ”  
*“I can now understand how they feel at times, since I have felt this way too, ”*  
*added Pastor David. The pastors were all very reflective as they recalled their recent*
