*interactions with the missionaries. They each had a new sense o f understanding that they*  
*had not considered before.*  
*The discussion leader concluded, “You all have identified roots in the proverb*  
*that* Naawen Wani *affirms. We need to understand that leadership will include some*  
*misunderstanding along the way. We should not be surprised when it happens.*  
‘Gingeling ale nyinga dom chaab alege ba bo ale chaab ’ *\(The tongue and teeth bite each*  
*other but they live together\). How we respond is critical. Matthew 18 tells us how to*  
*quickly discuss the root issues so that the misunderstanding does not grow into a big ugly*  
*situation. This is better than talking behind the backs o f others and gossiping since*  
‘Bumborim ale wang yeri ’ *\(Gossip will scatter a house\). We also discussed how we need*  
*to use wisdom in who we help and how we do it. We should feed and take care o f our own*  
*households first; otherwise, we will be worse than unbelievers. We should not give all o f*  
*our food away such that our own households are starving. We should use wisdom before*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 128  
*we jump in the water too quickly. You have all done well to discuss the root issues and*  
*use* Naawen Wani *to teach us those that are* magsi *and those that are not* magsi. ”  
*Pastor David added, “I can see by the looks on our faces that we are getting to*  
*the roots. Perhaps we should take a break and let this food settle in out stomachs. I f we*  
*eat too much without chewing it, then it is not easy to digest it. ” All agreed and arose for*  
*a break period.*  
While the church leaders are on break, let us reflect on what transpired. There are  
aspects of culture that are hard to explain and describe. Notice how several of the church  
leaders did not want to discuss a certain proverb initially. The proverbs functioned as  
symbols to provide a window into the culture that would otherwise be closed. Tillich  
described how symbols “open up levels of reality which otherwise are closed” and that  
they “open up levels of the human mind of which we otherwise are not aware”  
\(1955\:109\). This is crucial for contextualization to occur since hidden aspects of culture  
need to be understood before they can be engaged with Scripture. The proverbs serve to  
bring the issues to the surface so that the roots can be explored.  
As a result of exploring the window of proverbs, a world of discovery exploded  
open for the church leaders. The value of proverbs in this discovery process is affirmed  
by the Yoruba proverb, “Owe *I ’esin oro; bi oro ba nu, owe ni a f i nwa a”* **\(A proverb is a**  
**horse which can carry you swiftly to the discovery of ideas sought\)** \(Delano  
1976\:Foreward\). The church leaders were able to dig into the roots of the proverb in  
order to find Builsa cultural values. It is at the level of the roots that the Scripture can  
most actively engage the culture resulting in transformation. Often times, discussions  
center around aspects of the tree without digging into the roots. As will be shown later,  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **1 2 9**  
when people focus only on the tree \(aspects that can be seen such as ugly people, a man  
in the water, etc.\) and neglect the roots \(values that are underneath such as greed, mercy,  
wisdom in relationships, etc.\), this leads to people becoming entrenched in positions of  
total acceptance or total rejection. Diatribes are thrown at each camp, resulting in each  
camp becoming more entrenched in their initial positions. Unfortunately, this does not  
result in an engagement of the Scripture with the culture. No contextualization occurs. By  
digging to the roots of the issue, it is at this point that Scripture speaks powerfully. This  
results in a world of discovery for the group. This discovery is important in order for the  
church to develop a contextual theology and understanding. Notice the new  
understanding of the love of Jesus that was revealed through the proverbs, as Jesus had  
the courage to grab us out of the water. Also notice the deeper empathy for pastors and  
missionaries that resulted. A deeper understanding of the conflicts in leadership, as well  
as how to deal with these issues also resulted. The proverbs then are symbols that become  
“magic portals into the other world where the truth of ones’ religion is visible, felt, and  
far overshadows the inconsistent ordinary” \(Ellwood 1983\:100\). Once these windows are  
opened, a world of discovery awaits. That brings hope to people like Immanuel who are  
not sure if Scripture will address his ‘excluded middle’ issue. Let us return to the story to  
see how this occurs.  
Digging to the Roots  
*During the break, Immanuel was getting excited. He saw how the process o f*  
*digging to the roots o f an issue was helpful. This helped him to see how Scripture*  
*interpreted Builsa culture, even though he thought at first that they should not discuss*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 130  
*that proverb. He had this growing sense that perhaps this process would help him to*  
*address his own tragedy. Immediately after the break, Immanueljumped in, “I like what*  
*we have been doing here. This helps me to use* Naawen Wani *to interpret proverbs. I can*  
*see that they may have pieces that are* magsi *and some pieces that are not* mags/. *Will*  
*this also work with other problems that we may have? ”*  
*The discussion leader was happy to see that Immanuel was a step ahead.*  
*Discussing proverbs is entertaining and helpful, but it is really a small hook to catch a*  
*bigger fish. “Why don’t we try it and see how it can help, ” he answered. “Who has a*  
*problem that needs to be discussed? ” he said, as he baited the hook.*  
*Immanuel was a fish in need o f a worm. He quickly jumped at the bait, “Can you*  
*all please help me with my problem? ” Heads nodded in approval, and Immanuel*  
*discussed his problem again with the entire group. He ended with, “The elders are*  
*waiting to hear back from me. What should I d o - refuse them or allow them to take the*  
*dirt? ”*  
*Pastor David was very familiar with the problem. He recalled his previous advice*  
*and now he wasn ’t so sure as he was before. He started slowly, as if he was walking on*  
*soft ground and he didn ’t want to slip and sink in, “At first, I thought that you need to*  
*refuse the elders and not allow them to collect the dirt since they will use this to pull our*  
*people away fo r the path o f* Yezu Krista. ”  
*Several people saw the truth in what he said. One church leader summarized,*  
“Maga, maga alaa yik waung jiuk” *\(Slowly, slowly you catch the monkey’s tail\). I f we*  
*back down and give these elders the dirt now, then our church members will think that*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 131  
*our faith is wavering. These elders will then slowly draw away our people from the*  
*church. ”*  
*Pastor Kofi listened and understood the truth o f what was just said. Pastor Kofi*  
*was also very familiar with the problem and he recalled his former advice. He was not so*  
*sure anymore either. Even as he spoke, he was rethinking his position, “Ifirst thought*  
*that you should simply allow the elders to collect the dirt since we do not want a fight.*  
*Let the elders do what they want while we stay out o f it. ”*  
*Immanuel responded, “Yes. I can see the truth o f both positions, BUT we are still*  
*talking about the trees. These are the things that you can see - elders, dirt, grave,* bano.  
*We need to start talking about the roots - the things that you can’t see. I f we want to get*  
*to the roots, we need to start digging deeper. ”*  
*The discussion leader quietly smiled and remained silent. He wanted to see how*  
*fa r they could go to catch this fish.*  
*Pastor David asked, “Why do you think that elders are asking fo r the dirt? What*  
*is behind this? ’’*  
*Pastor Kofi replied, “Think about this. What do the elders think will happen if we*  
*do not give them the dirt? ’’*  
*“Well...they are probably afraid something bad will happen. ”*  
*“Like what? ”*  
*Immanuel jumped in, “They are afraid that some evil has caused this death. I f*  
*they do not fin d out why and stop it, then they are afraid that someone else will die! ’’*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **132**  
*“I f we just reject them outright, ” added Pastor Kofi, “then the elders will think*  
*that we don’t care if someone dies or not. We are saying to them that their death does not*  
*bother us, or worse yet, they may think that we hate them. ”*  
*This was starting to sink into Pastor David. He honestly asked, “So what should*  
*we do? ”*  
*“I am not sure yet, but we are digging up some roots - fear and how to get*  
*protection, ” Kofi responded. “I can identify with those roots. There are folks in my*  
*church that are still gripped by fear and they often ask fo r prayer fo r protection. ”*  
*“One church member asked me the other day about his* bagi. *He wanted to know*  
*if he could still keep it. I knew that he did not fully understand about the power o f* Yezu  
*yet so I had to talk about how* Naawen *showed us and gives us his power through* Yezu. /  
*then showed him* Naawen Wani *in Psalms 18\:2,* Naawen *is the* bagi *o f my salvation. ’ It*  
*helped him to know that* Naawen *gives us protection and strength through* Yezu *so that*  
*he could throw his old* bagi *away.* ”  
*Pastor Kofi added, “Suppose I asked you to take your clothes off and walk to the*  
*market. Would you agree? ’’ They all smiled. He continued,* *“That is what we are asking*  
*the elders to do when we reject them without an alternative. We are taking away their*  
*protection without giving anything to replace it. They are standing naked! ” Laughter*  
*filled the room and all seemed to catch the point.*  
*“Another root seems to be that they want to see* Naawen*’s blessing on them, ”*  
*another church leader added. “They know that evil has caused a problem and they want*  
*a spiritual answer to a spiritual problem. I f we reject them with no response then we give*  
*them no alternative than to go to the* bano. ”  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 133  
*Immanuel jumped in, “We also want to see* Naawen *’s blessing in our house and*  
*community. Doesn't* Naawen *want to bless* Buluk? *His plans fo r us are fo r good and not*  
*fo r evil. We can agree with the elders on that root, can’t we?* ”  
*David was beginning to see the point, “Yes, those are roots that* Naawen Wani  
*agrees with and we can discuss this with the elders. We should give a spiritual response*  
*to spiritual issues. The problem is that their response is to go to the* bano, *who will*  
*consult with the ancestors. When they make sacrifices to the* bogluk, *First Corinthians*  
*10\:20-22 reminds us that the evil spirits receive these sacrifices and not the ancestors.*  
*We don’t want to open the door fo r evil spirits to bother us, do we? ”*  
*“I think I have enough problems already without making it worse, ” Immanuel*  
*replied tongue in cheek.*  
*Kofi was following the point as well, “So what do we do? ”*  
*“This is a root that* Naawen Wani *says is not* magsi, ” *Pastor David replied. “We*  
*can discuss this with the community elders and then explain to them what the church will*  
*do to address the other roots o f fear, protection, and God’s blessing. ”*  
*“But will the elders listen to us? ’’ Immanuel asked. He knew that the traditional*  
*elders were not always in agreement with the church members. It often seemed that both*  
*sides were at odds with each other, and neither side was willing to back down.*  
*“I think they will discuss it with us, ” one church leader added, “if we are willing*  
*to provide spiritual answers to spiritual issues. A baby in my church died recently and the*  
*elders said that it was a* chichiruk. *They wanted the mother to bring a sacrifice to the*  
bano. *I explained that this baby was a gift from* Naawen, *and we accept all o f his gifts. I*  
*offered to walk around the village with the church members to thank him fo r this gift and*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 134  
*pray that* Naawen *would cleanse any evil and protect the people. The elders agreed to*  
*this and did not force the church member to bring the sacrifice. They know that the*  
*Christian prayers have power - they really fear the Christian prayers.*  
*Pastor Kofi agreed. “Yes, they do fear Christian prayers because they have seen them*  
*work. We can tell them the story o f Balaam who tried to put a curse on the Israelites. Do*  
*you remember what* Naawen *said? ‘No one can curse those that* Naawen *has blessed. ’*  
*We can explain that* Yezu *sits at the right hand* o/'Naawen, *always looking out fo r us. We*  
*can pray that* Yezu *will cleanse the land o f any evil and that he will protect the elders*  
*and the community from any evil. We will ask the church members to walk around the*  
*village and pray fo r* Yezu *to bless them so that no curse can fall upon them. ”*  
*Pastor David liked this suggestion. This was a spiritual response to a spiritual*  
*issue. It also took the cultural issue seriously and showed respect to the elders by*  
*dialoging with them. Even more important, Immanuel felt that* Naawen *cared about his*  
*problem and that he was not alone in solving it.* Naawen Wani *addressed the roots o f the*  
*issue in order to give him some guidance. He now had the help* o/'Naawen *and the church*  
*to address the problem. It seemed that a heavy weight just liftedfrom his shoulders. He*  
*knew that the problem was still not over and that he still had to face the elders. They may*  
*be difficult and stubborn. For the first time, though, he felt that he had a solution that*  
*helped him to be fully in the path o f* Yezu *and also fully in the Builsa culture.*  
*The discussion leader now joined in, “You have done well to help Immanuel with*  
*his problem. When we just looked at the tree and discussed the problem, people tended to*  
*group themselves in two extremes\: either totally rejecting or totally accepting the elders.*  
*Once you dug under the tree and found the roots o f fear, protection,* Naawen*’s blessing* -  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 135  
*and there still may be more - then you were able to use* Naawen Wani *to address the*  
*issues. This helped you to bring a response that was both faithful to* Naawen *and also*  
*relevant to our culture. This should help us in the future when other issues arise. ”*  
*All agreed. They knew that issues would arise that would take them by surprise.*  
*They also knew that many issues would come before them that they had never been taught*  
*how to address in the Bible training that they received. Perhaps they were starting to*  
*learn a process that would be helpful. We will have to wait and see when another cultural*  
*issue arises.*  
*“I am ready fo r some chop, how about you? ” smiled Immanuel.*  
*Pastor Kofi replied, “Yes. This is hard work.* ‘Popo jiib alaa jii ngmazuk jiib ’  
*\(The stomach load carries the head load\). ’’*  
*Smiling, Pastor David added, “My stomach has a puncture - 1 say that it is time*  
*to patch the puncture. ”*  
*They all departed to eat together so that they would meet again another day.*  
This interaction shows us many things. Notice the rich use of metaphor and vivid  
imagery. The fear that the elders felt at having no protection was highlighted when they  
were described as sitting naked in the market. Of course, that would be too embarrassing  
and not permissible. This imagery helped the group to feel and understand the elders’  
position. It stimulated further reflection and discussion. This supports Klem’s observation  
that “the use of traditional forms of oral art would help many West African audiences to  
hear and study Bible portions” \(1982\:125\). The richness of metaphor in oral art is highly  
valued and appreciated in the Builsa culture, which stimulated further creativity in  
resolving the issues underneath this situation.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. It should also be noted that the communication process here does more than  
transmit information from a sender to a receiver \(transmission model\). Rather, the  
process of communication helps to construct a Christian worldview. This is an example  
of the constitutive model of communication. Through this process, the church leaders are  
constructing a view of how Scripture relates to their culture. Along the way, they are  
understanding their own culture more and identifying roots of fear, protection, blessing,  
etc. They are experiencing aspects of Scripture that “come alive” due to the similarity to  
their own cultural context \(e.g., Numbers 22 with Baalam\). They are also constructing a  
closer bond together as they jointly resolve Immanuel’s problem in a hermeneutical  
community. Immanuel walked away with new hope since he no longer carries his burden  
alone - the church stands with him. Notice how Pastors Kofi and David, who held  
opposite extreme positions, realized the weakness of their own positions and were then  
able to come towards the middle. The communication process helped them to construct a  
Builsa Christian response as the Scripture and culture interacted. The proverbs acted as a  
catalyst for this process.  
The process of critical contextualization was carried out as the church leaders  
were able to dig to the roots of the issues and then critique them. This opened up various  
responses other than just the customary responses of total acceptance or rejection.  
Phenomenological analysis was conducted as they asked questions together to uncover  
the roots. Ontological reflection and critical evaluation were conducted as they used their  
experience and the Buli Scripture to interpret the issues to find those pieces that were  
proper/fitting \(*magsi\)* and those that were not. They were able to identify both the image  
of God in culture as well as the stain of humankind’s sin. Finally, this discussion  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 137  
presented a Christian response that could result in missiological transformation in the  
Builsa community. This response was missiologically significant since it addressed root  
issues in the culture instead of merely debating over the leaves of the tree \(outer forms\).  
By practicing this process with proverbs, the Builsa church leaders were able to dig to the  
roots of cultural issues and engage them with Scripture.  
Chapter Summary  
Through the above narrative, we get an inside look at the struggles of the Builsa Christian  
community and how the serious study of proverbs can be helpful. Some of the highlights  
that deserve repeat notice are\:  
1\. Middle level issues are not easy to address. The common responses are either to reject  
culture completely or to accept it completely. This leads to little, if any, engagement of  
the Scripture with the culture, thereby leading to Split-level Christianity.  
2\. Since it is difficult to engage Scripture with culture, a process needs to be introduced  
whereby the Christian community can practice this in familiar areas that are relatively  
easy.  
3\. Proverbs are familiar in Builsa culture; therefore, they provide a good starting place for  
learning contextualization. Proverbs are particularly potent in oral cultures since they are  
memorable, enjoyable/engaging, promote exploration and discovery, are rich with  
metaphors, fit the logic pattern of oral learners, and are windows that reveal aspects of  
the soul of the culture.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 138  
4\. Using proverbs as a model, a form of critical contextualization is introduced that helps  
the Builsa dig to the roots of the culture \(exposing unseen values and meanings\) and not  
just focus on the observable outward forms.  
5\. When this same procedure is used to engage cultural issues with Scripture, the  
communication process involved helps to construct a Builsa Christian world view.  
Proverbs can be an effective catalyst for this process.  
Proverbs can also help outsiders to understand Builsa culture and then engage this  
understanding with Scripture. This will be a catalyst for them to construct a Christian  
message that is culturally relevant as well as Scripturally faithful. This is the theme of the  
next chapter.  
1 Gospel Recordings is a parachurch organization originally from Australia that has  
developed picture books/flip charts that help to illustrate Bible stories. The name  
Language Recordings has also been used, but the newest name of this organization is  
Global Recordings Network \(GRN\). SIM Ghana and the Bible Church of Africa have  
cooperated with GRN to develop cassette tapes in local languages in Northern Ghana that  
narrate the Bible stories. These are used extensively in church planting in Northern  
Ghana. When starting a new fellowship, the “Good News” flip chart is used for several  
weeks. This provides a general overview of Scripture from Genesis to Revelation. Once  
this is completed, the churches often start with the first flip chart for the Old Testament  
and then work their way through the Bible with consecutive picture books. The entire  
fellowship can listen to the cassettes by using a hand-crank cassette player that is given to  
them. In addition, small picture books can be purchased by the new believers in order to  
take these stories home with them and pass them onto others. This has allowed many oral  
learners to grasp an understanding of Scripture and pass this onto others. The entire set of  
picture books is also taught to pastors at the Chiana Bible School in Northern Ghana.  
They are each given an entire set of the picture books prior to graduation. As a result,  
many pastors have a good working knowledge of Scripture via these Bible stories.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. Chapter 5 - An Outsider’s Perspective1  
*“Niva onyara kwivara Lugano rwange dare, ku umanyi mba”u* \(If you cannot explain a  
proverb in my language, then you don’t know me\).  
Learning to Paint  
*When Immanuel went home that day, he felt much lighter than when he started.*  
*He felt more hopeful than he hadfor a long time. The other pastors also sensed that they*  
*had learned something valuable that would help them in the future.*  
*When Pastor David returned to his village, Joe was visiting at his house. Joe was*  
*a white missionary who lived in Pastor David’s village. David remembered the first time*  
*that he met Joe several years ago.*  
*At that time, Joe was a new missionary and he was eager to learn the language*  
*and culture o f the Builsa. This was evident by the way he jumped into language learning*  
*and stuck with it. He also visited Builsa houses often, and his wife and he received*  
*visitors at his house in the village. They had a lot to learn but they were willing to learn*  
*andfind out what the Builsa questions were before giving quick answers.*  
*Joe noticed early that proverbs were often used in everyday conversation. It*  
*seemed that proverbs help to make the conversation more lively, enjoyable, and* “masa ”  
*\(sweet\). How something was said was as important as what was said, and proverbs*  
*helped to make the message become more attractive and palatable. Joe started to learn a*  
*few proverbs and understand the proper context for them. He then used them in his daily*  
*conversations.*  
139  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **1 4 0**  
*David remembered Joe's discouragement after 4 months o f language learning. It*  
*seemed to Joe that he was not progressing as quickly as he wanted. Joe wanted to hurry*  
*on to do “real ministry, ” and not take the painfully slow steps o f learning a language like*  
*a child.*  
*David encouraged Joe, “You are making good progress - keep with it. Any*  
*difficult task takes a lot o f patience and involves slow, small steps o f progress.* Maga,  
maga alaa yik waung jiuk *\(Slowly, slowly \[you\] catch the monkey’s tail\). ”*  
*Joe was not sure o f the meaning so he asked fo r an explanation.*  
*“A monkey is very difficult to catch since it is tricky and clever,* ” *explained*  
*David, “ I f you want to catch one, then you have to slowly take small steps and sneak up*  
*on it. Then, you can grab it by the tail; otherwise, it will run away. ”*  
*Joe laughed, “Yes! Language learning is tricky. I have been working hard at it*  
*and I think that I am learning something. Then, I get into a conversation with a Builsa*  
*and Ifeel totally lost - the language has run away from me! I have not been able to grab*  
*it by the tail yet. ”*  
*“Keep taking small steps - learn a little each day and use it a lot in conversation*  
*with Builsas. Soon, you will grab the tail, ” replied David.*  
*Joe persisted little by little to grab the monkey's tail. One day, he visited a*  
*neighbor as part o f his language/culture learning routine. After reciting his new Buli*  
*sentences and being corrected several times, the neighbor laughed, “You still sound like*  
*a little child ”*  
*Joe’s heart sank, partly because he was feeling frustrated but also partly due to*  
*the fact that the old man was right - Joe did sound like a child. His Buli sentences were*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 141  
*simple and short. Joe replied, “Yes, Baba, you are right. Your young grandson hears Buli*  
*better than I but I am still taking small steps each day. The elders have said,* “Maga,  
maga alaa yik waung jiuk. " *One day I will catch the monkey’s tail and bring it here for*  
*you to hear.* ”  
*Immediately, the old man jumped up and laughed so hard that people came out of*  
*the house to see what happened. The old man replayed the conversation fo r the others*  
*and then asked Joe to repeat what he just said. After Joe repeated the proverb, the old*  
*man sat down next to Joe with obvious pleasure and said, “Now you are talking like a*  
*Builsa. That is the way we like to talk with each other. When someone speaks without*  
*proverbs, they are like a baby that cannot reason fo r themselves -they are merely*  
*speaking in the rain. ’’*  
*Joe was starting to realize that speech in the Builsa area is an art form. Speaking*  
*Buli is more than just getting the correct grammar and syntax - it is more like learning to*  
*paint a picture than learning mathematics in school. As artwork, proverbs help to*  
*embellish and touch emotions as well as clarify and bring mental understanding. As a*  
*Westerner, Joe felt that he simply needed to fin d the right words to carry the meaning in*  
*order to communicate properly - he was looking for prepositional truth. He was now*  
*beginning to understand that the way words are couched in metaphor and indirect*  
*reference are highly valued and appreciated by the Builsa. Proverbs help to do this. Joe*  
*was not ready fo r what was to occur next.*  
*The old man affirmed Joe, “Our fathers spoke the truth* - ‘Maga, maga alaa yik  
waung jiuk. ” *You must do things slowly. They also said,* “Ba kan kali dai yeng a nak  
peesa a sue yui ya ” *\(They cannot sit one day and collect Fra-Fra potatoes to fill up a*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. *bag\). It takes more than one day to do a large task. Learning Buli is a large task and it*  
*will take time. I will help you to speak well. Remember these proverbs and use them in*  
*the correct place. People will then understand you and they will recognize that you are*  
*now a Builsa.* ”  
*That is how Joe started to appreciate and learn the use o f Builsa proverbs. Once*  
*he started to learn a few proverbs and use them in the correct context, other Builsa like*  
*Joe's neighbor, would tell him more. They would also take the time to use and explain*  
*them in conversation with Joe since they knew that Joe appreciated them. They felt free to*  
*show Joe how to paint oral art instead o f simply learning correct words and grammar.*  
While Joe is not a real person but a composite character based on missionaries  
that I interviewed and observed \(including myself\), this short narrative begins to describe  
how a new missionary could begin to use proverbs in ministry. The key is to begin as a  
learner and allow the local people to become the teachers. Tienou noted that,  
unfortunately, many missionaries come to Africa *after* their learning is finished instead of  
arriving in the middle of their learning.111 If missionaries have the attitude that they are  
still in the middle of their learning, then they will be open to new insights from the local  
culture and people. This will lead them to discover the Builsa questions. On the other  
hand, if they consider their learning to be finished, then they are more apt to give quick  
uninformed answers.  
Anthropologist Allison Howell observed many new missionaries in Ghana and  
commented that the willingness to learn is a big indicator of how thoroughly they will  
grasp the local culture and language.1V She affirmed that the use of proverbs in ministry  
falls into this category as well. If a missionary is willing to learn, then they will be open  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 143  
to learning new insights from the local proverbs. If this openness to learning does not  
exist, then excuses for not learning and using local proverbs are abundant. Some  
missionaries may complain that the proverbs are too hard to understand, their language  
level is not sufficient, they are afraid of using a proverb to offend someone, etc. While  
these may be valid, each of them can be overcome if there is a willingness to learn.  
Joe initially has a typical response to oral literature like proverbs - he overlooks  
their value in understanding the culture and the potential in theological discourse. This is  
partly due to his training in a literate culture, where the wisdom in proverbs is overlooked  
for more exact propositional truths. His theological training likely did not sensitize him to  
the value of oral literature like proverbs in oral cultures such as the Builsa, where the  
adult literacy rate is estimated at a mere 25% \(Akanling-Pare 1999\:42\). As a result, Joe  
sincerely wants to learn the Builsa culture and language but he overlooks the value of  
proverbs that are right under his feet. Once he understands the value they contain, he  
enters a new level of cultural appreciation.  
Joe also shows us how to begin using proverbs. It may help to think of oral  
literature more as an art form than a science subject. As such, the proper use of proverbs  
can be learned more like the learning of artwork as opposed to the learning of Algebra in  
school. In short, it requires careful observation, questioning, and large amounts of  
practice.  
Joe carefully observed Pastor David when he used a proverb well. By recording  
his observations for future reflection and reference, he is more likely to use them again.  
Especially note the context and occasion the proverbs are used. This forms the basis of  
the performance. Like the canvas upon which an artist paints, no proverb can be ‘painted’  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 144  
without the context. One African noted, “If you don’t sleep, then you don’t dream.” Also  
note how the audience participates in the performance since this is a group event and not  
a solo act.  
Joe was close enough to his elderly neighbor that he could ask questions of him.  
This will help him to learn more proverbs and their proper usage. They are the master  
painters and Joe is the apprentice looking for advice. The elder was delighted once he  
realized that Joe was sincerely interested in Builsa proverbs and culture. As a result, the  
elder wanted to sit and discuss more proverbs with Joe. In the process, this may open  
dialogue concerning Christianity, as well as understanding of the culture and Scripture  
that Joe had overlooked before - look for these discoveries to come later in the story.  
Joe also practiced using the proverb that he learned and this opened a world of  
discovery, including the learning of new proverbs. Every master artist has spent years to  
perfect their work. Oral art is the same. I like the advice that I was given for language  
learning, “Learn a little; Use a lot.” It is better to learn the artful use of a few proverbs  
than to learn many proverbs but not skillfully apply them. This takes time and patience  
but Joe will show us how to slowly catch the monkey’s tail.  
The positive response from the elder also reflects the value of the proper use of  
language. As discussed under the definition for the word “Proverb,” the Builsa consider  
communication that is direct and clear \(.*Biik-pieli*\) as simple speech used by a child or  
someone speaking to a child. In Joe’s language learning, he focused on *Biik-pieli* since he  
was learning sentences that were very direct and clear. From'Joe’s perspective, this  
would form the basis for good communication. From the Builsa perspective, this is  
simple speech that is not taken seriously by an adult audience. When speech becomes  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 145  
more obscure \(*Biik-wiani*\) or indirect/less clear \(*Biik-sobili*\), then this elevates the  
speaker from a child to an adult who should be heard. This may be the exact opposite of  
what Joe would expect, but the proper use of proverbs can help Joe’s communication  
effectiveness. Eventually, they will open windows for Joe to understand the Builsa  
culture and Scripture. Let us see how that happens  
Chicken Theology  
*Slowly but surely, Joe was learning the Buli language and culture. He started to*  
*collect and record proverbs. He noted the context, the possible meanings, and the English*  
*translations. He referred to these periodically when preparing to minister and he began*  
*using proverbs in his teaching, preaching, evangelism, etc. When he gave a proverb, it*  
*almost always had an instantaneous response. Builsa were surprised and delighted to*  
*hear their own proverbs. The Builsa appreciated hearing proverbs from other Builsa, but*  
*they seemed to enjoy it doubly when they heard their proverbs spoken by outsiders. It*  
*seemed that the sweet talk became extra sweet.*  
*Joe wondered what value there may be in studying the proverbs in the light of*  
*Scripture. Did God perhaps put this wisdom in the culture in order to prepare the Builsa*  
*to understand and appreciate Scripture? I f proverbs were used in communicating the*  
*Scripture, could this clear away the fo g in theological communication? Could proverbs*  
*help root Christianity in vernacular soil? Would proverbs open ears to hear Scripture*  
*that may otherwise be closed? Joe was eager to discuss these questions with others.*  
*When the Builsa church leaders met to discuss Buli proverbs and then reflect upon their*  
*meaning in the light o f Scripture, Joe was eager to attend.*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 146  
*Twelve Builsa pastors attended the meeting, and Pastor Kofi opened with the*  
*proverb* “Nurubiik a labri ka kpiak kawpta po” \(A human being hides in the feathers of  
a fowl\). *Joe was totally puzzled, while the Builsa seemed to enjoy reflecting on this*  
*proverb. Upon Joe’s request, the church leaders explained the background o f the*  
*proverb.*  
*In the life o f the Builsa people, fowls are used to hide shame or problems. I f*  
*someone comes upon a problem requiring money to solve it, they can always sell some o f*  
*the fowls at market and then use the money to solve the problem. In this way, they hide*  
*behind the chicken’s feathers so that the shame o f the problem does not reach the person.*  
*The fowl is also commonly used in situations requiring sacrifice to the ancestors or earth*  
*shrines fo r problems such as sickness, infertility, drought, famine, etc. The fow l is*  
*sacrificed to the ancestors or earth shrine to solve the problem and cover our shame. In*  
*this way, a Builsa will feel safe or protected as long as there are fowls in the house;*  
*hence, they feel they can “hide inside the feathers o f the fowl. ”*  
*Joe was catching on slowly, “So the chicken is an essential part o f the Builsa*  
*culture. It is used to solve problems so that the chicken receives the brunt o f the problem*  
*and it will not reach us. I f we have chickens around our homes, then we feel safe from*  
*dangers that may come. Is that right? ”*  
*“Yes, Joe, chickens help us to feel safe and protected. They are sacrificed or sold*  
*fo r us - they take our problems upon them and we hide safely in their feathers. They also*  
*help us initiate friendships, ” responded Kofi. “I f I want to start a friendship with*  
*someone, then I offer them a chicken fo r us to share a meal together, or I offer them a*  
*chicken to take home with them.* ”  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 147  
*Kofi continued, “Now that I am a* Kristobiik, *Ifeel that* Yezu *is the chicken that I*  
*hide under. When problems come, I can run to* Yezu *in prayer and ask him to cover my*  
*shame and protect me. He will bear the fu ll impact o f the problem that has come upon*  
*me, and I can safely rest in His feathers. ”*  
*Immanuel added, “When we rest in the feathers* o/’Yezu, *then we no longer need*  
*to have a* jiuk, bagi, *or any other black medicine to protect us. The feathers o f Yezu will*  
*cover us - our relationship with Him assures us that He will cover us with His wings.*  
Naawen Wani *says that* Naawen *will “cover you with his feathers and under his wings*  
*you will fin d refuge; his faithfulness will be your shield and rampart” \(Psalms 91\:4\). ”*  
*Joe was stunned. He remembered reading this verse in seminary while studying in*  
*the West. The imagery “Hiding under the wings o f God” was strange to his ears and it*  
*was difficult for him to gain the meaning o f the metaphor back then. Now, the meaning*  
*was starting to dawn on him. The perspective o f the Builsa culture brought out a richer*  
*meaning o f this Scripture passage. This dealt with protection from harm, shame, and*  
*difficulties. It also implied a close relationship with God, who was willing to receive the*  
*brunt o f our difficulties as we hid under his protection. “What a wonderful metaphor, ”*  
*Joe thought to himself, “and it took another cultural perspective fo r me to gain this*  
*insight. ” Little did Joe know that the best was yet to come.*  
*Immanuel added, “This proverb has touched me deeply and it helps me to*  
*understand the heart* o/’Yezu. ” *Joe was eager to hear more.*  
*“When I hear this proverb and read Matthew 23\:3 7,1 can feel* Yezu*’s heart and*  
*desire fo r us Builsa people, ” continued Immanuel.* Yezu *says, “How often I have longed*  
*to gather your children together, as a hen gathers her chicks under her wings. ” That is*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 148  
Yezu*’s desire fo r us - to protect us, cover our shame, receive the brunt o f our difficulties.*  
*That is a closer friend than I have ever known! ”*  
*K ofi’s eyes lit up, “Are you sure that is in* Naawen Wani - *let me see that. ” He*  
*read slowly in Buli how* Yezu *wanted to* “pawbi ni meena a tara ase kpiak ale pawbi ka  
bisa dii la ” *This literally means to “wrap you all up like a fowl wraps up her children*  
*\(under her wings\).* ” *Since fowls are a daily part o f the life-experience o f the Builsa, the*  
*picture o f a hen wrapping up her chicks under her wings in order to protect them from*  
*hawks and others dangers was a very vivid and concrete picture in K ofi’s mind Kofi sat*  
*back and smiled as he reflected on this picture o f Yezu and what it meant to him.*  
*David then added an application from the book o f Ruth, “Do you remember how*  
*Ruth was a widow? Like our widows here in* Buluk, *she had little hope fo r the future.*  
*When she placed herself under* Naawen*’s feathers,* Naawen *covered her shame and*  
*brought about a wonderful blessing. Listen to the praise she receivedfrom Boaz in Ruth*  
*2\:12, “May you be richly rewarded by the Lord, the God o f Israel, under whose wings*  
*you have come to take refuge ” \(emphasis mine\).*  
*It was Joe’s turn to be surprised, “Are you sure that is in the Bible? Let me read*  
*that. ” Joe had read the book o f Ruth several times before but he never noticed the*  
*imagery o f “Hiding under the wings o f God. ” He could now feel Ruth’s desperation of*  
*widowhood, and he also understood the imagery o f taking refuge under God’s wings. Joe*  
*was learning new things from Scripture that he had overlooked before.*  
*For the next month, Joe chewed on these thoughts. This proverb raised other*  
*questions fo r him concerning Scripture. Could this also give additional understanding as*  
*to the significance o f the cherubims' wings covering the ark o f the covenant in the*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **1 4 9**  
*tabernacle \(Exodus 25\:17-22\) and God’s words, “There, above the cover between the*  
*two cherubim that are over the ark o f the testimony, 1 will meet with you ” \(Exodus*  
*25\:22\)? This imagery was repeated in Solomon’s temple \(IKings 8\:6-11\) where “The*  
*cherubim spread their wings over the places o f the ark’’ \(I Kings 8\:7\). Again, Joe*  
*wondered if it may explain some o f the imagery behind God carrying Israel on his wings*  
*when bringing them out o f Egypt \(Exodus 19\:4\), as well as shed some light on the hard to*  
*understand passages in Ezekiel 10, etc.*  
*Joe was realizing that Builsa culture offered another perspective from which to*  
*see biblical truth that he normally overlooked. The proverb was the window to open this*  
*understanding fo r him* - *he was eager to learn more.*  
Let us step back and see what Joe is learning. Joe learned theology from a very  
reputable seminary in the West. His training was excellent. By reading his English  
Scripture through his own cultural background, he was able to understand certain aspects  
of God. Christian truth, however, is greater than what one culture alone can reveal. As  
shown in Figure 7 \(Chapter 2\), there are large aspects of Christian truth that are  
overlooked by an individual culture and therefore require the engagement of mother  
tongue Scriptures \(such as Buli\) with those cultures \(such as the Builsa\) in order for other  
aspects of God to be revealed. Proverbs can be a window into the Builsa culture that  
reveal aspects of this culture to Joe. When this understanding was engaged with the Buli  
Scriptures, Joe grasped aspects of Christian truth that his previous seminary training \(as  
good as it was\) did not address.  
Tarr \(1994\) refers to this as needing two lenses on a pair of glasses in order to see  
more clearly. He observes that both Western and African cultures provide insights into  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 150  
Scripture that the other overlooks; therefore, a greater appreciation for Scripture can be  
obtained by looking through both lenses. He uses oral literature, such as proverbs and  
stories, to provide windows into African culture.  
While this may sound radical to those who have not deeply engaged themselves in  
another culture, it is starting to make sense to Joe. A closer look at epistemological  
assumptions provides some insight to help us understand Joe’s discoveries. Our  
underlying assumptions of how we know what we know \(epistemology\) are often  
unquestioned; they are merely assumed. These unquestioned assumptions, however, can  
lead well-meaning and devout Christians like Joe to vastly different responses on issues  
of Scripture and culture. Hiebert \(1999\:56\) observed\:  
Many of the current debates, both in theology and in mission, seem to reflect  
epistemological differences rather than disagreements over theological content.  
The results can be disconcerting. We use the same words, but we have the uneasy  
feeling that we are not all using them in the same ways.  
Hiebert identified three commonly assumed epistemologies. These will be  
discussed in order to identify the epistemological assumptions that often underlie the  
views of Scripture and culture. These can be classified as various degrees of realism\:  
1\. A total realist position \(called Naive Realist or Positivist\) assumes that knowledge is  
totally objective, can be understood by scientific observations, and is an exact  
“picture of reality” without any distortions \(Barbour 1974\:33-35\). This position  
denies that the cultural or historical contexts affect the way people view reality since  
their view of reality is “the way it is” for all people everywhere. This epistemology  
was the underlying view for most of modem science and modem mission, often  
resulting in arrogance and ethnocentrism. Without knowing it, Joe will likely tend to  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 151  
2\. 3. fall in this category. It can be likened to the person who declares to the optometrist,  
“I don’t need glasses; I can see perfectly.”  
The second epistemological position is Critical Realism, which assumes that  
knowledge is both objective and subjective. There is an objective reality that can be  
observed and understood; however, our understanding of it is not complete due to  
our limitations of cultural and historical contexts. Barbour notes that valid theories  
are true and useful, but they “are abstract symbol systems which inadequately  
represent particular aspects of the world for specific purposes” \(Barbour 1974\:37\).  
“They are partial and inadequate ways of imaging what is not observable” \(Barbour  
1974\:48\). This leads to a confidence in one’s view of reality that is tempered with a  
humility that is willing to learn. Joe is beginning to enter this category. He  
understands some of the aspects of the “wings of God” from his own culture, but  
other aspects are obscure. He is limited by his own culture, and Builsa culture is  
helping him to understand this symbolism better. The critical realist view can be  
likened to the person who declares to the optometrist, “I can see well most of the  
time but I need glasses to see the blurry objects and to know what I am missing!”  
Subjective realism \(called Instrumentalist\), the third position, assumes that  
knowledge is totally subjective since it is created by culture. This view holds that one  
cannot know any exact truth since models “are neither true nor false, but only more  
or less useful mental devices” \(Barbour 1974\:36-37\). Religion is considered to be a  
“useful fiction” in that it is “a mental construct used instrumentally for particular  
purposes but not assumed to be either true or false” \(Barbour 1974\:38\). In reaction to  
modernity and the Critical Realist view above, postmodernism builds on this view of  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 152  
reality. It can be likened to the person who says to the optometrist, “Of course I need  
glasses - everyone has them. Can I try the orange tinted lenses for awhile, please?”  
These epistemologies are used by believers to shape their view of both Scripture and  
culture. A continuum can be plotted that shows common views of Scripture \(Figure 10\).  
The degree of realism described above can be plotted on a horizontal scale with the far  
right indicating Naive Realism \(position 1 above\) and the far left indicating  
Instrumentalism \(position 3 above\). The Critical Realist position is in the middle of these  
two with an entire continuum of possible responses in between.  
Instrumentalism Critical Realism Naive Realism  
*Degree o f Realism*  
# Figure 10. Epistemology of Scripture  
At the bottom of the realism scale \(far left in the diagram above\), the  
Instrumentalist view of Scripture affirms the validity of other religious texts on par with  
the biblical text. They may argue that the biblical text is unclear, only partial, not fully  
relevant, and certainly not inerrant. In this view, Scripture is critiqued \(but Scripture does  
not critique culture\). While this position is not tenable in evangelical theology, well  
meaning Christians may act this way when they regard the Scripture as irrelevant to  
certain areas of life. At the other extreme of the realism scale \(far right on the diagram\),  
a Naive Realist view of Scripture claims that all of Scripture is clearly understood and  
normative for today. Their understanding of Scripture is therefore the ultimate interpreter  
of culture and practice. There is no openness to learning from other cultures who are  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. reading their mother tongue Scripture since the truth of Scripture has already been  
essentially uncovered. In this view, one’s understanding of Scripture is not critiqued \(but  
this understanding of Scripture critiques culture\). In its extreme form, this led to  
missionary arrogance and paternalism. The Critical Realist view of Scripture rests  
between these two extremes. This view acknowledges that Scripture is the true infallible  
word of God that can be known \(like the Naive Realist\). It also acknowledges the human  
limitation of this truth, such that our understanding is not yet complete \(like the  
Instrumentalist\). Both views are held in tension to allow the Scripture to be more fully  
understood and relevant.  
Joe is shifting his epistemological position from Naive Realism to Critical  
Realism. As a result, he is still holding onto Scripture as the infallible word of God, while  
he is open to learning aspects of Scripture from the Builsa culture that his own culture  
overlooked. This is a huge step forward that will aid Joe in facilitating contextual  
theology, as will be described later.  
The view of culture \(Figure 11 on the next page\) has also varied across the  
continuum of the epistemologies described above. The degree of realism can be plotted  
on a vertical scale with the top indicating Naive Realism \(position 1 above\) and the  
bottom indicating Instrumentalism \(position 3 above\). The Critical Realist position is  
again in the middle of these two poles with an entire continuum of possible responses in  
between.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 154  
Naive Realism  
# a  
# \$  
# ' o '  
# I  
# Q  
Critical Realism  
Instrumentalism  
# Figure 11. Epistemology of Culture  
At the top of the realism scale, a Naive Realist view of culture holds that culture  
itself provides a snapshot of how life should operate; therefore, culture dictates which  
practices and theology are regarded as valid expressions of God. In this view, the culture  
is uncitically accepted. This leads to ethnocentrism - our cultural way is the only  
acceptable way. At the bottom of the scale, the Instrumentalist view holds that culture is  
totally subjective - neither true nor false. As a result, culture can be modified at will, or it  
can be ignored \(resulting in isolation\) - what matters is if it works. That is the criterion  
upon which culture is critiqued. Joe may tend toward this perspective on Builsa culture  
since he may feel that Builsa culture is hindering the way the church should work;  
therefore, he can ignore the Builsa culture and modify what he perceives needs changing.  
At the middle of the realism scale, a Critical Realist view recognizes that culture provides  
a pattern for reality for the people; however, culture also has some blind spots and  
weaknesses that prevent people from truly seeing reality. E. T. Hall states, “Culture hides  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 155  
much more than it reveals, and strangely enough what it hides, it hides most effectively  
from its own participants” \(1959\:29-30\). Both the image of God and the stain of sin are  
evident in culture. A Critical Realist view of culture more readily sees and understands  
this paradox.  
Joe started at the Instrumentalist position of Builsa culture. He may have felt that  
the Builsa culture can be ignored since it was hindering the proper communication and  
expression of Christianity. In other words, the Builsa culture was not working with the  
church but against it. As a result, Builsa culture can be changed or ignored, he reasoned.  
Gradually, Joe came to realize that Builsa culture has much to offer him in his journey to  
understand God. Their vibrant worship with drums and dancing helped him see aspects of  
worship that he missed before. The Builsa culture even helps him to understand aspects  
of God that he missed before, such as the metaphor “hiding under the wings of God.”  
Gradually, Joe moves to a critical realist view of culture. He realizes that his own culture  
has valid aspects of the image of God but it also overlooks important aspects of God that  
the Builsa culture can reveal. Again, the critical realist view of culture will help Joe as he  
strives to promote contextualization. When both the epistemologies of Scripture and  
culture are combined, then this interaction provides a helpful framework upon which to  
engage Scripture with culture - but that is to get ahead of ourselves \(this will be further  
developed in Chapter 6\). For now, Joe is gaining valuable insight into Scripture and  
Builsa culture as he adopts a critical realist position. Let us now return to Joe’s meeting  
with the church leaders to see what other discoveries he is making.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 156  
When a Man Loves a Woman...  
*A visiting pastor from Kenya shared the following Meru proverb with the group\:*  
*“When a man is in love, he doesn ’t count how long and steep the road is to his*  
*fiancee’s house. ” He recalled how he walked a long and dangerous path to visit the*  
*home o f his fiancee but he did not even consider staying home - his love compelled him to*  
*go. As he shared, smiles came across many faces as they began to picture their own*  
*journeys to visit their fiancees. Since the Builsa are an exogamous people, each o f these*  
*men had to walk a long way to visit their fiancees. This was something new to Joe. He*  
*knew that the Builsa were polygamous but he did not realize the taboos against marrying*  
*within the clan and the resulting difficulties offinding a wife from a place fa r away. Joe*  
*asked the church leaders to tell him more.*  
*David explained, “We often display love through actions instead o f words. We*  
*show love instead o f just speak wards o f love. The tangible expression is more*  
*meaningful than words that can easily be spoken but not sincerely felt. ” David could see*  
*that Joe was still not clear but that he was trying hard to grasp it.*  
*“It is like this, ” Immanuel explained, “When a friend is sick, we go visit them to*  
*show them that we care. When there is a funeral, we go visit and greet the family to*  
*express our sympathies. In the same way, to show a young girl that we love her, we will*  
*walk the long distance to her house and visit her and her family. That is what I mean by*  
*we ‘show ’ love instead o f ‘speak ’ love. ”*  
*Joe was catching on and he was pondering his own situation. He often said to his*  
*wife the words, “I love you,* ” *and he felt that was the best way to express his love fo r her.*  
*Perhaps tangible expressions may be a more powerful way to communicate love. As the*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 157  
*group analyzed the proverb and its cultural meaning, they reflected on the love o f God*  
*expressed in a Builsa manner. Just as Builsa often show love through actions and not*  
*words alone, God also showed his love through his actions. ‘But God demonstrates his*  
*own love fo r us in this\: While we were still sinners, Christ diedfor us” \(Romans 5\:8,*  
*NIV\).*  
*The group returned to the imagery o f the initial proverb, ‘‘When a man is in love,*  
*he doesn’t count how long and steep the road is to his fiancee’s home. ” Pastor Kofi*  
*noted,* "Naawen *is in love with his fiancee too. His fiancee is the church. Because o f this*  
*intense love fo r his bride-to-be, he did not count how long or steep the road was from*  
*heaven to earth. He knew the suffering o f the cross and the rejection by his own people*  
*that awaited him on that road. Yet, he did not consider the option o f staying home since*  
*his love compelled him.* Naawen *did not just speak his love fo r us. He showed us his love*  
*by coming to earth in the form o f Yezu to visit the home o f his fiancee - the church. ”*  
*Joe was speechless. He began to understand God’s intimate and compelling love*  
*in a way that he never considered before. The other group participants mentioned the*  
*same thing, even the one who initially offered the proverb fo r discussion. The Meru*  
*pastor was so excited about this theological understanding that he exclaimed, “I can’t*  
*wait to return to Kenya soon fo r an evangelistic ministry. I will use this proverb to*  
*express Christianity in a way that is African and Christian. This would surely be “sweet*  
*talk” in the Meru culture. ” It had already become sweet talk to the Builsa - and to Joe.*  
*This pastor did return to his home, but he did not forget the discussion o f*  
*proverbs and Scripture. He wrote back to share how 600 people came to Christ over the*  
*summer. He explained, “I used many proverbs at home, and the people became very*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 158  
*excited when I told them the proverbs. The young people were taught in the past to leave*  
*the old things behind, but the messages were very foreign. Now, they see me as a wise*  
*man and I speak wisdom in a way they can understand. ”*  
*By using the proverbs as a vehicle to convey the love o f God, the Christian*  
*message is not regarded as foreign - it is African. It is wisdom from Africa andfrom God.*  
*It is good news in Africa, and it is worth getting excited about! Joe was excited about this*  
*as well. He was considering how he could use proverbs in his evangelistic ministry. He*  
*observed how some Builsa perceive God as being distant, cold, and uninterested. For*  
*those people, perhaps he could help them to recall the love and anticipation that a*  
*groom-to-be has fo r his fiance. This compelling love knows no bounds - the man is*  
*willing to climb any steep or long road to give himself up fo r her \(Ephesians 5\:25\). This*  
*picture captures the heart o f our God for the Builsa people. Joe was starting to think and*  
*communicate more like a Builsa now. Though it was slow and required patience, he was*  
*getting closer to catching the monkey’s tail. There was more for him to learn, though,*  
*and he was eager to do so.*  
*Joe’s reflection was interrupted as several church ladies entered the room.*  
*“We hear that you are talking about things that are important to us, ” one lady*  
*spoke up, “and we would like to listen as well. ”*  
*The men looked at each other and smiled. “You are welcome to join us. We are*  
*discussing proverbs and can use your help to understand them.* Gandiuk yeng kan kaw  
bangga *\(One giant cannot kill a lizard alone\). ”*  
*The ladies all laughed and joined the discussion group. Joe had heard a proverb*  
*the other day that he wrote down. He thought o f several possible meanings but he wanted*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **1 59**  
*to hear more o f the cultural backgroundfrom the Builsa to clarify it fo r him. The proverb*  
*was, “Ba kan de tiim ale nipowa ” \(They do not eat medicine with the wife\). He shared*  
*this with the group to get their responses.*  
*The church leaders were slow to respond. Immanuel spoke up, “Maybe we should*  
*not talk about that proverb. L et’s try another one instead. ”*  
*Joe had the hunch that there was something beneath this proverb that revealed*  
*some deeper aspects o f Builsa culture. It would be easy to skip over these topics but that*  
*would only relegate these issues to the “excluded middle. ’’ Pastor David seemed to feel*  
*the same way, “I think we should discuss this, particularly since the ladies are here to*  
*help us. ”*  
*David continued, “The word* tiim *is more than just medicine. It is used to refer to*  
*the black medicine that gives you power. It is really your power source. This proverb is*  
*saying that you should not let your wife see your* tiim; *otherwise, she will see your power*  
*source and use it against you someday. ’’*  
*The others agreed. Joe was confused. He knew many o f the men and their wives.*  
*He did not know that there was such mistrust between husbands and wives. Joe asked,*  
*“Why would your wife use your* tiim *against you? ”*  
*Immanuel responded, “You may think that everything is* magsi *with your wife, but*  
*then when a conflict comes, she will side with her parents or she will destroy your*  
*medicine to humble you. ” Immanuel hesitated and then directed a question to the Builsa*  
*church leaders, “Should I tell him the story about the egg? ’’ Everyone chuckled. It was a*  
*well known story that has circulated all throughout the Builsa area with minor*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 160  
*variations. Everyone knew the story and it was indisputable proof that the proverb was*  
*true. In fact, the story and the proverb seemed to go together.*  
*Immanuel started the story, “A young man got married. He trusted his wife and*  
*felt that she loved him tremendously. Shortly after the wedding, his father warned him,*  
*‘Son, do not tell all o f your secrets to your new wife. She may seem nice now, but, on the*  
*day o f conflict, she will use your secrets to destroy you. ’ The young man protested and*  
*insisted that his father was wrong. Surely, his wife was different. ”*  
*The ladies in the room wore nervous smiles on their faces. They knew where the*  
*story was heading, just like the men did. Immanuel continued, “The father asked his son*  
*to do a simple test. ‘Take this egg and give it to your new wife,* ’ *the father advised his*  
*son. ‘Tell her that your power source is in this egg - it is your* tiim. *As long as this egg is*  
*safe, then your power will be safe. I f the egg breaks, however, then your life will end. Tell*  
*your wife that you trust her and you want her to keep the egg in a safe place.* ’ *The son*  
*obeyed his father’s advise. ’’*  
*Joe was confused but he knew that a point was coming so he held his tongue.*  
*Immanuel continued, “After a year or so o f marriage, the husband and wife got into a*  
*quarrel. They were angry and shouting at each other. The wife ran into her room and*  
*pulled out the egg fo r the man to see.*  
*‘You think you are so strong. I am going to drop this egg and humble you right*  
*now, ’ the wife said.*  
*‘No, please do not do that - 1 will die if you do!* ” *begged the husband.*  
*Ignoring his plea, the wife slammed the egg on the ground where it smashed to*  
*pieces. They each waited fo r the young man to die.*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 161  
*Now the father entered the room. He heard the whole discussion and entered the*  
*room just after the egg was dropped. The father advised the son, ‘You see, that is why our*  
*elders have said,* Ba kan de tiim ale nipowa. *On the day o f conflict, she will destroy you if*  
*she can. That egg was not really your* tiim. *1 just pretended this to be your* tiim *to make*  
*the point - do not show her your real* tiim *or you will regret it. ”*  
*The room erupted with laughter. Joe was learning aspects o f Builsa marriage and*  
*power sources that he had not uncovered before. Now, this proverb was a window to*  
*open up critical aspects o f Builsa culture. Once the window was opened, it would be hard*  
*to shut again.*  
*Joe asked, “Are ALL Builsa women like this or is it just some ladies that are like*  
*this?”*  
*It was the women’s turn to speak. One woman quickly responded, “Yes, we are*  
*ALL like this. ”*  
*The men laughed as if to say, “It is so true that even the women can admit it. ”*  
*Some o f the women protested. An older women stood up slowly and responded*  
*with emotion, “Some ladies are like this and some are not, just like there are some good*  
*men and some bad ones. Do not let the bad ladies convince you that all ladies are the*

*\[all are\] monkeys\). Let me prove to you that ladies are different.*  
*A man had three wives. One day, the youngest wife said to him,* 7 *am more*  
*beautiful than the other wives and I love you more than the others. Please send the other*  
*wives away. ’*

*The husband replied, ‘Let me think about this. 'After he thought awhile, he*

*The youngest wife quickly replied,* 7 *am still young and beautiful, while my*

*in or should I go to your husband?* ’  
*She hesitated a little, but then replied, ‘I am still young and have a lot left to do.*  
*Please do not enter - go to my husband instead. ’*  
*The husband finally arrived at the door o f the oldest wife, ‘Knock, knock. I am*  
*DEA TH. Should I come in or should I go to your husband? ’*  
*The old women did not hesitate, ‘You should enter and take me. That way, my*

*The ladies in the room erupted in applause fo r the storyteller. She proved the*
