*Joe had been following the intriguing mixture o f stories with proverbs intently. He*  
*now asked the ladies, “Are all men the same? ’’*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 163  
*A young lady added, “No. Some men will sit and discuss their problems with you,*  
*while others will not discuss anything with you - they just discuss things with their male*  
*friends. ”*  
*An older lady replied, “Some men bring other wives in the house. I f they are fe d*  
*up with the first wife, then they will not take good care o f her. This drives her away.* ”  
*A light went on fo r Immanuel. Now it was starting to make sense. He remembered*  
*the discussions they had previously about digging down to the roots. Atfirst, he wanted*  
*to ignore this proverb, but now they were digging to the roots o f the proverb, which*  
*revealed some critical issues in Builsa culture. There were some deep roots that have*  
*never been discussed in the church. Men are afraid that the wives will gossip, they will*  
*run to another man, or they will leave and go home to their parents. Women fear that the*  
*men will bring home another wife and neglect them, they will not discuss close things*  
*with them, and they will keep secrets from them. This can breed mistrust, disunity,*  
*fighting, and separation. These are some roots that need to be addressed by Scripture.*  
*Immanuel could not wait to jump into the discussion, “The elders say,* Ku  
paalimu po, ku gbeli gbeli; Ku kpagsa po, ba maa worn’ *\(The initial stages o f marriage*  
*are pleasant, but as time goes on people hear you \[fighting or quarrelling\]\). We have*  
*been discussing some reasons that this proverb is true, haven’t we? What does* Naween  
Wani *have to say about these reasons? Can it help us here? ”*  
*Pastors David and Kofi both smiled. They also recalled the previous discussions*  
*they had, and they saw that Immanuel was catching on. They remembered digging to the*  
*roots o f a proverb and then engaging these issues with* Naawen Wani. *They could do this*  
*with this proverb as well.*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 164  
“Naawen Wani *\(Ephesians 5\) says that a husband and wife should become one,* ”  
*explained David, “By keeping secrets from each other, we are not allowing our*  
*marriages to be what* Naawen *wants them to be. Instead o f becoming one, we are*  
*remaining two. This root o f the proverb* Naawen Wani *rejects. ”*  
*One o f the older women added, “Yes, we want to become one with our husbands*  
*but they need to sit and talk with us so that they do not hide things from us. Doesn ’t*  
Naawen Wani *say that the husbands should love the wife as he loves himself? ”*  
*Pastor Kofi jumped in, “At times, we are afraid to reveal all o f our secrets since*  
*we fear that they will be told to others. Do you remember how Delilah told Sampson’s*  
*secrets? She promised to keep the secrets but she repeatedly passed them on to his*  
*enemies. In the end, it destroyed him. ”*  
*“I think that is why our wives are told in* Naawen Wani *\(I Timothy 3\:11\) that they*  
*not become gossips, but they should have self-control and speak the truth, ” added Pastor*  
*David. “I f this occurred in the marriage, then it would be easier fo r the husbands to*  
*reveal secrets and not look fo r other women either. ”*  
*A big round o f applause was then given since everyone knew that this was the way*  
*things should be. Immanuel now asked, “Are there any aspects o f this proverb about not*  
*eating* tiim *with the wife that* Naawen Wani *affirms as* magsi *?* ”  
*There was a short silence as the people were reflecting on the proverb and*  
Naawen Wani. *Finally, Pastor David shared, “Going back to Delilah’s case, she was a*  
*nonbeliever. Sampson was warned not to be one with a nonbeliever but he did anyway.*  
*We are also warned in* Naawen Wani *not to marry nonbelievers. Sampson shows us the*  
*consequences o f rejecting this advice and marrying a nonbeliever. ’’*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 165  
*The pastors all shook their heads in agreement. This was a problem fo r the*  
*church, even fo r young male pastors who are not married. Sampson provided a good*  
*concrete example fo r them all.*  
*Joe had been pondering this proverb ever since he heard it. He now added,*  
*‘‘There are times when I am counseling someone from the church. The issues are*  
*sensitive and I do not want my wife to know others ’problems; otherwise, she will think*  
*negatively about this person and it will hurt her relationship with them. People also trust*  
*me not to tell their problems to others. I think that this part o f the proverb* Naawen Wani  
*would say is* magsi *here. ”*  
*Pastor David’s face lit up, “I agree. I f I tell my wife how others treated me in the*  
*church ministry, then it often bothers her more than me. She carries it fo r a long time and*  
*it does not help her or the situation. I think that some o f these secrets o f others in the*  
*church should not be told to our wives. ”*  
*Pastor Kofi agreed that it made good sense. He knew that God’s work was hard*  
*at times, and people do not always treat pastors kindly. This proverb serves as a good*  
*reminder about using wisdom concerning when to reveal hardships with your wife.*  
*Sometimes, it is better fo r her sake that you do not reveal the negative side o f others who*  
*have mistreated you. That is helpful wisdom to prayerfully consider fo r ministry.*  
*Joe learned a lot that day about Builsa culture and Scripture. By the end, he was*  
*exhausted. He needed time to reflect on what was discussed about Builsa marriages,*  
*oneness, trust, and confidentiality. Even though he felt emotionally spent, he could not*  
*wait fo r the next meeting to discuss proverbs and Scripture. He knew that more*  
*discoveries awaited them.*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. As the meeting closes, let us reflect on what Joe was experiencing. Figure 7 \(see  
Chapter 2\) illustrates how Joe was looking through the window of proverbs to see aspects  
of Builsa culture that he had not seen before. It should be noted that various genres reveal  
aspects that other genres cannot fully explain. Note how, several times, a story was given  
to explain the meaning of a proverb. The proverb can only go so far and then the story  
helps to explain aspects that the proverb cannot reveal. In the same way, a proverb can  
often be the summation of the story. In this way, the story can be more readily retrieved  
and reflected upon by remembering the proverb. The proverb about eating medicine with  
the wife brings to mind the story about the egg. In my travels throughout the Builsa area,  
I heard this same story over and over, with minor variations. As Figure 3 \(see Chapter 2\)  
illustrates, each of these genres are important in order to understand the Builsa culture  
and religion.  
While proverbs are a window for Joe to look from the outside and see into Builsa  
culture, they also become a mirror for Builsa to see their own culture. The Builsa, like  
any other people, do not usually critically reflect on their culture - they merely drink it in  
with their mothers’ milk from birth. As a result, there are attitudes that are never  
challenged, just simply assumed. The attitude about marriage described above is one  
example. As Figure 8 \(see Chapter 2\) shows, the discussion of the proverb then helps to  
reflect back to the Builsa aspects of their own culture that they normally do not observe.  
When this occurs, then they can use Scripture to critique those attitudes. This fosters  
critical contextualization in Builsa culture. With Joe as an outsider sitting in on the  
discussions, the proverbs then become both a window for him and he observes them  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 167  
functioning as a mirror for the Builsa. This creates a situation where Joe is poised to learn  
various aspects of Builsa culture and Scripture, which is what occurred for Joe here.  
This points out the benefits of having both the etic and emic perspectives in  
contextualization. The Builsa can provide insight into Builsa culture that Joe would not  
understand. Joe can only gain this perspective as he listens and observes the Builsa, and  
this is crucial for contextualization. The Builsa culture must be understood at the deeper  
worldview level in order for Scripture to engage it on more than a surface level. On the  
other hand, Joe provides an etic perspective that is also valuable. He can step back from  
the Builsa context and ask questions/make observations about Builsa marriages that the  
Builsa themselves are too close to the situation to ask/make. He also brings the  
experience of counseling church members from another context that provided some  
helpful wisdom for the Builsa. Both the emic and etic perspectives benefit from each  
other.  
Note also the benefit derived from having both gender perspectives. While the  
pastors in the Builsa churches are largely men, the women’s perspectives on marriage  
opened areas of discussion that would likely not have been heard if the women were not  
present. This stimulated deeper reflection for the men \(including Joe\), resulting in a  
greater understanding of both Scripture and culture. It may also stimulate the  
development of women in the church as they enter the theological dialogue with the  
Builsa men. Both gender perspectives can help move the contextualization process  
forward by touching wider issues than one gender alone would address.  
We should also consider how the proverbs touch both the emotions and provide  
instruction at the same time. Note how the church leaders enjoyed the discussion about  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 168  
the proverb concerning visiting the fiancee’s house. It touched a soft spot for each of  
them. At the same time, they understood God’s intimate love for His people. This is an  
example of Turner’s bipolarization of symbols. Proverbs, functioning as symbols, can  
serve as bridges between the sensory and ideological poles. While some have studied  
proverbs for their aesthetic value and others have studied them for their didactic value,  
Turner’s bipolarization indicates that proverbs actually function as bridges between the  
two. This makes proverbs potent tools for contextualization, as noted by the discussions  
above. Proverbs can also be effective in reaching nonbelievers since they touch both the  
sensory and ideology poles at once. This is evident by Joe’s next encounter with a  
Muslim man who has had questions about Christianity.  
The Feet Must GO  
*Joe was visiting another village one day when he ran into a friend that he had*  
*known fo r some time. Upon seeing Joe, Ali runs over and gives him a big hug. Putting his*  
*arm around Jo e’s waist, Ali says, “You have come back! I knew that you would not forget*  
*me.*  
*Joe responded, “Forget you? How could I do that? I have sent messages to you*  
*but it is always better to come see you in person. The elders say,* ‘Tuntoming a yok ka  
nansa; ka kan yok suiya ’ *\(To send a message by someone cools the legs; it does not cool*  
*the heart\). Ali, laughing and smiling at Joe’s response, launches into the greetings o f*  
*asking about Jo e’s family, health, etc. Joe returns the greetings. After awhile, Ali*  
*\(knowing Jo e’s interest in proverbs\) says, “We have a proverb in Hausa that says,*  
*“What the heart loves, there the legs will go.* ”  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **16 9**  
*Joe smiles and begins to ponder the meaning. He looks to Ali fo r help.*  
*“Think about it, ” Ali urges. Ali knows that a lot o f the enjoyment with proverbs is*  
*in discovering the meaning. He shouldn't tell the meaning too fast and spoil the*  
*challenge fo r Joe. Ali helps out a little, “What the heart wants to do and where it wants*  
*to go, it tells the feet to move and the feet obey. ”*  
*Joe catches on, “Yes, I see. The heart leads the way and the body then follows. ”*  
*“Mmm. Have you ever seen the heart go somewhere that it did not want to go but*  
*it had to listen to the feet? ” Ali asks rhetorically.*  
*Joe shakes his head, “No. ”*  
*Ali \(with a smug look o f satisfaction as if Joe hadjust proved the validity o f this*  
*proverb without a shadow o f a doubt\), “You see. What the heart loves, there the legs will*  
*go!”*  
*Joe, laughing and enjoying the entire process o f bantering and negotiating*  
*meaning between two people from two different worlds responds, “Now I understand.*  
*The feelings o f the heart are so strong that they pull the feet to go where they may not*  
*otherwise want to go. ”*  
*Ali has a look o f total satisfaction, as if to say, “I told you that my worldview is*  
*correct. I am glad that you are now understanding me and seeing things my way. ’’*  
*Joe continues, “It is interesting how the heart pulls so strongly that it forces*  
*people to go to fa r away places. I think that God’s heart is like that as well. ”*  
*A li’s curiosity was now aroused. Joe adds, “God loves people since he created*  
*them. He made them to be with him and he loves them. His heart pulled so strong that his*  
*feet had to come to earth and be with them.*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 170  
*Ali, “You mean that he wanted to move among them? ”*  
*Joe was pleasantly surprised by A li’s choice o f words since Jesus is perhaps best*  
*described as Immanuel, meaning “God among us. ”*  
*“Yes, ” Joe affirms, “that is why Jesus came to earth. God’s heart pulled so*  
*strong that his feet had to come among us. Jesus was the feet o f God! ”*  
*Ali was listening intently. Joe could see that he also enjoyed the friendly banter.*  
*He could also see that Ali was seriously pondering what was said. Joe and others had*  
*spoken to him before about faith in Jesus, Jesus ’ life/death/resurrection, etc. As a*  
*Muslim, Ali agreed that Jesus was a good person but he did not understand why Jesus*  
*was necessary. Why would God want to come to earth anyway? Ali felt that God created*  
*everything and was now fa r removedfrom daily events. He understood God as the*  
*ultimate judge but he had not considered before how God’s heart may be touched by the*  
*people he created. It was a new thought to Ali, and it touched his own heart as well as*  
*gave him something to think about.*  
*While all o f A li’s questions were not answered that day, he was beginning to*  
*understand the ways and purposes o f God in terms and concepts that he could*  
*understand. After all, this explanation o f Jesus affirmed something that he already held to*  
*be true in his own worldview. It described the meaning o f Jesus in terms and metaphors*  
*that were uniquely African and also fully Christian. In the past, Christianity was*  
*presented to Ali in ways that were foreign. By using African metaphors and concepts*  
*contained in A li’s own proverbs, Jesus ’ coming to earth started to make sense and was*  
*congruent with some o f the deeply held core values o f A li’s culture.*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 171  
*“There is a God whose heart pulls so strong that his feet must come to be with*  
*us, ” Ali thought, “That is good news. I would like to know more about this. ” That was*  
*also sweet talk to Ali... and Joe.*  
Before continuing, let us reflect on what transpired with Joe and Ali. They come  
from two different worldviews; hence they have different concepts of reality. Their inner  
concepts of reality have been shaped by their own cultures. Symbols, such as words, are  
used to describe this inner view of reality. For example, take the concept of God.  
According to Pierce’s triadic view of symbols, the word “GOD” \(Sign\) is used to connect  
the mental image \(Signification\) of the transcendent being to the external reality  
\(Signified\) of this transcendent being. Through the communication process, the symbols  
help to construct reality for people. As Figure 6 \(see Chapter 2\) shows, this process is not  
exact since people have different inner concepts when using symbols to discuss the same  
external reality. In this figure, we see that there is an external house that is built \(at the  
top\) and the plan sheets are used to discuss it between parties \(these are the symbols\), but  
the inner concept in each person’s mind of the way the house looks is slightly different  
from each other as well as from the external reality.  
In a cross-cultural situation, like Joe’s, this becomes even more difficult. While  
discussing the same external reality of God using the same symbol \(the word “God”\),  
they both have in their minds different inner realities. Ali sees God as far removed and  
the ultimate judge. Joe sees God as intimate and desiring to be in a close relationship with  
people.  
The constitutive model of communication reminds us that reality is continually  
constructed, reconstructed, and negotiated via the communication process. As a result,  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 172  
the inner meanings can change. In this instance, Joe uses a proverb to help reconstruct  
Ali’s view of God. Functioning as a symbol, this proverb is particularly powerful since  
Ali already affirms its value. As a matter of fact, Ali was the one that brought this  
proverb to Joe in the first place. This proverb then helps Ali to understand the external  
reality of God in a way that alters his inner view of reality of God.  
The proverb is also potent since it touches both Ali’s senses and ideology. Note  
how Ali enjoys the process of bantering as well as the negotiating of meaning. In a way,  
the truth of the proverb “softens up” Ali since the proverb prepared his heart to receive  
the message. In the past, others witnessed to Ali but his defenses were up and he rebuffed  
the message as not understandable or simply irrelevant. By allowing the proverb to pave  
the way first, the hard ground of his heart was plowed with the sweetness of the proverb.  
Once this occurred, his prepared heart was like plowed ground that was more open to  
receiving the seeds of the Word. I have seen the use of proverbs in evangelism do the  
same thing in other occasions \(Moon 2004b\: 163-164\).  
In addition to evangelism, proverbs can be very helpful in counseling and  
discipleship. To see this fleshed out, let us join Joe again as he learns to “paint with  
proverbs,” and then he ministers to Immanuel in the midst of tragedy.  
Cold Water on a Hot Fire  
*Joe was learning how to speak with sweet talk. He realized that learning to use*  
*oral art is more like learning to paint than it was a class in school...and he was enjoying*  
*it. As he visited Pastor K ofi’s house this day, he reminded himself o f how much he*  
*learned. Joe wanted to learn more.*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 173  
*Kofi shared with Joe, “I used to think that our own proverbs were not very*  
*valuable and were just something that we didfor fun. We had the proverbs in our own*  
*hands fo r a long time but did not realize their value in helping to communicate* Naawen  
Wani *in the Builsa area. Now, I realize how important they are and how* Naawen *himself*  
*gave wisdom to our ancestors to help us understand* Naawen Wani. ”  
*Joe was beginning to appreciate this as well. God was here in Builsa culture long*  
*before the first missionary arrived. God created humans in his image and the image of*  
*God still exists in Builsa culture, like all cultures. Like all cultures, as well, the stain of*  
*humankind’s sin exists in Builsa culture. Scripture is the interpreter to show what aspects*  
*o f Builsa culture, including proverbs, reveal the image o f God and which reveal the stain*  
*o f humankind’s sin. Some proverbs can inspire and motivate Christians.*  
*“This proverb encourages me to do the right thing, ” shared Kofi. “During*  
*difficult times, it has helped me many times. Our elders say,* “Pukawgi a laa ka tuik po ”  
*\(A widow laughs at the spot where she pounds millet\).*  
*Joe was puzzled at first. How could this proverb inspire him so deeply? It was so*  
*short; perhaps that is why he was able to recall it so readily? Joe knew that millet was a*  
*major crop fo r the Builsa, and he saw women at the* tuik *each day pounding millet seeds*  
*into millet flour in order to make* saab *\(see Appendix E\). Joe understood the words but*  
*i*  
*not the meaning.*  
*Kofi could see that Joe was puzzled. Kofi enjoyed this proverb and he relished the*  
*opportunity to explain it to Joe. “ When a husband dies, then the oldest son is responsible*  
*to look after his mother who is now a widow, ” Kofi explained. “The son now has a very*  
*delicate problem to work out. How does he show love to his mother and take care o f her*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 174  
*in his house without making his wife jealous by feeling that she is not caredfor as well as*  
*before? ” Kofi added*  
*Joe had not considered that before. Relationships are crucial in* Buluk *since their*  
*very survival depends on them. The man has to find a way to give the appropriate amount*  
*o f food, time, affection, etc. to both his mother and his wife who all share the same house;*  
*otherwise, critical relationships will be damaged.*  
*Kofi picked a millet stalkfrom the house that had a fu ll head o f white millet on the*  
*outside. Joe could see that the millet seeds grew on the outside so they could easily be*  
*removedfrom the husk and roasted or ground into flour \(see the right millet stalk in*  
*Appendix B\). Kofi continued, “This millet is called* zaapiulik *or* gmankarik. *The man*  
*gives this millet to his wife and she is happy since she knows that she can make a good*  
*meal out o f this fo r the family. ” Kofi picked up another millet stalk that did not look like*  
*millet to Joe \(see the left millet husk in photo 1\). Joe couldn’t see the millet seeds since*  
*they grew inside the husk. Kofi continued, “At the same time, he gives* zaabuluk *to his*  
*mother. ’’* Zaabuluk *was millet that had to be poundedfirst to remove the husk, and then it*  
*could be pounded again into flour. At first glance, it looks like there is not much millet in*  
*the* Zaabuluk.  
*Kofi was smiling widely now. Joe knew the punchline was close so he hung in*  
*with K ofi’s long explanation. Kofi added, “When the man first gave the* zaabuluk *to his*  
*mother in the sight o f his wife, the wife felt like she got the better part o f the bargain. The*  
*wife's millet looked much fuller and she thought, ‘My husband still loves me since he*  
*gave me the better millet! ’ When the widowed mother finally took her* zaabuluk *to the* tuik  
*and pounded it, she realized that she had a larger bowl o f winnowed millet than the wife!*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 175  
*At the* tuik, *she laughed and laughed since she knew that her son still loved her- he*  
*actually gave her the larger amount o f millet. ”*  
*Joe laughed along with Joe. The man in the story had skillfully negotiated two*  
*very important relationships and he found a way to show love and concern to both*  
*without either one getting mad or jealous. In the end, the widow laughed since she*  
*received the greater amount o f millet to bless her amidst her suffering. Joe enjoyed the*  
*story but still wasn't sure o f how this encouraged K ofi’s faith.*  
*Kofi concluded, “This has encouraged me fo r a long time now. When I first*  
*followed* Yezu, *some people ridiculed me and did me harm. They were trying to push me*  
*away and I felt like a widow who was rejected. In the middle o f the persecution, this*  
*proverb reminded me that I will one day laugh again. Others may mean harm to me but*  
Naawen *will work it out fo r good in the end. Today, I am laughing with joy since* Naawen  
*has helped me.* Naawen *has become my millet fo r me. People used insults and difficulties*  
*to discourage me, but* Naawen *used those very difficulties to encourage me. He turned*  
*their insults into good. I have seen* Naawen *bless me and now I am laughing at the* tuik/ ”  
*Joe clapped with joy fo r Kofi. On the inside, his heart clappedfor Jesus. It was a*  
*wonderful testimony o f what Jesus could do fo r a believer in the midst o f trials and*  
*difficulties. Joe knew that many young believers face trials from their families, especially*  
*when they reject the traditional sacrifices to the ancestors. Those difficult “excluded*  
*level” issues would test their faith and resolve to the limit. Joe thanked God that this*  
*proverb helped Kofi hold onto the promise in i Peter 5\:12, “And the God o f all grace,*  
*who called you to his eternal glory in Christ, after you have suffered a little while, will*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 176  
*himself restore you and make you strong, firm and steadfast. ” One day, the widow will*  
*again laugh under the* tuik.  
*As Joe left to go home, he pondered K ofi’s proverb. He remembered other*  
*believers who suffered and he thought perhaps this proverb would encourage them as*  
*well.*  
*Then he remembered Immanuel.*  
*You may remember how Immanuel’s son recently died and now the village elders*

*Joe was praying fo r Immanuel andfelt it was time to go visit him. He called*

*By this time, several o f the local elders noticed the visitors and they also join them*

Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 177  
*share something that is meaningful to me. I also have children and I sympathize with*

*the load \[on the head\] knows how heavy it is\).*  
*The village elders laugh and repeat to each other,* “Jiib jeeroa a seba bu  
dobirim. ” *They are delighted to hear a white person speaking their own proverbs in their*  
*own language. It makes good sense as well. Only a person who is in the same situation*  
*can understand the difficulty involved, whether it is carrying a heavy load or watching a*  
*son die.*  
*Immanuel also smiles and chuckles. He is thinking about how* Naawen *knows his*  
*true feelings \(even the ones that he does not share with anyone else... ever\). Since*  
Naawen*’s own son died, Immanuel thought, he truly understands all o f this.*  
*Joe continued, “There is a proverb from our elders that helps me,* “Pukawgi a laa  
ka tuik po’ *\(A widow laughs at the spot where she pounds millet\). ”*  
*This starts a roar o f laughter. David is pleasantly surprised to hear this proverb*  
*coming from Joe. Every Builsa knows this proverb and the associated story, but how*  
*could Joe know this? Does he know what he is saying? David was stunned to hear what*  
*Joe had to say next.*  
*Excitedly, Joe continues, “Yes. We are like that widow at times. Hard things*  
*come, people close to us die, and we have no laughter. We may even think that* Naawen  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 178  
*has forgotten us. One day, Immanuel, you will go see your son in heaven. Then, you will*  
*laugh hard since you will see how he has been enjoying* Naawen*’s blessings all o f this*  
*time. The event that was hardfor you has become a blessing for him - even now he is*  
*laughing at the* tuik *in God’s house!* ”  
*All eyes intently focused on Joe and the laughter stopped. Now, the meaning was*  
*settling in like the harmatton dust that slowly blankets the land. Immanuel was pondering*  
*how his suffering today will result in blessing and laughter one day. Immanuel pauses to*  
*let it settle in a little more.*  
*Immanuel then responds, “It is true.* Pukawgi a laa ka tuik po. ” *One day, I will*  
*see my son and I will laugh again. I will thank* Naawen *as we laugh together at the* tuik.  
*Now I know why* Naawen *sent you to* Buluk *at this time, Joe. He sent you to help me now.*  
*Your words are like cold water that has put out a fire within me. I thank you very much. ”*  
*Pastor David and the elders all clapped and responded, “Thankyou very much.*  
*Thank you very much. ” As Joe responded, “You are welcome, ” he was amazed himself at*  
*what just happened. A few years ago, the Builsa culture and Buli language were so*  
*foreign to his experience. He felt that he would never be able to communicate beyond a*  
*child’s level. Furthermore, the culture was so different from his own culture that he*  
*constantly felt like a fish out o f water. Would he ever get beyond a mere scratching o f the*  
*surface and understand some o f the deeper worldview level issues in* Buluk? *These*  
*questions and doubts nagged Joe like a warm bed o f coals that could quickly fan into a*  
*flame.*  
*Today, he saw how a proverb opened a window into the soul o f Builsa culture that*  
*touched deep emotions and communicated powerful truth. While various aspects o f*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **1 79**  
*Builsa culture were still strange to Joe, he was amazed at what God did to help him*  
*minister inner healing to a suffering believer. Immanuel never said what the fire was - it*  
*could be anger, disappointment, raging questions, or even nagging doubts. Whatever the*  
*fire Immanuel carried in his soul, today it was put out by cold water. God had sent Joe to*  
*help a disciple o f Christ, and both were blessed in the process. Immanuel's fire was put*  
*out - and so was Joe’s.*  
Let us step back and analyze what Joe is beginning to learn. Cultures are hard to  
understand, especially for someone whose home culture is vastly different from his host  
culture. Joe, a Western missionary, is striving to be a good Builsa who can become good  
news to them. In order to do this, he attempts to learn the local language. Early on, he  
learns that learning language is more than just learning what Buli words correspond to an  
English words. Spradley \(1979\:17\) observed that language actually helps to construct  
reality, “Language is more than a means of communication about reality\: it is a tool for  
constructing reality. Different languages create and express different realities. They  
categorize experience in different ways. They provide alternative patterns for customary  
ways of thinking and proceeding.” Joe is starting to appreciate and learn the ways Builsa  
create and express reality.  
The proverbs become windows for him to see this reality from a Builsa  
perspective. As this unfolds for Joe, he can better understand Builsa core values and what  
it means to be a good Builsa. It may be slow work, but gradually he is catching the  
monkey’s tail.  
Through my field research, 639 Builsa proverbs were collected. From this  
collection, 365 were analyzed for the common values that emanate from this body of  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **1 80**  
wisdom. This information was stored in the Mother Tongue Oral Literature, Version 1.0  
database program. This facilitated the sorting and systematizing of the proverbs so that  
common themes could be discovered.  
It should be noted that not all of the Builsa theology and core values are contained  
within the proverbs. The proverbs contribute to an understanding of Builsa theology  
concerning God, human beings, good and evil. The Builsa theology related to the  
ancestors, shrines, and the spirit world is predominately derived from other sources.v Not  
withstanding this limitation, valuable information can be gleaned from the proverbs in  
order to help us understand Builsa primal religion.  
Each proverb was analyzed for its theme and the entire body of proverbs was  
analyzed in order to discover recurring themes. The number of times a particular theme  
was noted is shown below, along with the frequency of occurrence. This gives an  
indication of the most strongly felt values within the Builsa culture. The ten most  
common values are listed in Table 3 on the next page.  
The reader should not assume that the Builsa people always obey each of these  
core values, rather they should be seen as the ideal for the Builsa people to strive toward.  
It is a systematizing of the strongly held Builsa values. A careful analysis of the Builsa  
ten core values would greatly help Joe in understanding the Builsa theology and  
worldview. He can then know what Builsa value and consider to be the ideal Builsa  
person. This comes to the surface through the proverbs.  
Joe is learning the first core value “All people have problems; you need to learn  
how to deal with it.” Joe experiences his own problems with language learning as well as  
those around him \(e.g., Immanuel’s tragic loss of his son\), and he is learning how Builsa

cope with these difficulties. It is assumed that life in Builsa land is difficult and problems  
are a normal part of everyday life. One should just accept this fact and not complain or  
get upset about it. Instead, the people experiencing these problems need to learn how to  
manage with them. The theme that all people are suffering is exemplified by the proverb,  
*“Ngpteruk a kan kuri nipokyeng biiga”* \(the rain does not fall on one woman's child\).  
This means that the baby being carried on the back of her mother during a rain storm will  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 182  
get wet; however, she is not the only woman carrying a baby who will get wet in the rain  
storm. Many others would suffer the same problem as well.  
In order to cope with the problems of life, the proverbs encourage the people to use  
several strategies. Let us look at four coping strategies that occur in the proverbs  
themselves. *First, people are encouraged to be content with what they have since it could*  
*be worse than it is.* The following proverbs bear out this strategy\:

Since these Builsa values \(and accompanying strategies\) are most commonly  
expressed in the Builsa proverbs, one would expect this theme to also be portrayed in  
their songs. In an analysis of over one hundred Builsa Christian songs, the above theme is  
very prominent.VI These songs were composed by the Builsa people where this is the first  
generation of Christianity taking root in the Builsa culture. As a result, the songs give  
another excellent window into how Christian Builsa theology is developing as they think  
through how the Scripture interacts with traditional Builsa culture. Several of the songs  
deal with suffering and accepting the fact that others will insult Christians or drive them  
out of their houses. Christians need to accept this fact and bear with others in order to free  
them from their sins. The following song exemplifies this theme\:  
*Ba poom yiage, ba poom yiage.*  
*Ba poom yiage, Naam ga be.*  
*Nya, ba poom yiak ta koalima nyini ga jo Yeezu.*  
*Ba poom yiage, Naam ga be.*  
The translation is\:  
They have already driven us away, they have already driven us away.  
They have already driven us away, where is the Chieftancy?  
You see, since they have already driven us away, we take our possessions and go  
enter Jesus.  
They have already driven us away, where is the Chieftancy?  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 184  
Since the people have already driven us away from the house, the song implies that we  
should accept this as part of the cost of following Jesus. However, the song goes further  
than the traditional thought for it offers a new strategy to manage problems. We should  
now pack our things and enter into the presence of Jesus, who is our Chief, for shelter  
and protection.  
Secondly, another common way that the proverbs encourage Builsa to manage  
their problems is to *accept their limitations.* By accepting their limitations, they can deal  
with events and conditions that are out of their control. The following proverbs bear this  
theme\:

A third strategy for coping with problems that occurs in the proverbs is to *remain*  
*patient.* This theme is what helped the widow to endure the pain of widowhood. This is  
also helpful guidance for others, such as Kofi and Immanuel, to help them deal with their  
own suffering. This strategy is emphasized in the following proverbs\:

This strategy also emerges in the Christian songs, as exemplified by the following\:  
*Ba dan nak mi Yeezu nyinga, A Yeezu nyinga,*  
*Diila kan dom mi suiya.*  
*Ba dan le mi Yeezu nyinga, A Yeezu nyinga,*  
*Diila kan puuri mi suiya.*  
The translation is\:  
If they beat me because of Jesus, because of Jesus,  
This doesn’t pain my heart \[make my temper flare up\].  
If they insult me because of Jesus, because of Jesus,  
This will not make me angry.  
This song again encourages the believers to endure suffering such as beating and  
insults. This can be expected and managed with patience.  
In the fourth strategy for coping with problems given in the proverbs, one is  
encouraged to do things slowly or just a little at a time. The Buli word “*maga*” can mean  
both “slow” or “little.” By attempting to do things slowly or just little by little, then big  
problems can be managed. This was the coping strategy that was given to Joe for learning  
the Buli language. The following proverbs convey this theme\:

As shown above, the proverbs encourage Builsa to manage with their problems by\:  
1\. Being content with what they have - no matter how small,  
2\. Accepting their limitations,  
3\. Exercising patience, and  
4\. Doing things slowly or a little at a time.  
In addition, the following proverbs offer other miscellaneous advice\:

Joe is learning these core values through these proverbs. As a result, he can then  
incorporate them into his own life and ministry to others in the Builsa area. If he does  
not, then he is likely to make serious mistakes. Before Joe, as a Western missionary,  
arrives in the Builsa land, he will have his own cultural expectations of a “Good  
Westerner” and carry these with him. Due to his own ethnocentricity, he will assume that  
the values which made him a Good Westerner will also be the same ones that will make  
him a Good Builsa. Problems occur when the values from two different cultures are  
widely different. Take for example, some American values.  
Many have written about American cultural values \(Althen 1988; Lingenfelter  
and Mayers 1986; Stewart and Bennett 1991; Storti 1999; Wanning 1991\). Nussbaum  
\(1998\) took a unique approach by analyzing over 230 American proverbs and common  
sayings in American culture. Based on the values portrayed therein, he extracted the ten  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **1 8 9**  
most common values and called them the Ten Commandments of American culture, as  
summarized below\:  
1\. You can’t argue with success \(Be a success\).  
2\. Live and let live \(Be tolerant\)  
3\. Time flies when you’re having fun \(Have lots of fun\).  
4\. Shop till you drop  
5\. Just do it.  
6\. No pain, no gain \(Get tough. Don’t whine\).  
7\. Enough is enough \(Stand up for your rights\).  
8\. Time is money \(Don’t waste time\).  
9\. Rules are made to be broken \(Think for yourself\).  
10\. God helps those who help themselves \(work hard\).  
When comparing Nussbaum’s Ten Commandments of American culture with the  
above ten core values of Builsa culture, some major differences immediately come to the  
forefront. Compare the ten Builsa core values with the ten American commandments as  
follows\:  
1\. Instead of managing with problems since all people suffer \(Builsa value  
number one\), Americans feel that life should be fun \(American commandment number  
3\). Nussbaum \(1998\:2\) notes, “Having fun is the major preoccupation of youth, retired  
people, and many of those in between.”  
2\. Instead of knowing your role in the community \(Builsa value number 2\),  
Americans praise success above other things \(American commandment number one\) and  
they look at success as “*individualism*, freedom, goal-setting, progress, experimenting.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 190  
\[italics mine\] \(Nussbaum 1998\:1\). Nussbaum \(1998\:5\) notes, “When we Americans think  
of success, we naturally think first of individual success.”  
3\. Instead of being careful not to make mistakes to hurt others \(Builsa value  
number 3\), “Americans love freedom and privacy.. .we want to be left alone. We don’t  
want anyone interfering in our affairs, giving us advice, or trying to run our lives”  
\(Nussbaum 1998\:2\). As part of being tolerant \(American commandment number 2\),  
Americans are discouraged from interfering with other peoples’ lives.  
4\. Instead of accepting the unexplainable and unexpected as part of life \(Builsa  
value number 4\), American commandment number 7 says, “Stand up for your rights.”  
The Constitution of the U.S. says that everyone has the right to pursue happiness. If an  
unexplainable event is blocking this right, then you should stand up against it and get to  
the bottom of it. If every roof leaked in Builsa culture, they would be encouraged to  
accept it as part of life. If this same event occurred in America, it is likely that a  
government body would be expected to address it.  
5\. Both cultures agree that you need to “work hard” \(Builsa value number 5 and  
American commandment number 10\).  
6\. Instead of honoring those in authority \(Builsa value number 6\), Americans  
“... strongly dislike authority structures where people are expected to inform several other  
people before they do anything. We get an idea and we want to just do it” \(Nussbaum  
1998\:3\). As part of American commandment number 5, Americans are prone to act  
without taking time to collaborate or honor authority structures.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **191**  
7\. Instead of keeping his actions and character open to those close to him \(Builsa  
value number 7\), “Americans love freedom and privacy. ..We want people to...mind their  
own business” \(Nussbaum 1998\:2\) as part of American commandment number 2.  
8\. Instead of taking the time to find the underlying meaning behind things \(Builsa  
value 8\), American commandment 8 advises, “Don’t waste time... Wasting time is as bad  
a wasting money” \(Nussbaum 1998\:3\).  
9\. According to Nussbaum, Builsa value number 9 \(use what you have today  
since tomorrow is uncertain\) does not have a parallel in the top ten American values. This  
Builsa value, however, is similar to the American proverb, “A bird in the hand is worth  
two in the bush.”  
10\. An American missionary will trust that God is chief and wants to help \(Builsa  
value 10 and American commandment 10\), even though “For most Americans, God is  
much less a concern than success, money, and time” \(Nussbaum 1998\:4\).  
While this is neither a criticism on either the Builsa or American cultures, it does  
point out that the analysis of proverbs can help identify some areas likely to bring  
tensions due to conflicting cultural value systems. Knowing this, Joe can adjust some of  
his thinking and actions in order to be seen as a “Good Builsa;” otherwise, he will  
encounter resistance and not understand why. For example, an American may think that a  
good leader is one who is an independent thinker, action-oriented, “their own person,”  
and does not waste time before implementing their plans. This type of leadership will be  
greatly misunderstood in the Builsa area. The American will likely be seen as being too  
proud since they are breaking Builsa core values No. 2, 6, and 8! Unknowingly, he is  
conveying a message that he does not intend to convey. By analyzing the Builsa proverbs  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 192  
resulting in an understanding of the Builsa core values, Joe can recognize some areas that  
will create tension. In this way, he can adapt his ministry in order to reach the people.  
It should be noted here that any Christian worker who is not initially from the  
Builsa area would greatly benefit by the study of the Builsa proverbs. The example above  
was for a Western missionary; however, this equally applies to Christian workers from  
Southern Ghana, for instance, who come to work in the Builsa area. They are cross-  
cultural workers, just as Western missionaries are cross-cultural workers. Their danger in  
being misunderstood may be even greater since they may assume that since they are from  
Ghana, they understand how all Ghanaians think. They may not see the need to take the  
time to analyze the Builsa proverbs. In the end, this ministry among the Builsa people  
will be greatly hindered at best, and totally misunderstood at worst. It would be better to  
heed Abrahams \(1967\:184\) advice,  
the collection of proverbs is one of the first things one should do when entering  
into a strange community, for they can point to the sensitive spots in the social  
arrangement of the group and therefore place the observer in a position of great  
future advantage in trying to collect from and understand the members of that  
community.  
The above comparison between the Builsa values and the American  
commandments shows how a good American can be seen as a bad Builsa. Joe is learning  
to see the Builsa values through the window of their proverbs. As a result, he can adjust  
himself and begin acting as a good Builsa. This will be helpful to Joe as he becomes an  
important part of the process of contextualization in the Builsa church. Joe can provide an  
important etic perspective to help the Builsa church address “middle level” issues. This  
will be important as the church is confronted by serious issues dealing with the Builsa  
culture.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **193**  
Chapter Summary  
The serious analysis of proverbs has taught Joe much about Builsa culture and  
helped him to discover aspects of Scripture that he overlooked before. Joe has learned  
many lessons that include\:  
1\. While the value of oral literature is easily overlooked by missionaries entering  
oral cultures, it is abundant and provides invaluable insight into the reasoning processes  
of oral people.  
2\. The use of oral literature, like proverbs, is more an art form than a science. To  
learn the proper use of proverbs, it requires careful observation, questioning, a lot of  
practice, and the attitude of a learner. It helps to start the learning process by learning a  
few proverbs well, and know the proper context to use them appropriately.  
3\. When outsiders learn the proper use of proverbs, their speaking is more likely  
to be regarded as adult speech. In Buluk, *biik-sobilik* or *biik-wiani* is considered the type  
of speech that carries more weight among adult speakers. The proper use of proverbs will  
often touch both emotional and ideological senses of the Builsa audience.  
4\. Since Christian truth is greater than what any one culture alone can reveal,  
there are aspects of Scripture that missionaries can learn from Builsa culture that were  
overlooked before. Builsa proverbs can be a catalyst to help reveal some of these  
discoveries. These insights can be useful in the contextualization process.  
5\. A critical realist epistemology of both Builsa culture and Scripture can help  
facilitate contextualization. This epistemology encourages missionaries to hold firmly to  
the authority of Scripture, as well as recognize that there are aspects of Scripture that they  
do not understand yet.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **1 9 4**  
6\. Proverbs can be a window for missionaries to understand Builsa cultural  
values. When these are compared to the cultural values of the missionaries’ home culture,  
it can help identify areas likely to result in cultural conflict. The missionaries can then  
make adjustments in order to become good news to the people.  
7\. The analysis of proverbs can also lead to discussions and discoveries about  
hidden or deeper aspects of culture that are often overlooked. This can reveal root issues  
and “excluded middle”areas in the Builsa culture that are difficult to identify otherwise.  
For outsiders, the proverbs can serve as a window into these areas, while they can also  
serve as a mirror to Builsa as they reflect back to them aspects of Builsa culture that are  
seldom discussed openly.  
8\. Proverbs and stories are often interrelated. Each genre highlights and filters out  
aspects of Builsa culture that the other genre omits. By working together, these two  
genres complement each other well by providing a wider view of Builsa culture.  
9\. The proper use of proverbs can help missionaries in evangelism, discipleship,  
and counseling, as well as contextualization. The narrative provides examples of each.  
10\. Missionaries provide an etic perspective that is helpful in the contextualization  
process. The analysis of proverbs can help sharpen the missionaries’ insight and be a  
catalyst for contextualization.  
In the previous two sections, we have focused on the emic perspective and then  
the etic perspective. Now, these two can be combined together. In the next section, we  
will see how both the emic and etic perspectives are important for the church to engage  
the Scripture with their culture. The issues are different than those discussed previously  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 195  
but the process of engagement is the same. Let us see how the church responds to an  
issue concerning drumming in the church - let the drum roll begin.

*“Mawli Wusum kan jueli guwa*” **\(The cheap \[easy to obtain\] horse cannot climb the**  
# hill\)  
Should We Drum or Listen to the *Teng Nvonol*  
*“Stop the drumming immediately!” demanded the young man who just walked*  
*into the BCA church.* “The teng nyono *sent me to deliver the message that there was a*  
*ban on all drumming in the area; therefore, the church must cease drumming*  
*immediately and not resume again until he lifts the ban. ”*  
*The church was stunned! How should we respond?*  
*There were two instant knee-jerk reactions by this church o f 80 people. David*  
*represented the first \(and largest\) group, “We need to worship* Naawen. *How can we do*  
*that without drumming and dancing? We should reject the* teng nyono*’s ban and continue*  
*drumming. After all, we want to please* Naawen *more than men, don’t we? ”*  
*Kofi represented the other instant reaction, which was the exact opposite, “Wait -*  
*the drumming ban is a cultural matter. This is not addressed in* Naawen Wani *so we*  
*should just abide by the cultural rules until this thing blows over. Then we can get back*  
*to church as usual. Besides, the* teng nyono *has a lot o f power in the community, and we*  
*do not want to upset him. ”*  
*Immanuel was quiet. He listened to both sides and saw that each side was correct*  
*- at least partially. As he thought back on his own tragedy and the advice given to him,*  
*this all sounded very familiar. This polarization o f responses between rejection and*  
*acceptance is not new or unusual. Those were the initial responses to his situation -*  
*reject the dirt or accept their request. Unfortunately, that did not allow* Naawen Wani *to*  
196  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **1 9 7**  
*engage the issues. Most o f the time the discussion centers around, “which view is right -*  
*rejection or acceptance? ” Is there a way forward to help us deal with issues concerning*  
*Scripture and culture? While there are no easy answers to these questions, Immanuel*  
*remembered how they dug to the roots o f a proverb in order to fin d the meaning, which*  
*then was critiqued by* Naawen Wani. *Once they did this with proverbs a few times, it*  
*helped them to do the same process with cultural issues affecting the church. “Could this*  
*process help us again? ” he thought.*  
*Immanuel blurted out, “I think that we should call all of the church leaders*  
*together in the area tonight to discuss the issues and pray. As a church family, we need*  
*the counsel and support o f one another. ”*  
*Immanuel’s suggestion was well received. That afternoon, Immanuel and Joe*  
*visited the local church leaders and invited them to attend a meeting. The outcome o f this*  
*situation would affect all o f their churches and their input was needed. 12 church leaders*  
*gathered to discuss this case.*  
*Pastor David started off, “I f you read Psalms 150,* Naawen *says that we should*  
*worship him with different instruments and dancing. That is what we want to do. I say*  
*that we should ignore the* teng nyono, *and obey* Naawen.  
“/ *know these people, ” replied one church leader who grew up in the village. “I f*  
*we give them our little finger today, then they will take our whole arm tomorrow! ”*  
*Another jumped in, “Let us stand together and ignore him. I f he attacks one o f the*  
*churches, then we will all stand together andfight back. ’’ Those who took the position to*  
*reject the* teng nyono *were very vocal and emotional.*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **198**  
*Some others were less vocal andfelt that we should not be so combative. “We*  
*should heed the* teng nyono*’s words fo r this short period o f time, ” responded Pastor*  
*Kofi, “The drum ban will not last forever. We will just wait it out. ” Another pastor*  
*agreed, “There is nothing in* Naawen Wani *that says we HA VE to beat the drums. We can*  
*still worship* Naawen *without the drum during this time. ” This position o f acceptance*  
*seemed like backsliding to the ones who advocated rejecting the* teng nyono.  
*The more the discussion continued, the more it seemed that each o f the two*  
*parties became more entrenched in their positions. They each became more justified*  
*using their own portions* o/'Naawen Wani *to strengthen their resolve. Since no consensus*  
*could be reached that night, we agreed to fast and pray during Monday to Friday and*  
*meet again on Friday night. During the week, we would ask* Naawen *to inform us how to*  
*respond. We would also discuss the issue with other Christian leaders to seek their*  
*guidance. This would allow time fo r the Holy Spirit to move us gently away from the*  
*polarized extremes. Then, we would all report our findings on Friday, where we would*  
*make a decision.*  
As the church leaders leave the initial meeting, let us step back and analyze what  
transpired. This was an “excluded middle” issue that the Builsa church had never  
addressed before. Note how the two typical responses occur again\: either total acceptance  
or total rejection. This does not allow for Scripture to critically engage the issues. Both  
groups are composed of sincere Christians who honestly want to follow the will of God.  
How can they have such opposite opinions? To make it worse, the more they discuss it,  
the more entrenched they each become in their own positions.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 199  
To understand how this occurs, it helps to return to our discussion on  
epistemology. We discussed in the last section how believers hold a wide range of  
epistemologies of Scripture from Naive Realism to Instrumentalism, with Critical  
Realism in the middle. Believers also hold a wide range of epistemologies of culture.  
Mission application has stretched across the continuum between the two extremes.  
The critical discussion occurs when the two factors of Scripture and culture are  
applied together \(see Figure 12\).  
Combined Views of Scripture and Culture  
# NaiVe  
# Realism  
# •»- Critical  
# ? Realism  
# Instrumentalism  
# Instrumentalism Critical Realism  
# Naive Realism  
View of Scripture  
Fig. 12. Combined Epistemologies of Scripture and Culture  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 200  
Often, a consistent epistemology is not applied for both Scripture and culture;  
rather, these two factors are often applied in an *inverse relationship.* For example, those  
who wanted to ignore the drum ban maintained a Naive Realist view of Scripture  
\(Scripture is totally objective and we need to obey Psalms 150 to the letter\), while also  
holding an Instrumentalist view of culture \(culture is totally subjective and can be  
changed or ignored\). The inconsistent epistemology applied to the interaction of Scripture  
and culture provides insight into the confusion that occurs. Let us look at how mission  
practice has often combined the two factors of Scripture and culture in order to more  
clearly see this inconsistency and learn from it.  
As the Scripture and culture interact across the length of this continuum, five  
common areas are identified. It is helpful to think of these areas as common resting  
places and not fixed end points since this interaction of the Scripture and culture is an  
ongoing process. This chart reveals the underlying epistemological assumptions for the  
views held for the drumming ban discussed above. The five common resting places can  
be described as follows\:  
**1\. Acceptance** = This is a Naive Realist view of culture together with an  
Instrumentalist view of Scripture. Culture supercedes Scripture or deals with issues that  
Scripture does not address. All cultural practices are seen as valid. Scriptural knowledge  
is seen as totally subjective. This leads to cultural relativism and theological  
universalism. Pastor Kofi holds this position.  
**2\. Absorption** = This is a tendency towards \(though not a total commitment to\) a  
Naive Realist view of culture and an Instrumentalist view of Scripture. Culture tends to  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 201  
interpret Scripture. Cultural practices are absorbed into the life of the church with very  
little change. This leads to assimilation. Scripture is like cheesecloth that strains out large  
pieces but allows much of the cultural liquid to flow through unchanged. The practical  
outcome is often syncretism.  
**3\. Critical Engagement=** This is a Critical Realist view of both Scripture and  
culture. An example is the mother tongue Scripture, which contains both the Scripture  
and language of culture; therefore, it becomes the truth that is the interpreter of culture. In  
addition, the culture is actively engaged with the Scripture allowing for a creative tension  
that releases new insight into both the culture and the Scripture. Both Scripture and  
culture receive new appreciation as the process of engagement draws out the best from  
each. It is not a mere accommodation or absorption of cultural practices; rather, this  
engagement of the mother tongue Scripture reveals the glory of God in ways that have  
been hidden in the past. This view does not have a low view of Scripture; it merely  
recognizes the human limitations of language resulting in an understanding of Scripture  
that is not yet complete. A fuller understanding comes when the Holy Spirit speaks  
through the creative interaction of various cultures and the mother tongue Scripture.  
Scriptural knowledge is then both objective and subjective, in accordance with I  
Corinthians 13\:12 \(“Now I know in part; then I shall know fully”\). The practical outcome  
is proper contextualization resulting in indigenous Christianity.  
**4\. Modification** = This view tends toward \(though does not commit to\) a Naive  
Realist view of Scripture and an Instrumentalist view of culture. The Scripture tends to be  
the final interpreter of culture. Culture engages the Scripture in “familiar territory,  
allowing modifications to areas such as music, dress, worship style, language, etc. Since  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 202  
the engagement of the two is only partial, only the categories and themes that are familiar  
to the traditional church are treated. This allows the modification of practices that are  
“safe” but no new insight is generally gained. The practical outcome is often a “foreign  
gospel” \(e.g., Western theology in African clothing\).  
**5\. Rejection** = This views holds a Naive Realist view of Scripture and an  
Instrumentalist view of culture. Scripture critiques all culture as evil. The culture is  
prejudged to be unredeemable; therefore, there is no engagement of the two - simply  
rejection of the culture. This leads to the *“tabula rasa"* doctrine. Some have tried to  
maintain this position by removing themselves from cultural practices and not becoming  
engaged in them. This implies that the Scripture is fully understood and totally objective.  
This perspective is captured by those who say, “The Bible says what it means, and means  
what it says. Period.” Since knowledge of Scripture is seen to be timeless and universal,  
there is no other interpretation that will be considered. The practical outcome is often  
“split-level” Christianity, isolation, or Fundamentalism. Pastor David holds this position.  
In light of this continuum, the target or goal for the interaction of Scripture and  
culture is brought to focus. While the other positions on the continuum reflect  
inconsistent epistemologies resulting in confusion, critical engagement \(area 3 above\)  
maintains a consistent Critical Realist epistemology for both culture and Scripture,  
thereby providing clarity. Proper contextualization of Christianity will occur as a result of  
the process of critical engagement. By critically engaging the Scripture with the culture,  
both the image of God and the stain of sin will be revealed in the culture. Our approach to  
mission will have cultural relevance with biblical integrity. In addition, the Scripture will  
be further understood and appreciated by those within the culture. This Critical Realist  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 203  
epistemology is advocated by Heibert \(1994\:51\) as a biblical approach to knowledge,  
which forms the basis for critical engagement of the Scripture and culture. The benefit of  
this understanding and approach will be demonstrated by the drum ban in Ghana.  
Pastor David and others who argued that we should obey God in Psalms 150 and  
and ignore the *teng nyono* demonstrate a Naive Realist view of Scripture, whereby  
Scripture is a picture of reality that is fully understood apart from varying cultural  
contexts. At the same time, they held an Instrumentalist epistemology concerning culture,  
whereby culture can be readily ignored or changed. By combining the epistemologies of  
Scripture and culture, this position can be plotted on the “Interaction of Scripture and  
Culture” continuum on the far right in the position of Rejection \(area 5\).  
Pastor Kofi and others who felt we should uncritically heed the *teng nyono*’s  
words for this short period of time demonstrate a Nai ve Realist view of culture, where  
culture is a picture of reality that should not be challenged. They also have an  
Instrumentalist view of Scripture since they felt that Scripture could be readily ignored or  
dismissed. The combination of the epistemologies of Scripture and culture can be plotted  
on the continuum on the far left in the position of Acceptance \(area 1\).  
By plotting these positions on the continuum, it shows the two extreme positions.  
The continuum helps to see which direction each position needs to consider moving  
towards in order to see this issue as an opportunity to engage the Scripture with culture.  
Those in the Acceptance position \(Pastor Kofi\) need to critique culture more and take  
Scripture more seriously by engaging it with culture. Those in the Rejection position  
\(Pastor David\) need to reconsider their incomplete understanding of Scripture and engage  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 204  
culture more with the Scripture. By doing so, both positions can head toward the critical  
engagement position in the middle \(area 3\).  
Combined Views of Scripture and Cutture

# Realism
