Let us return to the Church leaders and see how they work this out  
Digging In  
*As they were leaving the meeting, Immanuel spoke up, “Do you remember the*  
*conflict I had recently with the local elders after my son died? ” Silence blanketed the*  
*room. “I was torn by two opposing positions, just like we heard tonight. Some advised*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 205  
*that we reject our culture and others advise that we accept it. The problem is that we are*  
*still talking about the tree and not digging down to the roots. ”*  
*Pastors David and Kofi were impressed with how Immanuel was gaining wisdom*  
*concerning issues o f the Scripture and culture. Perhaps his tragedy had deeply impressed*  
*this upon him. Pastor David responded, “You are right, Immanuel, we are only talking*  
*about the tree* - *those things that we can see...a drum, a* teng nyono, *a ban. We should*  
*dig down to the roots and find out why those things exist in our culture, just like we did*  
*before with the proverbs and then with your own tragedy. ”*  
*“It is hard work to dig down to the roots, ” added Immanuel, “but it is much more*  
*effective than simply giving quick answers that do not help in the long run. The elders*  
*say,* “Mawli wusuum kan jueli guwa” *\(The cheap \[easy to obtain\] horse cannot climb*  
*the hill\). It is easy to give quick responses o f rejection or acceptance but, when it comes*  
*to difficult hills, those cheap \(easy to obtain\) responses do not get us over to the other*  
*side. ”*  
*Pastor Kofi chuckled, “You are right, brother. Let us reflect on that over the*  
*week. Then, we can discuss the roots more on Friday. ”*  
*This was a good suggestion since it was late already. Everyone departed.*  
*On the way home, Joe was pensive. He was mainly quiet during the meeting since*  
*he wanted to listen to the different positions. While the Builsa church may not have*  
*addressed this drum ban before, Joe seemed to remember this being an issue in other*  
*parts o f Ghana. He would check into it.*  
*When discussing the drum ban case with the leadership o f the missionary*  
*organization in Accra, they informed Joe that problems had arisen in Southern Ghana*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 206  
*over the traditional practice o f banning drumming fo r a month during the Homowo*  
*festival. When a church ignored the ban and continued drumming in May, 1999, the*  
*church was attacked by young people who vandalized and hurt several worshippers. The*  
*church went to court over the issue and the court sided with the traditional authorities,*  
*thereby bringing shame on the church. The end result was that it brought a lot o f*  
*negative publicity to the church. Kwami Bediako criticized the church leaders fo r not*  
*entering into dialogue to engage Scripture with culture. He stated, “We may well find*  
*that people are resisting us, not the gospel. They are challenging us, the church, not the*  
*gospel, because they do not see Jesus in us” \(Bediako 1999b\: 14\). This information was*  
*most helpful as a warning about what can happen when the church rigidly maintains the*  
*Rejection position. It also hinted at one possible root to this situation. The elders want to*  
*receive respect and not just be ignored.*  
*This was confirmed when Immanuel and Joe went to visit another BCA pastor in a*  
*neighboring people group. He informed them that their church previously dealt with this*  
*issue. His advice was to show respect fo r the authorities, “The* teng nyono *is the one who*  
*oversees the earth shrine fo r the area. It is his responsibility to make the necessary*  
*sacrifices to ensure a good harvest from the land. He will intervene when there is not*  
*enough rain, too much rain, too much wind, etc. When the millet is close to harvest time,*  
*a wind storm could destroy the millet. The ban on drums is in place so that the noise will*  
*not disturb the ancestors; otherwise, it is believed that they will bring wind on the land*  
*and destroy the crop. ”*  
*That was new to Joe and Immanuel. “It looks like we are getting to another root*  
*here, ” observed Immanuel. “The* teng nyono *has a responsibility to do what he can to see*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 207  
*blessing on the land. I f he is lax in carrying out these taboos, then he could be held*  
*responsible. ”*  
*The pastor recommended that the church dialogue with the* teng nyono *to show*  
*him honor and respect. This is particularly important since the church will one day need*  
*the* teng nyono*’s assistance when dealing with land issues. The* teng nyono *can deny land*  
*to anyone he wants, and the pastor knew a church that was unable to acquire land since*  
*they did not show respect to the* teng nyono.  
*This was an important underlying cultural issue. Joe remembered the Builsa core*  
*values revealed in the proverbs. Core Value number six advised, “Honor those in*  
*authority since they can help you. ” Joe remembered the proverb,* “Zula ka Ngandiinta”  
*\(Honor is food\), and he also knew how important food was in a farming community like*  
*this. To give honor to someone then, is like presenting a nice gift o f food to them.*  
*Joe spoke to Immanuel, “Perhaps, the* teng nyono *is looking fo r some respect*  
*from the church. After all, the church is near his house and many o f his children are*  
*attending the church now. I f we do not show him respect, then he may make things*  
*difficultfor us. On the other hand, he can be helpful to us, if we respect and honor him. ”*  
*Immanuel agreed, “He can be a help to us someday, just like the pastor*  
*mentioned about the church acquiring land The elders have said,* "Fi ma dan bo kuub  
yeri, fi a ngobi ka kamsa tuila” *\(Ifyour mother is in the funeral house, you will eat hot*  
*bean cakes\). ” Joe’s puzzled look made Immanuel explain, “I f your mother is at the*  
*funeral house, then she will be helping to cookfood to receive the visitors who arrive.*  
*Since she is your own mother, she will get some o f the best bean cakes and give them to*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 208  
*you when you arrive. So, honor and respect your parents, fo r they will be able to help you*  
*in the future. ”*  
*Joe enjoyed the proverb and its meaning. It opened up other questions fo r Joe*  
*about funerals, receiving visitors, etc. For now, though, this proverb revealed and*  
*affirmed an important cultural value that may be a root to this particular situation -*  
*authorities should be honored since they can help you. This smacked against Joe’s sense*  
*o f equality and egalitarianism. Shouldn’t all people be treated alike? After all, aren* 7  
*they all equally created by God? While it is true that they were all created equally, Joe*  
*was also reminded that authorities are given by God and should be respected \(Hebrews*  
*13, which was not often preached in his home church\). This relationship with the* teng  
nyono *can be helpful to us later if we treat him properly.*  
*Immanuel asked, “The* teng nyono *had not called this ban before; why was he*  
*doing this now? Many o f the children in his house were coming to the church now; was*  
*he trying to indirectly stop them from attending by “arranging*” *an argument to discredit*  
*the church? There must be more that we do not yet know that we need to fin d out.*  
*Perhaps, the* teng nyono *is trying to get our attention fo r some reason, and we need to*  
*fin d out from him what it is. ”*  
*Only by entering into dialogue with the* teng nyono *was it later discovered that*  
*this was actually the case. Immanuel was astute to consider this. He was digging deep*  
*and asking the right questions to find the roots.*  
*Joe agreed, “The elders have said,* “Jakpak kan chali kapiokapo kinla” ***\(The old***  
***man does not run in the valley for no reason\).** There must be an underlying meaning*  
*behind unusual or strange events. ” Joe again remembered the Builsa core values*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **2 0 9**  
*revealed in the proverbs. Core value number 8 was “There are underlying meanings to*  
*things and events. ” Joe was starting to reason more like a Builsa.*  
*Immanuel noticed this too, and he laughed, “That is exactly right, Joe. You are a*  
*Builsa now. ”*  
*“Maybe I will catch the monkey’s tail one day, ” thought Joe to himself.*  
*Immanuel’s encouragement also helped Joe to reflect on some o f the other core values*  
*that he remembered from the proverbs. He remembered core value number 3, “Small*  
*mistakes can lead to big problems and small acts o f kindness can lead to greatness. ”*  
*Then he shared with Immanuel, “I f we take this issue too lightly, then this small problem*  
*can blow up into big problems later.* “Gbeera, gbeera tintain alaa cheeri zuk ” *\(The stone*  
*children use to play with cuts the head\). The church in Southern Ghana realized this too*  
*late. Their experience was a warning to humbly and patiently move forward with caution*  
*and not just respond with a knee-jerk reaction.*  
*Joe and Immanuel were taking the time to dig to uncover some o f the possible*  
*roots. By reflecting on the culture, key cultural themes and issues were revealed. This*  
*was an important first step to move toward a Critical Realist epistemology. An equally*  
*important step was to reflect on the Scripture.*  
*Joe and Immanuel also consulted with a BCA pastor in another neighboring*  
*people group. He informed them that they also had the same issue some time ago. Their*  
*solution was to talk with the* teng noyno *and use Scripture to explain about God and his*  
*control o f the wind. “We cited Psalms 135\:7 \(also described in Jeremiah 51\:16 and*  
*10\:13\) ”, explained the pastor, “where God “brings out the wind from his storehouses. ”*  
*We told the* teng nyono *that the church would meet and pray to the God who controls the*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 210  
*wind so that God will keep it in the storehouses during this period o f time. A spiritual*  
*issue needs a spiritual response - use God’s power and Word to respond. ”*  
*Immanuel and Joe were beginning to realize that Scripture had a lot to say about*  
*the roots o f this case. When discussing the tree only, Scripture may not appear relevant;*  
*however, Scripture can speak powerfully to the root issues.*  
*Joe was pondering other Scripture passages that he knew. Joe explained, “It is*  
*interesting to read other passages that discuss* Naawen *’s control and use o f wind.*  
Naawen *made the wind pass over the Earth during the flood of Noah’s time in order for*  
*the water to withdraw \(Genesis 8\).* Naawen *also brought the east wind all day and night*  
*in order to bring the locusts to plague Egypt \(Exodus 10\). ”*  
*Immanuel added in, “Yes.* Naawen *also sent the eastern wind to divide the Red*  
*Sea so that the Israelites could escape from the Egyptians, and then* Naawen *sent the*  
*wind again to cover the Egyptians in the Red Sea \(Exodus 14-15\). Do you remember how*  
Naawen *also sent wind to bring manna and quail to the Israelites in the wilderness*  
*\(Numbers 11\)?”*  
*Joe was getting excited as well. He had never discussed God’s use and control o f*  
*the wind in seminary, but now it was the most pressing, relevant, and interesting topic for*  
*this case. When Joe and Immanuel returned home that day, they looked in a concordance*  
*to see other Scripture portions that dealt with Naawen’s use o f wind.*  
*“Job discusses how* Naawen *quiets the earth by the south wind and controls the*  
*clouds \(Job 37\:14-17\), ” Joe observed.* “Naawen *also causes the east wind to blow in*  
*heaven and* Naawen*’s power brings in the south wind \(Psalm 78\:26\).* Naawen *even walks*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 211  
*upon the wings o f the wind \(Psalm 104\:3\), speaks and raises up the wind \(Psalm 147\:18,*  
*107\:25\), and has gathered up the wind in his fists \(Proverbs 30\:4\). ”*  
*Immanuel joined in, “Look here. Naawen created the wind \(Amos 4\:13\).* Naawen  
*uses the wind to bring judgment \(Jonah 1\:4, 4\:8; Hosea 13\:15; Ezekiel 13\:13\).* Naawen  
*speaks to the wind to restore the dry bones \(Ezekiel 37\).* Yezu *even demonstrates his*  
*command over the wind as he speaks to it and it obeys \(Matthew 14\:32\), and when* Yezu  
*gets in the boat, the wind ceases \(Mark 6\:51\). Lastly, the book o f Revelations describes*  
*the angels as holding back the windfrom the earth fo r a period o f time \(7\:1\). ”*  
*Joe concluded,* “Naawen Wani *gives a picture* o/Naawen*’s authority and control*  
*o f the wind that I never considered before. It certainly was not a part o f the systematic*  
*theology that I learned in seminary!” Through the engagement o f the Scripture with this*  
*cultural issue, a new understanding and appreciation o f God was realized. Perhaps this*  
*would be news that is both good and new to the* teng nyono.  
*Immanuel and Joe also discussed the story in Luke 19\:37 - 44. In this passage,*  
*the disciples were worshiping* Yezu *as he arrived in Jerusalem but the Pharisees*  
*requested that* Yezu *tell them to stop.* Yezu *replied that, even if he tells them to stop, the*  
*rocks will cry out to praise* Naawen. *They discussed this passage and its relevance to*  
*their present context.*  
*Immanuel noted,* “Naawen *will receive praise from creation. Even* /\^Naawen*’s*  
*people do not cry out, the rocks will cry out and praise him. Perhaps, we should tell the*  
teng nyono *that we will let the rocks cry out and worship* Naawen *while we let the drum*  
*rest fo r this period o f time. ”*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 212  
*That was a radical suggestion. They would have never come to that point unless*  
*they had taken the effort to dig to the root issues and then engage them with Scripture.*  
*They were now ready to meet with the others on Friday night and discuss some o f these*  
*roots issues.*  
Before the Friday meeting, let us reflect on what has been happening. Note how  
this case falls into the “excluded middle.” Joe had never discussed God’s use of and  
control of wind in any of his seminary training. He never learned about the *teng nyono*  
either. As a result, he did not know how Scripture could address the case. It was not until  
they started to dig to the roots that they realized how Scripture does speak powerfully to  
the case. Once the roots are discovered, then a spiritual response can be given. Note how  
the pastor stated, “Spiritual issues need spiritual responses.” That is certainly true in this  
case, as well as in many other “excluded middle” cases.  
Note also how the genre of proverbs provides key insight for Joe and Immanuel.  
By analyzing the proverbs, Joe was able to articulate and understand some Builsa core  
values. These were extremely helpful to him as he was trying to deal with this case. The  
core values helped him to recognize what is important and to know where to look. This  
helped to guide the digging process for Joe.  
We can also see that mutual respect and understanding are starting to develop  
between Joe and Immanuel. Joe appreciates Immanuel’s friendship, and Immanuel is  
beginning to see Joe as an acceptable outsider. Immanuel even complements Joe that he  
is now a Builsa. The constitutive model of communication helps us interpret this. In the  
process of communicating, more occurs than just the transmission of a message. In this  
case, mutual respect and friendship start to develop. In the process of communicating,  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 213  
they also start to reconstruct their concept of God and the wind. As they uncover cultural  
issues and then engage them with Scripture, this is the beginning of a Builsa Christian  
theology.  
It may help to see how this contextualization occurred. The study of proverbs was  
a catalyst in several ways to promote contextualization. As Joe studied the proverbs, he  
found some Builsa core values. These core values helped him to understand the Builsa  
culture, which then helped him to identify root issues in this case.  
Joe and Immanuel’s previous conversations with proverbs showed them a process  
to use in understanding and engaging culture with Scripture. They learned to ride the bike  
on familiar ground \(using proverbs\), and then they were able to climb this hill involving  
the drum ban. They knew that it was hard work, but they also realized that it would result  
in a response that was both faithful to Scripture and culturally relevant. If they did not  
take the time to learn how to ride this bike of contextualization, then they would likely  
take one of the two typical responses that were noticed early in the discussions - either  
total acceptance or total rejection.  
By digging to the roots of the case, they identified issues of honor/respect, God’s  
use of wind, blessing on the land, and other underlying issues that need to still be  
discovered. Discussions at this level can get to the heart of the matter leading to a well-  
informed response.  
Notice how Scripture is the “interpreter of culture and tradition” \(Bediako  
1999a\: 1\) since it provides the clearest revelation of God in culture. The role of the mother  
tongue Scripture is key to forming a Christian response. Also, note how the etic  
perspectives on Scripture are helpful. The pastors from neighboring people groups  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 214  
identified perspectives of Scripture that Joe and Immanuel had not considered. This then  
opened their eyes to see aspects of Scripture that they had overlooked before.  
The etic perspectives on culture were also helpful. Joe’s knowledge of the church  
in Southern Ghana provided helpful insight for the Builsa context. Since the pastors in  
neighboring people groups already dealt with this situation before, their input was also  
helpful. It was through this interaction that they first learned that an underlying issue was  
the wind and protection of the crops. This also enlarged their understanding about the  
role of the *teng nyono* and how he can exert control in a community.  
When this etic perspective was coupled with Immanuel’s emic perspective, both  
Joe and Immanuel were able to gain insight that was far beyond what they anticipated.  
This would be very helpful for the pastors’ meeting on Friday night. Let us proceed to  
that meeting and see what happened.  
Dialogue with the *Tens Nvono*  
*When Friday came, we all met and discussed what we learned and how* Naawen  
*had spoken to us. Through the process o f engaging the Scripture with culture, the Holy*  
*Spirit began to reveal an understanding and appreciation o f both the Scripture and*  
*culture in areas that we had not considered before. The attitude o f the meeting exhibited*  
*much greater unity than the previous Sunday. Everyone shared what they had learned*  
*that week. After Immanuel and Joe shared, there was a long pause ....*  
*David broke the silence, “I did not realize that one o f the issues was the wind and*  
*that the* teng nyono *was trying to do what he could to help the crops be bountiful. I was*  
*too critical o f the culture at first, and I did not allow* Naawen Wani *to speak to the core*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 215  
*issues. I had never considered* Naawen **’5** *use o f the wind to do his purposes, and I doubt*  
*that the* teng nyono *knows this either. In addition,* Naawen Wani *speaks a lot about*  
*blessing the land, and the church can play a big role in prayer to seek G od’s blessing on*  
*the crops. ”*  
*David was moving away from his former position o f rejection. He was taking the*  
*culture more seriously and understanding the core issues. He also was open to learning*  
*new aspects o f Scripture that he overlooked before. This helped him move away from his*  
*Naive Realist view o f Scripture and Instrumentalist view o f culture so that he could move*  
*towards the middle epistemology o f Critical Realism fo r both Scripture and culture.*  
*Kofi, who represented the other position o f acceptance, was also moving more*  
*towards the middle, “I understand more now. The* teng nyono *is looking to the ancestors*  
*to provide blessing on the land This will likely involve sacrificing animals. We should*  
*not accept that since* Yezu *is the sacrifice that* Naawen *provided fo r us. We should be*  
*more critical o f the culture and show the* teng nyono *from* Naawen Wani *how* Yezu *is the*  
*mediator between the Builsa and* Naawen. ” *Kofi was taking Naawen Wani more*  
*seriously and also critiquing culture more. As a result, he was moving from his extreme*  
*position o f acceptance \(Naive Realist view o f culture and Instrumentalist view o f*  
*Scripture\) towards an engagement* o/'Naawen Wani *with culture.*  
*After lengthy discussion, it was agreed that we all go together and visit the* teng  
nyono *on Saturday morning. We would approach him and ask him to explain why this*  
*ban was occurring since we did not understand how the ban originated, why it*  
*originated, etc. We hoped that the dialogue would reveal what was behind the drum ban.*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 216  
*Then, we could engage the mother tongue Scripture to facilitate dialogue and*  
*understanding.*  
*As we gathered the next morning, the mood was tense. Would the* teng nyono  
*accept us? Was he planning a surprise fo r us? Questions like this raced through our*  
*heads. Immanuel spoke up, “We do not know how we will be received. The Holy Spirit*  
*has been speaking to us this week. Let us pray fo r him to go before us and guide us in our*  
*dialogue. ” This suggestion was well received.*  
*When we arrived at the* teng nyono’s *house, he received us andfreely discussed*  
*with us why the ban was in effect. He explained, “This is a* kisuk *from our ancestors. I*  
*did not originate it and I do not know who started it. I am only there to obey it. Our*  
*ancestors started this* kisuk *in order to help get a good crop so that we can have health. ”*  
*Pastor David asked, “Our churches have met fo r several years now and we have*  
*never heard o f this. Why are we first hearing o f it now?* "  
*The* teng nyono *hesitated for a moment. He then confided, “A* teng nyono *from*  
*another area came to me and asked why I am not enforcing the ban in my area. He*  
*pointed out that the church is close by my house and I have said nothing. I f I can not do*  
*what I am supposed to do in my area, how can they do so in their area? ”*  
*This revealed a hidden but important insight into the* teng nyono*’s conscience - he*  
*had to save face in the sight o f his spiritual colleagues. I f we did not obey the ban, then*  
*the* teng nyono *would be obligated to act against us - not because o f anger against us*  
*necessarily, but in order to save face. I f we would agree to his ban \(even fo r a short*  
*time\), then he would save face. This was an important consideration when appealing to*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 217  
*his conscience. We had to show our humility and respect his authority in some way so*  
*that he could save face.*  
*Another important point in speaking to the conscience o f the* teng nyono *was that*  
*he was responsible fo r the success o f the crop. Due to the high value o f community*  
*\(remember core value number 2 gainedfrom the proverbs\), it was important fo r him to*  
*do what he could to ensure a blessing on the crop. I f he did not do what he could, then he*  
*could be held responsible fo r negligence. This was another important consideration*  
*concerning his conscience.*  
*“We can help askfor* Naawen*’s blessing on the land, ” offered Kofi. We too are*  
*concerned about the harvest and we know where* Naawen *keeps the wind. ”*  
*The* Teng nyono *was listening intently, as Kofi explained how* Naawen *kept the*  
*wind in his storehouse, and how he would open the storehouse to do what he wanted.*  
*This was all new information to the* teng nyono. *He was shocked that* Naawen Wani *was*  
*in his language and that it spoke about the concerns that were extremely relevant to him.*  
*Gradually, he came to see that we could help him do his job since we too were concerned*  
*about the harvest in the community. We could help him in ensuring a blessing on the land*  
*by calling on the church to pray fo r* Naawen, *the one who kept the wind in his storehouse,*  
*to intervene. The* teng nyono *was hearing good news. He was about to hear more.*  
*Immanuel shared, “When Ifollowed* Yezu, *he changed my life andfilled me with*  
*his blessing. That is why I go to church and drum. I want to worship* Naawen, *thank*  
Naawen, *and ask his blessing on the Builsa area. The elders have said,* “Vanchoa kan  
kum vanchoa a yaali nin nyiam a kpieri ” *\(A friend will always fin d tears to cry at a*  
*friend’s funeral\). When you hear o f a friend’s death, you are touched so deeply that you*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **2 1 8**  
*spontaneously cry at his funeral \(you do not have to force yourself to bring tears to show*  
*your sorrow\). It is the same way with* Yezu. *He has touched my life so deeply that I*  
*spontaneously want to praise and worship* Naawen. ”  
*The* teng nyono *was speechless. He knew Immanuel and he could see the changes*  
*in his life. The* teng nyono *knew that some o f the people that attended church were not*  
*like him, but he could not deny what he saw in Immanuel.*  
*Joe then opened the Buli Bible and read the passage in Luke 19. The* teng nyono  
*again was surprised that this book was in his language and talked about his very own*  
*pressing issues -asking the church members to not make noise and stopping the worship.*  
*Even though several o f his children were in the church, he still had not understood why*  
*they were there and what relevance it hadfor their lives. He was beginning to understand*  
*fo r the first time.*  
*The dialogue went back andforth, until finally David concluded, “It is our*  
*intention to bless the land and not curse it. That is why we do the drumming. Instead of*  
*looking to the ancestors to help, we will pray in the churches fo r* Naawen, *the one who*  
*controls the wind, to keep it in his storehouse during this time. Out o f respect fo r you,*  
*Baba, we will stop drumming and let the rocks sing praise to* Naawen. ”  
*The* teng nyono *smiled widely and the atmosphere was free and light hearted.*  
*The* teng nyono *then explained, “I did not know these things before. I did not know why*  
*you drummed in church and I did not know why you did not like the drum ban. I thought*  
*that you were just being stubborn and disrespectful o f the elders. ” Now it was the church*  
*leaders turn to be silent, as well as surprised.*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 219  
*The* teng nyono *said in closing, “We are very appreciative o f your coming. May*  
Naawen *bless you all. We too will send a message back to the one who sent us that you*  
*are also helping the land and not destroying it. Thank you once again. ”*  
*“Would you like us to pray to* Naawen *fo r you and our crops now? ” asked Kofi.*  
*He agreed and Immanuel prayed.*  
*As we rose to leave, Joe said to the* teng nyono *jokingly, “When you come to*  
*understand who* Yezu *is one day, then you will also want to drum and dance to give Him*  
*praise.* ”  
*“I may come to church one day, ” replied the* teng nyono.  
*We departed on good terms. When we left the* teng nyono, *we gathered in the*  
*church to pray and also gathered again on Monday night to pray. That Sunday, all o f the*  
*churches let the drums rest. We explained to the churches the reason fo r not drumming,*  
*and we worshipped* Naawen *in song and dance the best that we could. The churches also*  
*prayedfor the harvest, which turned out fine that year. We still did not know how long*  
*the ban would be in place so we continued to pray.*  
*Three days after meeting with the* teng nyono, *he sent a message to us that the*  
*ban was now rescinded. We could drum again! That was in time fo r the Wednesday*  
*service andfor a wedding on Friday. The wedding had plenty o f dancing, singing and*  
*drumming. In addition, the* teng nyono *saved face among his peers and had a clear*  
*conscience that we were all doing our best to see* Naawen*’s blessing on the harvest. The*  
*church and the* teng nyono *were pleased with the outcome.*  
*Best o f all, the drumming ban had opened a wide pathway fo r future dialogue,*  
*witness, and understanding between the church and the* teng nyono *that was not open*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 220  
*before. On top o f that, the church had learned a process to use in dealing with issues o f*  
*culture and Scripture.*  
*A few months after the drum ban was over, the* teng nyono *came to visit the*  
*Immanuel’s house and said, “I remembered our conversation about how the church*  
*wanted to bring* Naawen*’s blessing on the land. I have seen you helping communities to*  
*dig and line wells fo r clean drinking water. We have a problem with water also. Can the*  
*church help us with a well in my area? ”*  
*Immanuel was shocked and also elated. The* teng nyono, *one o f the most powerful*  
*men in the community, was coming to the church with his problems. Since the church*  
*humbly sought his help previously, he humbled himself as well to ask help from the*  
*church to solve this community problem.*  
*Immanuel explained, “We will help you, Baba, to help our people. ”*  
*The dialogue between the church and the* teng nyono *created by the drum ban*  
*was still ongoing. While the* teng nyono *did not come to church right away, he gained a*  
*much better understanding o f why the church is there and how it can bless his people and*  
*land. Now that is something to drum about!*  
*A year or so later, the* teng nyono *fell sick. Guess who he came to fo r help? He*  
*asked the church to come to his house and pray fo r him so that* Naawen *would make him*  
*healthy again. The church agreed and went. He later became well.*  
*A few weeks ago, the* teng nyono *was preparing to take a trip down South. He was*  
*nervous about the trip and he called on Immanuel to come and pray fo r his safe journey*  
*and return. We are still praying fo r him.*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 221  
Let us pause here to reflect on what has happened with the *teng nyono.* Consider  
the issues that are being dealt with - health, blessing on the crop, safety on a journey.  
These are all “excluded middle” issues. Previously, the *teng nyono* would not look to the  
church for help for these spiritual issues. Now, he turns to them. The church responds in a  
way that is culturally relevant and Scripturally faithful, and God responds. How did we  
get to this point?  
There are no easy answers or quick formulas for dealing with issues like the  
banning of drums in Ghana. The common default responses of rejection or acceptance  
limit the discussion and creative energy that could be released by the Holy Spirit. When  
discussing historical mission practices in Africa on issues such as polygamy, bride  
wealth, circumcision, etc., Hastings points out that, rather than being an obstacle, in the  
long run, these issues have been an instrument to focus on what being African and  
Christian means. He concludes, “Christianity has often thrived upon internal argument”  
\(1994\:322\). In other words, authentic expressions of African Christianity thrive upon the  
arguments \(dialogue\) created when Scripture and culture are critically engaged.  
In analyzing the narrative, several key elements emerge. First of all, note how the  
process was facilitated by the catalysts, Joe and Immanuel. They were the ones who  
called the leaders together. If not, the church leaders would have likely remained in their  
initial positions of acceptance or rejection. The catalysts also facilitated the process by  
consulting with others outside the group. They were able to provide the etic perspective  
through this interaction with church leaders from other people groups who were removed  
from the original context. In this case, the two catalysts, Joe and Immanuel were ideal  
since Immanuel could offer the emic perspective while Joe added the etic perspective. At  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 222  
any rate, these two catalysts were crucial to the entire process. They had learned the  
process from their engagement of proverbs. Once having learned to ride the bike, they  
felt they could take on the challenge of climbing this big hill. They could not do it alone,  
though.  
This narrative also shows the importance of involving a group in the process of  
contextualization. This hermeneutical community will listen to various voices. Most of  
these voices will be local, but others may be regional or international \(consider the role  
that the mission played, as well as church leaders in the region\). In the next section, we  
will have a closer look at the functioning of this hermeneutical community. For now, it is  
important to note that contextualization is not the work of a few in the academic world;  
rather, it can be conducted by grassroots church leaders as they gather in a hermeneutical  
community. It is in this community that the Holy Spirit can often speak.  
The role of the Holy Spirit can not be underplayed. Contextualization does not  
depend on the creativity or scholarship of people; rather, contextualization is a work of  
the Holy Spirit as he operates in the lives of Christians. Schreiter \(1985\:16\) states, “The  
Holy Spirit, working in and through the believing community, gives shape and expression  
to Christian experience.” Acknowledging this promotes a humility that God is at work in  
other Christian’s lives, regardless of their education levels, positions, experience, etc.  
This humility can help people to move from their initial positions to a critical realist  
epistemology.  
By articulating the underlying epistemological assumptions on issues like the  
drum ban, the knee jerk responses of the church can be evaluated and discussed. This  
helps direct the church community toward the goal of critical engagement by showing  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 223  
which area of the Scripture and cultural interaction is being neglected. By facilitating the  
critical engagement of both the Scripture and culture, contextualization occurs that  
produces “contextualized expressions of the gospel so that the gospel itself will be  
understood in ways the universal church has neither experienced nor understood before”  
\(Whiteman 1997\:4\). The drum ban then is no longer regarded as a thorny cultural issue to  
be ignored; rather, it is a growth opportunity for the universal church.  
Chapter Summary  
The narrative revealed several necessary ingredients to promote contextualization.  
By way of summary, these include.  
1\. There must be a deep conviction that the mother tongue Scripture is the word of  
God that is the ultimate “interpreter of culture and tradition” \(Bediako 1999a\: 1\) since it  
provides the clearest revelation of God in culture.  
2\. Space and time are needed for the Holy Spirit to speak to the issues of Scripture  
and culture, realizing that the Holy Spirit is more interested in this process than we are  
since it ultimately reveals the glory of God.  
3\. Humility must be paramount in order to accept our limited understanding of  
both Scripture and culture. This motivates one to be open to learning about areas of  
Scripture that have been overlooked. In addition, it persuades one to learn from the  
culture via the people, proverbs, and powers such as the *teng nyono*, etc.  
4\. Contextualization is best done in a hermeneutical community. This includes  
listening to the local, regional, and global church, as well as those within the culture who  
are the repositories of sacred wisdom or authority. Both the etic and emic perspectives  
provide valuable insight for contextualization.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 224  
5\. Cases like the drumming ban become part of the local church history that can  
be drawn upon for future understanding. These need to be recorded to help guide the  
church family of tomorrow.  
6\. By plotting the initial positions on Figure 13, the underlying epistemologies of  
Scripture and culture can be articulated. This heuristic helps to point out the  
inconsistencies in the epistemological positions, and it demonstrates the direction that the  
church needs to move in order to critically engage the Scripture and culture.  
Epistemologies really do matter, and they can help resolve issues involving the Scripture  
and culture, once articulated.  
7\. Catalysts are helpful to initiate and sustain the contextualization process. They  
are instrumental in drawing together the stakeholders, going outside the group for wider  
perspectives, and patiently guiding the group to informed consensus instead of reacting  
with the knee-jerk responses of total acceptance or total rejection.  
With these ingredients in place, the process for contextualization can proceed  
forward. In the next chapter, we discuss more details concerning this process.

2004 "Should We Drum or Listen to the *Teng Nyono*?" *Missiology\: An International*  
*Review* 32\(2\)\:203-215.

*uPopo jiib alaa jii ngmazuk jiib* ” **\(The stomach load carries the head load\)**  
The Circle Widens  
*Joe and Pastor Kofi were excited. They had been learning many proverbs*  
*and using them in ministry. Joe felt like he was finally catching the monkey’s tail. He*  
*knew that he still had a lot to learn but he was using the few proverbs he knew in the*  
*proper contexts* - *and people noticed. Whenever he introduced a proverb, the Builsa*  
*immediately focused their attention on what he had to say. It was almost magnetic - the*  
*Builsa wanted to hear the proverb and its meaning.*  
*After Joe used proverbs in his preaching last Sunday, a church member came up*  
*to him, “When you used those proverbs, it was like you were singing a song that I wanted*  
*to hear. You can sing that song all day long and I will listen. ” Joe finally felt like he was*  
*reaching the Builsa hearts, and the proverbs were a key to do so.*  
*Pastor Kofi was also getting excited. The church members were recognizing his*  
*use ofproverbs and they liked it. “We see that you have wisdom that will help us, ” one*  
*church member said. “Those that go to school learn much school teaching, but you have*  
*Builsa wisdom more than them that helps us. ”*  
*Pastor Kofi was starting to feel honor and respect from his congregation. He did*  
*not always feel this way. His lack o f extensive formal Bible training made him feel*  
*inadequate at times, especially when he compared himself to pastors like David who had*  
*been to Bible School. It made him question, at times, how effective he could be as a*  
*pastor without more formal schooling. He was beginning to feel differently now.*  
# 225  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 226  
*Another church member grabbed Pastor Kofi on Sunday and shared, “The*  
*proverbs from last week helped me to remember what was said when I went home. Then,*  
*I was able to pass it on to others. ” She continued, “I can now see that you know* Naawen  
Wani. ”  
*When a good proverb was spoken in church, it was almost impossible fo r the*  
*church members to NOT pass it on when they went home. As they repeated the proverb, it*  
*allowed the teaching to be remembered and repeated as well. This helped the Christian*  
*message to spread via communication channels that already exist in the Builsa culture.*  
*Pastor Kofi confided in Joe, “I used to think that our own proverbs were not very*  
*valuable and they were just something that we didfor fun. We had the proverbs in our*  
*own hands fo r a long time but did not realize their value in helping to communicate*  
Naawen Wani *in* Buluk. *Now, I realize how important they are and how* Naawen *himself*  
*gave wisdom to our ancestors to help us understand* Naawen Wani. ”  
*“We need to continue meeting together, ” replied Joe. “This is too important to*  
*miss. ”*  
*Joe and Pastor Kofi called all o f the church leaders together to meet every 2*  
*months. They would meet at Joe’s house and then Joe would drive them to a market*  
*center where they could meet with other church leaders on market day.*  
*On one particular day, a local government official heard about the meeting, and*  
*he asked to join the discussion with the twelve or so church leaders. Immanuel started*  
*the discussion in usualfashion, “Who has a proverb to share with us? ”*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 227  
*Pastor David replied,* *“This is the proverb that I heard\:* “Ba kan gering wusum  
kpalinsa ale kingkanga” **\(They cannot separate the fighting of horses with millet**  
# stalks\) ”  
*When the government official heard the proverb, a gentle smile went across his*  
*face. He had never heard that proverb before and he sat there trying to understand the*  
*metaphors involved. He knew all about millet stalks that are 10 feet long and very flimsy.*  
*Every house kept them after they harvested millet \(the main Builsa crop\). He knew how*  
*difficult it was to tame horses. I f those strong animals were to fight, it would be difficult*  
*to intervene without getting hurt. He sat there quietly absorbing the metaphors and*  
*enjoying the challenge o f discovering the meaning.*  
*Pastor David continued, "If two horses begin fighting, then this is a serious*  
*problem that requires serious measures to resolve it. You cannot solve this matter by*  
*using weak things like millet stalks; they will merely break and be ineffective. We must*  
*not rely on weak means to solve weighty or difficult issues. ”*  
*The government official smiled all over and clapped his hands fo r David.*  
*Everyone could tell that this official enjoyed the whole process tremendously. This was*  
*“sweet talk” that he enjoyed. He may even use this proverb in the near future to help*  
*settle disputes and the Builsa parties involved would likely give him greater respect as a*  
*result.*  
*Pastor David then added,* *“This reminds me o f how we tend to be in a fight with*  
Naawen *because o f our bad deeds and thoughts. We try to resolve the fight by using*  
*animal sacrifices or good works, but* Naawen Wani *says that all o f our works will one*  
*day be tested in the fire. All o f the things we do will be like millet stalks that will be*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **2 2 8**  
*burned'. That is why* Naawen *sent His son from heaven to separate the fighting between*  
*humankind and* Naawen. *He is the only one who can solve a weighty issue like this one.* ”  
*At this point, the government official started to deeply ponder the meaning of*  
*what was being said. People had witnessed to him before, but this time the*  
*communication went deep into his heart. The proverb had plowed the ground o f his heart*  
*so that the seed o f the word could fin d readily prepared soil. He understood* Naawen*’s*  
*will fo r the Builsa that day in a way that he never had before and it reached both his*  
*head and his heart.*  
*Other proverbs were then given by other church leaders. Each time, the church*  
*leaders discussed the local meanings and then used* Naawen Wani *to interpret them.*  
*Often, some o f the older church members attended these meetings even though they did*  
*not attend many other leadership teaching/training events. It seemed that these meetings*  
*were finally discussing matters in a way that they could appreciate, and the older*  
*members also had something meaningful to contribute.*  
*At the end o f the meeting, Kofi suggested, “This has been a good meeting. I now*  
*have some new proverbs that I can use this week in my ministry. I suggest that we each*  
*use at least one proverb in our teaching, preaching, or counseling each week. Then we*  
*can meet together again and discuss what we learned. ”*  
*This idea was well accepted by all. Joe was eating it all up. He was writing down*  
*notes as the Builsa were talking so that he could later type these and give copies to the*  
*church leaders at the next meeting. It was hard work to understand Builsa culture and*  
*then engage this with* Naawen Wani, *but this process was helping him.*

*Joe suggested, “We have been working hard and carrying a heavy head load. Our*  
*fathers have said,* “Popo jiib alaa jii ngmazuk jiib ” **\(The stomach load carries the head**  
**load/** *I suggest that we go to market and I will get us something to eat. ”*  
*“Great idea! Let's hurry to market though, ” suggested Immanuel,* “’Ling suido  
kan suik saganga ’ *\(The first person to put their* **saab** *ball in the soup will not eat* **saab**  
*without soup\). ”*  
*Everyone enjoyed the meal and the discussions. True to their word, each o f the*  
*church leaders used at least one or more proverbs in their ministry each week. They*  
*began writing these proverbs and reflections down on paper so that they could remember*  
*to share them at the next meeting. It further helped the discussion meetings to have fu ll*  
*and lively discussions.*  
*Joe and some o f the church leaders started to use proverbs more intentionally in*  
*their teaching at the Chiana Bible Training Centre, which included Builsa and non-*  
*Builsa students. The students instantly appreciated the proverbs and this helped to*  
*stimulate further reflection and discussion. They began to see that theology dealt with*  
*issues o f their own culture, and this could be expressed in terms and concepts that were*  
*highly valued in their own culture.*  
*Joe was teaching a class on church planting, and he asked the students to*  
*summarize the stage o f church planting that they we discussing by using one o f their*  
*proverbs. This reversed the flow o f communication and power since Joe \(the teacher\) was*  
*now the struggling learner. He was amazed at the response.*  
*When discussing the needfor small groups, a student stood up with obvious*  
*pleasure, “We have a proverb that states, 'Ifyou put plenty \[too much\] in the mouth, you*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 230  
*can’t chew it all. ’ When our groups are too large in the church, then the people cannot*  
*adequately digest God’s Word. We need small groups in order to chew together on what*  
*we have learned. ”*  
*The students all clapped for him. The excitement in the class room increased as*  
*they all enjoyed listening to the proverbs and their applications. They also were eager to*  
*share their own proverbs and their applications.*  
*Another student stood up, “We say that ‘A smallfire burns all o f the bush*  
*\[unpreparedfield\]. ’ We burn off the bush grass in order to get ready fo r planting the*

*to bring fire to the whole church!”*  
*Again, the class erupted with applause.*  
*“This was the kind o f homework assignment that the students enjoyed and*  
*appreciated, ” Joe thought. It also encouraged them to reflect on their own cultures and*  
*express God’s truths in artistic ways that clearly and concisely communicated deep*  
*within their own soil. In the process, Joe gradually understood and appreciated the*  
*students more, and his relationship with the students deepened.*  
*Some o f the students started being more intentional in using proverbs in their*  
*ministry. Once they started looking, they realized that the hippo was right under their feet*  
*after all. Now some non-Builsa and Builsa church leaders were using proverbs to*  
*understand their culture and then engage this with Scripture. Others became interested*  
*and wanted to meet as well.*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 231  
*Joe knew another missionary in another people group that was also meeting with*  
*church leaders to discuss local proverbs and Scripture. They arranged a joint meeting o f*  
*all o f the people groups. Forty-five church leaders from 17 different language groups met*  
*at the Tamale Institute fo r Cross-Cultural Studies \(TICCS\) fo r 3 nights. Just like the*  
*Builsa meetings, they shared proverbs, provided the cultural backgroundfor the*  
*proverbs, then used Scripture to interpret them111.*  
*“I am impressed with this particular workshop, ” concluded one participant. “It*  
*has opened us up very much to our culture. I have learned how we speak our language in*  
*the proper way. ”*  
*Another church leader added, “It has drawn most o f us to go back to our culture,*  
*as the Ashantis say, ‘Sankofa. ’ So, we should not leave our culture and run after the*  
*Western culture, as some o f us may be tempted to do. It is good that we live our own*  
*culture and see how we can use it in relation to God and in preaching. ”*  
*Joe, Immanuel, Kofi, and David all left the Tamale meeting excited. Since the*  
*meetings were recorded with a cassette recorder, Immanuel took the tapes home with him*  
*and typed them up. The missionaries then helped get them printed into a booklet form so*  
*that the meeting proceedings would not be lost. Church leaders from the other groups*  
*promised to type up their own proverbs as well. The interest fo r using proverbs to*  
*encourage contextualization expanded beyond the Builsa - the circle had definitely*  
*widened*  
As the circle widens, let us pause to reflect on what is happening. We are seeing a  
process that can expand in various ways. Notice how the older people are encouraged to  
participate in the discussions. Often times, only the young men are encouraged to attend  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 232  
church training/teaching events due to their skills in reading and academics. Klem  
\(1995\:62-63\) observed how this tendency often excludes the older community leaders  
who may have greater moral and interpersonal skills, yet they are oral learners. Klem  
concludes, “This preference for the young and the literate may be so basic to our Western  
mission policies that we have perhaps avoided the very best leaders who could have  
given the churches the best stability and greatest growth and integrity.” The use of oral  
literature, like proverbs, helps to value and esteem those who know this oral literature. As  
a result, this type of training may tap into quality leadership that was overlooked by the  
church in the past.  
Also note the empowerment of Kofi as a recognized church leader. In the past, his  
lack of formal schooling made him \(and likely others\) question how effective he could be  
as a church leader. Now, he was starting to be recognized as an effective leader in his  
church. Instead of running from cultural issues, he had a process whereby he could begin  
to engage these issues with Scripture. The proverbs discussions were empowering him to  
be the leader God called him to be in his church.  
The discussion of proverbs can also be attractive to those who are outside the  
church. Notice how the governmental official asked to participate in this discussion.  
While he was previously not open to evangelistic efforts, he asked to come to an event  
that would eventually become an evangelistic opportunity for the church leaders. We  
have seen previously in the narrative how the use of proverbs encourages evangelism and  
we will encounter this again shortly. Suffice it to say that the engagement of Scripture  
with culture via proverbs can result in evangelistic opportunities that attracts those  
outside the church.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 233  
The use of proverbs again attracts other people groups into the circle. Note how  
those from other people groups quickly joined the discussions, finally resulting in a larger  
group in Tamale. The same occurred at the Asbury Theological Seminary in Wilmore,  
KY. During classes on “Contextualization” and “The Indigenous Church,” several  
African students and I formed an African Proverbs discussion group that met over a ten  
week period. In the midst of busy study schedules, these students took the time to analyze  
proverbs and interpret them in light of Scripture, resulting in contextual theology. This  
interest indicates the potential impact of the church beginning a process to engage  
Scripture with culture via proverbs in different contexts.  
This narrative also shows how the circle could be widened to include various  
venues as well as contexts. While we have already seen the church leaders meeting in the  
local church, we now see some of the possibilities with theological training schools. Up  
to the present, I have largely focused on grass-roots theologizing and not formal  
theological schools. While much could be said here, suffice it to say that the intentional  
use of proverbs in theological training schools could promote a discovery of culture and  
engagement with Scripture that could promote contextualization at the formal school  
level. Instead of replacing formal theological training schools altogether with oral  
teaching methods, the narrative shows how oral teaching methods can supplement formal  
theological training. By mixing oral education with formal schooling and perhaps some  
apprenticeship/mentoring, a better process for training primary oral learners may be  
discovered. This is mentioned in passing since the focus of the research is on using  
proverbs at the grass-roots level among the Builsa who are primarily oral learners.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 234  
At the grass-roots level, we have mentioned the importance of the hermeneutical  
community. The narrative reveals some important details regarding the starting and  
functioning of the hermeneutical community.  
First of all, we again see the important role of the catalyst in this process. There  
must be someone like Joe and/or Immanuel who see the potential. The catalyst\(s\) are  
instrumental in bringing forth the idea, starting to gather people together, and arranging  
the transportation/lodging/food if necessary. This could be a missionary like Joe or a  
church leader like Immanuel, but it is more effective when both combine their efforts.  
In order for the discussions to continue over a longer period of time, it will be  
necessary for a local church leader to be committed to the catalyst/organizer role. Once  
the missionary goes home, the local church leaders will need to maintain the discussions;  
otherwise, it will likely slip away. When I left Buluk to come home, the church leaders  
met for a period of time to discuss proverbs, but they eventually stopped meeting. On the  
other hand, the proverbs meetings in Tamale continued when the missionary went home,  
since a church leader in Tamale continued to play the role of catalyst/organizer.  
Another important, but easily overlooked, aspect of the discussion meetings is the  
provision of food. A Builsa church leader explained to me that he wanted to keep the  
discussion meetings alive when I was away but “If I call them to meet, they may think  
that I will have prepared food to eat. Since the money is not there, we can’t eat and  
meet.” Since the Builsa are all subsistence farmers, it is difficult to miss a day of farming.  
The provision of food at the meeting conclusion is most helpful. Hence the proverb,  
***“Popo jiib alaa jii ngmazuk jiib*” \(The stomach load carries the head load\).**  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 235  
The catalyst/organizer helps in many other ways as well. They can keep notes of  
the discussions for future use, they help start the discussion and steer the conversation  
back on track, make suggestions for application \(e.g., encourage the group to more  
intentionally use proverbs in ministry each week and report on the results\), and make  
suggestions for future events \(such as the larger meeting in Tamale\).  
It is interesting to note how informal “assignments” were helpful in encouraging  
more thoughtful reflection. By asking the church leaders to use proverbs in ministry each  
week and then report back, this encouraged them to reflect on proverbs throughout the  
week. One church leader noted that, once he started thinking this way, proverbs just kept  
coming to him all the time. The more he thought of proverbs, the more he used them, and  
vice versa. The assignment also urged many of them to write down some of these  
reflections so that they could discuss them at the next meeting. This again promoted  
reflection and discovery that would make for richer discussions with the other church  
leaders. Some other “assignments” that were used included asking the church leaders to  
find proverbs that related to the GR picture books, and sitting with community elders to  
collect additional proverbs. These all helped to provided more material for reflection and  
use.  
Proverbs can also be used for additional contexts that we have not seen in the  
narrative yet. It is time to turn to another one of these contexts - youth ministry.  
Youth Night  
*A Builsa church youth leader attended some o f the Builsa proverbs discussion*  
*groups. He decided to try a similar type o f meeting with the youth, so he called them*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 236  
*together one school night. It was too hot inside the church building so we sat outside the*  
*church on benches and logs. A kerosene lantern was placed in the middle o f the circle to*  
*provide some light fo r those arriving and those searching fo r places to sit. Twenty-eight*  
*people gathered to “enjoy the show. ”*  
*“Welcome to everyone who has came tonight, ” the youth leader started. “I asked*  
*you to bring some proverbs with you and be prepared to share how you think these relate*  
*to* Naawen Wani. *Who wants to start?* ”  
*Instantly, several hands shot up.*  
*“I like the proverb,* “Kuri, kuri kan wari vorub po’ ***\(\[The sound of\]*** Kuri, kuri  
***will not stay inside a hole\),** ” the first youth offered “When you hear some noise inside a*  
*hole, then you know that something is inside. I f you wait long enough, then you will see it*  
*come out o f the hole eventually. I f you live with* Naawen *and try to do things in secret,*  
*you may think that no one will see it. One day, it will come out for people to see. ”*  
*Another youth added, “Girls and boys may think that they can sleep together and*  
*no one will know. When the girl gets pregnant, the stomach will show. Then, the secrets*  
*will be revealed. That is why they say,* “Kuri, kuri kan wari vorub po.  
*The point hit home. The proverb was addressing issues that the youth were most*  
*concerned about, and* Naawen Wani *was in agreement. There was no way to deny the*  
*validity o f what was said.*  
*Another youth shouted out, “I like the proverb,* ‘Chali mumilik bolim a go jo  
sakpak bolim po’ ***\(Ifyou dodge the light o f a firefly, you may enter the light o f a***  
# witch\). ’’  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 237  
*The youth erupted in laughter, and their curiosity was aroused at the same time.*  
*Witchcraft is an issue that is often just below the surface, but it is seldom talked about*  
*openly. The Builsa believe that witches come out at night to catch the soul o f people.*  
*They can be identified by a small light. Everyone was listening intently to the speaker.*  
*“Ifyou try to avoid a small problem like the light o f a firefly, ” he continued, “you*  
*may end up getting in more trouble. It is better to endure the difficult situation instead of*  
*trying to get around it and making it worse. Sometimes it is hard to follow* Naawen ’.s' *path*  
*and do* Naawen*'s work. You may be tempted to leave* Naawen*’s path. I f you do, you will*  
*enter Satan’s path and your life will be worse. That is why they say,* ‘Chali mumilik  
bolim a ga jo sakpak bolim po.  
*All clappedfor the speaker and his application. Several more hands shot up since*  
*they also wanted to share their proverbs and Scriptural application. This continued for 1*  
*Z 2 hours with no sign o f stopping. After 73 proverbs were given, Pastor Kofi felt that it*  
*was time to end.*  
*“It is late tonight and you all have school tomorrow. We will have to stop here for*  
*now, ” Pastor Kofi stated.*  
*Groans were heard all over since many youth still had additional proverbs that*  
*they wanted to share. Some also had proverbs that they heard somewhere but they did not*  
*know the meaning. They wanted to ask the others what these proverbs meant. For*  
*everyone, the hearing and learning o f new proverbs was “sweet talk” that they could*  
*listen to long into the night.*  
*The youth leader spoke up, “Next Saturday night, come back and we will*  
*continue. Between now and Saturday, I want you to sit with your elders and ask them to*  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 238  
*share some proverbs and their meanings. Then, you can reflect on what they mean in*  
*light* of Naawen Wani. ”  
*This suggestion was well accepted. Next Saturday, they all returned and even*  
*brought some more youth along. Again, they stayed up until 10 PM offering one proverb*  
*after another. Once the pump was primed with a few proverbs, the water continued to*  
*flow. By the end, 47 additional proverbs were shared along with Scriptural applications.*  
Let us take a quick break and discuss what we see occurring here. For the first  
time in the narrative, we hear from some of the youth. The Builsa youth are plentiful in  
many of the churches. They are often quite active as well. Youth ministry can have a  
large impact in the future direction of the Builsa church. The use of proverbs to engage  
Scripture among youth can affirm aspects of Builsa culture, while using Scripture to  
modify or reject other aspects. This process is critical for contextualization; otherwise,  
youth will likely fall into the two knee-jerk reactions that we have seen of full acceptance  
or full rejection without an engagement of the issues.  
Since proverbs contain open-ended metaphors, they can reveal issues relating to  
youth as well as adults. Notice how the proverbs promote reflections about pressing  
youth issues - teen sexual activity and pregnancy, difficulties in following God’s path,  
fear of witchcraft, etc. These are brought to the surface and openly discussed instead of  
being pushed into the “excluded middle.” When both Scripture and proverbs speak about  
these issues, then this carries a lot of weight and youth may take this more seriously.  
Due to the forces of globalization and westernization, some are concerned that  
local proverbs will be discarded by the youth as archaic and not helpful for the  
contemporary context. The narrative here shows that the Builsa youth are still interested  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **2 3 9**  
in their proverbs, and they derive value from them. The youth pastor indicated to me that  
the youth are not interested in proverbs when the elders do not take the time to explain  
the meanings to them. When the meanings are explained to the youth; however, they still  
value them and like to use the proverbs in conversation. They are not too “old-fashioned”  
after all. Proverbs can help stimulate creative thinking and still help guide the youth in  
the church.  
In discussions with some of the community elders, one of their common  
complaints is that the youth do not have time for the elders anymore. Once the children  
start school, they may not have time to help around the farm, and the elders feel like the  
traditional ways are scorned and rejected in favor of more modem practices. In the  
narrative, the youth leader encouraged the youth to sit with the elders and discuss some of  
the proverbs. This helped the Builsa youth to understand some of the positive aspects of  
Builsa culture and then engage them with Scripture. While each of the proverbs and  
Scriptural applications narrated above just started to scratch the surface, the process  
followed here helped the youth to begin engaging the Builsa culture with Scripture. That  
is a big step forward toward the process of contextualization. The youth leader  
recognized this potential with proverbs; hence, he used this process on two separate  
nights. It was a process that could be replicated in other church youth groups as well.  
Another aspect worth noting is the opportunity that the use of proverbs provides  
for evangelism. Several youth brought their friends to hear the proverbs. This was sweet  
talk that many of them enjoyed. It may be a connection for them to understand  
Christianity and embrace it. The attraction of proverbs provides fertile ground to direct  
Builsa toward Scripture, both young and old.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 240  
There are several other contexts where I observed proverbs being used effectively  
in the church that I will just mention in passing. When a large crowd is gathered \(e.g., in  
public ceremonies such as baby dedications, well dedications, weddings, funerals, etc.\),  
proverbs can help communicate the message in a way that both touches the emotions and  
the ideology. When Builsa leave the ceremony, they are likely to remember the proverb  
amidst everything else that transpired. Another context that was effective was when a  
Builsa pastor called the local house heads together in order to hear proverbs from them  
and then discuss them. This turned into a wonderful evangelistic opportunity, as the  
discussion on proverbs gradually revealed cultural issues like witchcraft, drinking, and  
polygamy that the nonbelievers want to know about. In the end, the house head said,  
“Our ways are changing. Our people did not know in the past. Now, Christians should  
teach us well.” These are just a few of the ways that can be used with proverbs in  
ministry.  
Summary and Application  
The above narrative demonstrates some of the possible ways that proverbs can be  
useful in the Builsa area for evangelism, discipleship, and contextualization. Several  
methods were used in various contexts and venues. I have described just a few of the  
possibilities. By analyzing and comparing this and all of the preceding narratives, the  
following summarizes a helpful process that can promote contextualization in the Builsa  
church.  
1\. A catalyst is helpful to call together the hermeneutical community. Ideally, this  
community should be represented by both emic and etic voices, where possible. The  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. **2 4 1**  
catalyst’s role is to help people see the potential of oral literature when it is engaged with  
Scripture. This person or persons will also start to gather people and consider how to  
work out the logistics of transportation, food, and lodging, when needed.  
2\. Participants should be prepared to discuss a proverb with the group. They  
should state the proverb and then explain its meaning/origin in the culture. They should  
explain the context that this proverb is often used, as well as explain its meaning with  
other oral literature when needed \(e.g., a story or song may be needed to explain the  
proverb more fully\). The other participants should also contribute to this exegesis. The  
aim is to dig down to the roots of the proverb to see the cultural values that this proverb  
reveals. In order to reveal these roots, some questions to ask are\:  
a. Who says this proverb and to whom do they say it \(e.g., elder to young, elder to  
elder, chief to disputing parties, etc.\)?  
b. When do they say this? What is done before or after this is spoken?  
c. Where do they say this \(e.g., market, ceremonies, private insult\)?  
d. Why do they use it \(e.g., complement, insult, clarify meaning, entertain, etc.\)?  
3\. The participants search the Scripture for appropriate Scripture portions or  
themes that address the issues in the proverb. While this can be done in a cursory way  
\(such as the youth meeting\), Scripture should be identified that speaks to the deeper roots  
identified above. Scripture can speak most powerfully at this level. This helps  
participants to identify which roots the Scripture will affirm, modify, or reject. The aim is  
to encourage critical reflection so that participants do not fall into the typical responses of  
rejecting everything or accepting everything. Reliance upon the Holy Spirit to guide this  
process is critical. Discussions at this level are most fruitful for contextualization.  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 4\. Consider the insight and implications that this provides for other Christian  
teaching and cultural practices, values, beliefs, etc. The catalyst/organizer should  
encourage the group to think as widely as possible to see if they are uncovering pieces of  
the wider puzzle. The synergy created by other people groups can help reveal aspects that  
may be hard to uncover. Some typical questions to consider asking are\:  
a. Does this analysis affect other traditional practices or beliefs?  
b. Does this provide helpful information into wider issues like witchcraft, funeral  
practices, ancestors, sacrifices, marriage relationships, polygamy, or other “thorny  
issues?”  
c. Does this fill in some missing information concerning some particular problems  
that we are struggling to resolve biblically in our culture?  
d. Does this shed light on some passages that we have a hard time understanding  
or obeying?  
5\. Encourage the group to come to a consensus on suggestions for ministry. This  
is where the pieces are pulled together and the group seeks to make application for their  
own lives and ministry. At this point, the group should think specifically and ask  
questions like\:  
a. How can the results of this analysis be used in teaching, evangelism,  
counseling, and discipling?  
b. What is the best way to remember and communicate this? This will be helpful  
for transforming not only our own lives but also the life of the church.  
c. What occasions would this be useful for \(e.g., weddings, funerals, baby  
dedications, baptism, communion, church discipline, etc.\)?  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 243  
d. In addition to the proverb, what other oral literature, object lessons, metaphors,  
pictures \(e.g., GR picture books\), etc. would be helpful in communicating this  
truth?  
6\. It is helpful for someone in the group to record the discussion for future use.  
This can be done on cassette tape \(for future transcription\) or handwritten \(for future  
typing\). By recording the discussion, others can review and use it later when other issues  
arise. It becomes a part of church history that may help others to deal with similar issues.  
In this regard, it is best to write the results in the mother tongue. This maintains the  
integrity of the thinking processes and conceptual categories used. Then, the results can  
be back-translated into a lingua franca \(such as English\) for wider discussions with other  
people groups. This is more laborious than simply writing everything in a lingua franca  
but the mother tongue usage encourages creative indigenous expressions to truly emerge  
in the contextualization process \(Sanneh 1989\:207\).  
7\. Once this process is familiar with “easily understood” proverbs, do the same  
process with more difficult proverbs. This will stretch the participants to understand and  
apply the process more fully. After these difficult proverbs are addressed, do the same  
process with cultural issues that the church is encountering, in the same way that  
proverbs were engaged with Scripture.  
We have seen how the study of proverbs can be useful to the Builsa church in  
various ways. Adaptations can be made for dealing with youth, the elderly, and various  
officials. In addition, various venues can be used, such as the local church, theological  
schools, regional training centers \(e.g., TICCS\), etc. This can produce contextual  
expressions of Christianity to be used for evangelism, counseling, and discipleship. A  
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission. 244  
multitude of applications can be made. The next chapter discusses some of the wider  
implications of this research for the Builsa church and beyond - the circle keeps  
widening.

2004 "Sweet Talk in Africa\: Using Proverbs in Ministry." *Evangelical Missions*  
*Quarterly* 40\(2\)\: 162-169.  
II Millet stalks are long and slender like com stalks \(only longer\). When the millet is  
harvested, the stalks are kept for various usages. They are commonly used to start fires  
since they ignite easily.
